# 6

## International relations

- Foreign and defence policy
- Empire, Commonwealth and Ireland
- The European Union (EU)
- _Exercises_
- _Further reading_
- _Websites_

The UK has long had significant international relations with other countries, and people have constantly moved to and from Britain because of its colonial past and because its power and survival have depended on global trade. This chapter examines these historical connections and concentrates on significant contemporary areas: foreign and defence policy, the Commonwealth and Ireland, and Britain’s (planned) departure in 2019 from the European Union (EU) after a 46-year membership.

Historically, Britain’s foreign and defence policies aimed to achieve a balance of power in Europe, so that no single country could dominate the Continent. Its main rival, from the Hundred Years’ War until the defeat of Napoleon (1337– 1815) was France, and it won decisive wars (although not the French-supported American War of Independence 1775–83). It created continental alliances with European powers, which were buttressed militarily by a powerful Royal Navy, and established a global British Empire which reached its peak in wealth in 1900 and maximum size by 1920–40. Britain’s economic and political status then progressively diminished, and it built relations with the USA, a military alliance with Japan in 1902, and a Triple Entente with France (1904) and Russia (1907), which opposed Germany in the First World War (1914–18).

After the Second World War against Germany, Italy and Japan (1939–45), Britain’s traditional position as a colonial, economic and political power on the world stage was in further relative decline. Some large colonies, such as Canada and Australia, had already achieved self-governing dominion status. The growth of nationalism and a desire for self-determination among African, Asian and Caribbean countries persuaded Britain to decolonialize further from 1945, colonies became independent, and British involvement in the Middle East was curtailed by the Suez Canal Crisis in 1956.The effects of global economic competition, two world wars, the emergence of Cold War politics (dominated by the USA and the former Soviet Union) and domestic economic and social problems forced Britain to recognize its reduced international status. It was obliged to seek new military alliances such as NATO, political and economic organizations such as the EU, and global agencies such as the United Nations.

It tried with difficult y to find a new identity and to establish different priorities. Some of the previous overseas links continued with the Commonwealth (formerly the British Empire), traditional trading partners and the connection with the United States, while other relationships, such as the EU, were new. But, in spite of fundamental changes, Britain still experiences uncertainties about its defence and foreign policies, its potential international influence and its appropriate role in global affairs.

PLATE 6.1 Queen Elizabeth II greets the Namibian High Commissioner at a reception in Buckingham Palace, 28 April 2009 to commemorate the sixtieth anniversary of the 1949 London Declaration.

This marked the birth of the modern organization, which became the Commonwealth of Nations, commonly known as the Commonwealth. © Shutterstock

##### Foreign and defence policy

Britain’s position in the world today is that of a medium-sized country which faces increasing commercial competition from emerging global powers and was ranked (2018) economically in ffth place behind the USA, China, Japan and Germany. Some of its politicians (and people) still believe that it can have an international influence and role. However, British governments have developed foreign policies that have shifted away from traditionally aggressive unilateral action to persuasive partnerships; embarked on coalition or shared military actions around the world; emphasized ethical and human rights in international and nationalist conflicts; and used versions of cultural non-military ‘soft power’ to try to exert influence.

British national self-interest is still evident in global cooperation and in the concerted fight against terrorism because Britain is a key actor in some military actions. Yet it is argued that such commitments are financially unsustainable and that Britain’s foreign policies and self-image do not reflect the reality of its world position. It has engaged in recent wars and peacekeeping duties in Bosnia, Kosovo, Afghanistan, Sierra Leone, Iraq, Libya and Mali. But, while

military action in Iraq (from 2003) was initially supported by a majority of the British public, there was increasing opposition over its aftermath; distrust of the previous Labour government’s justification for the war, and scepticism that overseas military involvement would protect Britain’s internal security. The war in Afghanistan attracted widespread public opposition and concern at the loss of British lives and the alleged lack of adequate military equipment and protection. It is also felt that the current costs of defence and global commitments could be more profitably directed to solving domestic problems. In a 2013 BBC poll, 75 per cent of respondents believed that Parliament was right to reject UK air and (particularly) ground military action in the later conflict in Syria.

Britain’s foreign and defence preoccupations reflect its traditional position as a major trading nation and finance centre based on a worldwide colonial empire. In changed circumstances, it is now concerned to maintain stable commercial, economic and political conditions through global connections. Britain is a large exporter of goods and services, has substantial overseas or outward investments, imports much of its food and basic manufacturing requirements, and also has substantial inward investment from abroad. It is dependent upon maintaining global commercial links, although it was increasingly committed to the 27 EU countries during its membership, who constituted Britain’s 53 per cent import and 44 per cent export markets (60 per cent total trade – ONS 2015). However, other European countries, the USA, China, India, Japan, South America and Commonwealth nations are also important trading and investment areas.

###### Foreign policy

Britain’s foreign policy and membership of international organizations is based on the principle that overseas objectives in the contemporary world can be best attained by persuasion and cooperation and by actively working with other nations on a regional or global basis and according to the obligations of international treaties or coalitions. The imperial days of unilateral action are now largely past, although Britain did take such action in the 1982 Falklands War. However, its foreign policy can reflect particular biases, with support for one country outweighing that for another.

The USA has often been seen as Britain’s closest ally. A ‘special relationship’ exists between the two based on a common language, cultural traditions, history and military partnerships, particularly in the Second World War and subsequent actions. Yet this association has varied according to geopolitical circumstances, personal relations between political leaders in both countries and divergent policies. There have been differences of opinion about American entry into the Second World War, opposed attitudes to participation in the Vietnam War, and questions about Britain’s membership of the EU, British involvement in the Suez Canal invasion (1956) and US support for decolonialization. Nevertheless,

British politicians are generally concerned to maintain American military and security influence within Europe and NATO and to preserve the global stability of the Atlantic connection.

However, public opinion polls have recently suggested more varied support among both British and American respondents for the special relationship. A March 2009 Gallup poll reported that 36 per cent of Americans regarded Britain as their country’s ‘most valuable ally’ and 89 per cent generally viewed the UK favourably. A 2009 Pew Research Centre poll found that 70 per cent of Britons had a favourable view of the USA. Yet a Pew poll in 2013 found that attitudes towards the special relationships differed between political leaders and the public, with the latter thinking that it was not all that special. While the British and American publics broadly agree on many international issues, they do sometimes differ on the details of foreign policy challenges. Interestingly, a 2013 BBC poll found that 72 per cent of British respondents did not think that Parliament’s decision to reject military action in Syria in 2013 would damage the UK–US relationship and two-thirds said that they would not care if it did. Furthermore, 67 per cent thought that the special relationship was not relevant in the modern age. This suggests a majority feeling among British respondents, reflected in other polls in 2010, that the UK has little or no influence on American policies, that the US does not consider

PLATE 6.2 Prime Minister Theresa May, 2016–19 (second from left) at a summit meeting with other G7 leaders in Charlevoix, Canada, 8 June 2018. © Neil Hall/Pool/EPA-EFE/ Shutterstock

British interests and that the relationship is unbalanced. It also reflects a feeling that Britain was allegedly dragged into the Iraq invasion and that the British were becoming more determined to avoid foreign entanglements and to pursue their own paths.

Britain’s membership of the EU meant that it was to some extent dependent upon EU foreign policy. In 2009 the EU ratified the Lisbon Treaty, which was intended to tighten institutional and policy functions, and created a President of the EU Council and High Representative for Foreign Affairs, which gave the EU a legal negotiating identity and status on the world stage. But, although the EU is moving to more unified foreign policies, member states have conflicting interests and Britain also had its own priorities. EU foreign policy is still in its infancy and many critics doubt its validity and value, although it has proved relatively effective in European and Middle Eastern conflicts. Britain insists that leaving the EU will not lessen its commitment to taking part in European security and defence.

Britain has diplomatic relations with 160 nations and is a member of some 120 international organizations, ranging from bodies for economic cooperation to the United Nations (UN). Support for the UN and the principles of its charter has been part of British foreign policy since 1945, although there has sometimes been scepticism about its effectiveness as a practical and decisive body (for instance, over Iraq resolutions in 2003).

Yet, as a permanent member of the UN Security Council, Britain has a vested interest in supporting the organization. It sees a strong UN as a necessary framework for achieving many of its own foreign policy objectives, such as the peaceful resolution of conflict, arms control, disarmament, peacekeeping operations and the protection of human rights. UN agencies also provide forums for discussing issues in which Britain is involved, such as disaster relief, terrorism, the environment, energy development and world resources. However, Britain, like other nations, may distance itself from the UN when it sees its own vital interests challenged.

###### Defence policy

Britain’s major defence alliance is with the North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO). This currently comprises 28 members (Belgium, Canada, Denmark, Iceland, Italy, Luxembourg, the Netherlands, Norway, Spain, Portugal, Britain, the USA, Greece, Turkey, Germany, Poland, Hungary, the Czech Republic, France, Bulgaria, Estonia, Latvia, Lithuania, Romania, Slovakia, Slovenia, Albania and Croatia). The original justification for NATO’s creation was that it provided its members with greater security than any could achieve individually and was a deterrent against aggression by the now-defunct Warsaw Pact countries. The principle of collective defence is still maintained and many former Warsaw Pact countries have joined NATO.

All the major British political parties are in favour of retaining the NATO link and, according to opinion polls over the years, the public would not support any party which tried to take Britain out of the alliance. Membership of NATO also allows Britain to operate militarily on the international stage. Its defence policy is based on NATO strategies and it assigns most of its armed forces and defence budget to the organization.

Despite changes in Eastern Europe since the fall of the Berlin Wall in 1989 and moves to transform NATO into a more flexible, leaner and high-technology military association, British governments has taken such developments cautiously and are concerned to maintain Britain’s own military defence with conventional and nuclear forces. It recognizes that global uncertainty, international terrorism and unstable states would pose increased risks to its own security if it were to reduce its and NATO’s armed defence capacity. However, there are doubts (and some critical opinions) about NATO’s capacity to respond to global problem areas and it is felt that all its members should increase their defence spending and viability to better support NATO objectives. Britain has achieved the NATO 2% contribution target in the last 8 years.

In 1998 Britain argued that the EU must have a credible military and security capability to support its political role, enabling it to respond quickly to international crises. The EU has slowly developed its own ‘rapid reaction force’ plans from 2007. This force is supposed to consist of battalion-sized military units known as battlegroups of some 1,500 troops under an EU lead nation and other EU participants. These would be under the direct control of the EU Council which operates the Common Security and Defence Policy of the EU. The units have been seen as a new standing army for Europe and were welcomed as helping the UN to deal with trouble spots.

Initially, the debate focused on whether the battlegroups should be seen as an independent force outside NATO or whether they should operate within NATO frameworks. The EU felt that it could respond to international crises, without prejudice to NATO (which would continue to be the foundation of collective security). Some critics argue that this development has been inadequately planned, has cooperation problems and could potentially weaken NATO. Britain’s withdrawal from the EU suggests that it will not be such an integral part of EU developments in the future. But the current Conservative government maintains that Britain will continue to share in Europe’s defence and security arrangements alongside its primary commitment to NATO.

The British government’s defence spending was 2.3 per cent of GDP in 2015 (and a public spending budget for 2015 of 746 billion) putting it in fourth position in terms of world defence budgets after the US, China and Russia. Over the last few years it has controversially reduced armed forces personnel, ships, aircraft and equipment, but has increased spending in other priority areas, such as capabilities and structures. It aims to depend on leaner, multifunctional, fast-reaction, more flexible forces, although there have been strenuous

objections from military leaders to these policies and the considerable reduction of army numbers, planes and ships. The primary objectives of defence policy are to ensure the country’s security and the NATO commitment and to allow British forces to engage in high-intensity war as well as in peacekeeping roles.

A budget reduction to 1.9 per cent of GDP by 2017 did not suggest a Britain that would be able to cope with contemporary and future uncertainties. Further defence cuts connected to an exit from the EU could endanger the armed forces’ new leaner structures, put national security at risk and limit Britain’s capacity to send troops abroad at short notice. This reduced power will result in a lower number of troops in sustained operations and critics argue some British national identity will be lost because it is connected to images of Britain as a martial nation.

However, despite these concerns, defence spending came ffth in 2015 on the list of government expenditure. UK defence spending over the past 5 years has been stable at around 736 billion and is planned to be 737.6 billion by 2019–20. But questions are still raised as to whether the money could be better spent in other areas of national life. At the same time, although the British armed forces are in demand for global commitments, they are arguably undermanned and military equipment is often out of date and in short supply. The

PLATE 6.3 British troops arrive in Kandahar in October 2014 as British and US forces withdraw from Helmand province, Afghanistan after 13 years of combat operations. © WAKIL KOHSAR/AFP/Getty Images

military claim that they need more effective and reliable resources to cope with the demands placed on them. Defence cuts have resulted in a reduction of aircraft carriers, aircraft, destroyers and frigates, and replacements are only slowly being built.

Nuclear weapons, which account for a very large part of the defence budget, continue to be fiercely debated. Britain’s nuclear deterrent consists of long-range American-built Trident nuclear missiles carried by a fleet of four Vanguard submarines (although only one is on patrol at any given time). The nuclear missile arsenal is being upgraded at a cost of 735.8 billion or one-third of the defence equipment budget. Critics want cheaper alternatives, or the cancellation of the nuclear system (on the grounds that the system was based on Cold War planning which is allegedly no longer applicable). Yet while a YouGov poll in 2015 showed that 25 per cent of respondents wanted to give up nuclear weapons completely, 25 per cent wanted to replace them with an equally powerful missile system, 31 per cent wanted a less powerful and less costly system, and 18 per cent did not know. It seems that the British nuclear strategy will continue as a deterrent in a worsening international climate despite the question of whether nuclear missiles can protect against cyber warfare, terrorism and drones. The major political parties are still multilateralist (keeping nuclear weapons until they can be abolished on a global basis), and they are aware that a number of countries now possess nuclear weapons or the means to develop and use them.

Britain can operate militarily outside the NATO and European area, although this capacity is increasingly expensive and questioned. Military garrisons are stationed in Germany (now largely withdrawn), Brunei, Cyprus, the Far and Middle East, the Falkland Islands, Diego Garcia, Ascension Island and Gibraltar. The 1982 Falklands War, the 1991 and 2003–04 Gulf Wars and Afghanistan in 2001 showed that Britain (with other nations) was able to respond to global challenges outside the NATO area, although the operations did draw attention to defects and problems in such actions. Iraq and Afghanistan in particular also demonstrated a lack of suitable equipment and manpower. Britain’s choices are to retreat from global commitments, greatly scale back defence spending, create one combined military force rather than separate components or cooperate more with NATO and EU countries across a range of defence activity.

National Statistics (ONS) figures in April 2018 showed that the total strength of the regular or full-time UK armed forces, which are now all volunteer following the end of conscription in 1962, was 146,560, a planned reduction of some 2.0 per cent since 2017. They consist of 81,120 in the Army (with 3,150 Gurkhas), 32,480 in the Royal Navy and Royal Marines and 32,960 in the Royal Air Force. The cost and personnel-reduction has been balanced by the recruitment of 36,480 part-time reservists and 7,960 specialists across the forces. This policy is controversial, has been heavily criticized and is seen as a way to cut the numbers of the regular forces. The reserves (now known as the

Volunteer Reserve), perform a crucial role, support the regular forces at home and abroad, serve with NATO and coalition ground or front-line troops and help to maintain security in Britain. Women personnel in the Army, Navy and Air Force (some 7 per cent of the total figure) are integral parts of the armed services. They were previously confined to support roles, but some may now be employed in certain front-line military action.

Criticism of defence cuts and arguments that these will risk British military forces being seriously undermanned at home and overseas arguably fail to recognize the resources required to meet the security challenges of the future, such as cyber and technological weaponry, which means shifting the balance of defence investment. It is not conceivably possible to maintain traditional regular forces at historic levels while also investing in trying to counter future non-traditional threats.

Despite such questioning, the defence budget is considerable, given that Britain is a medium-sized economic power which faces the prospects of a domestic anti-EU mood and an EU exit, which may further reduce its influence. By 2020, Britain could well be a smaller, less impressive power. If the loss of a manufacturing base is compared together with provision of essential military force, the costs of defence might seem too great.

The strength of the armed forces could be further cut in future years and the Ministry of Defence budget is likely to fall because of the rising cost of employing military and civilian personnel and the costs of equipment. Much will depend on the development of future warfare, such as cyber warfare and the use of drone and robot technology to replace manned aircraft and ships. Recruitment of both regular and reservist forces to fill gaps is behind schedule at present and the military restructuring programme is facing problems.

##### Empire, Commonwealth and Ireland

The British Empire and later Commonwealth have changed considerably in membership status and scope over the years. The Empire was gradually built up from the sixteenth century, though colonization had in fact begun with the attempted internal domination of the islands (including Ireland) by the English from the twelfth century, together with military conquests in mainland Europe. These were followed by trading activities and the establishment of settlements in North and South America. Parts of Africa, Asia and the Caribbean islands were also exploited commercially over time and many became colonies. Emigrants from Britain settled in countries such as the USA, Australia, Canada, South Africa and New Zealand. By the nineteenth century, British imperial rule and possessions embraced a quarter of the world’s population.

In the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries large colonies such as Canada, Australia, New Zealand and South Africa became self-governing

dominions and eventually achieved full independence. Many of their peoples were descendants of those settlers who had emigrated from Britain in earlier centuries. They regarded Britain as the ‘mother country’ and nurtured a shared kinship. However, this relationship has changed as separate national identities in these countries have become firmly established.

In 1931, the British Empire became the British Commonwealth of Nations by the Statute of Westminster and self-rule was gradually granted to other colonies. India and Pakistan became independent in 1947 (leading to the emergence of the modern Commonwealth with the 1949 London Declaration), followed by African territories in the 1950s and 1960s and later by many islands of the West Indies. Eventually, a majority of the remaining colonies became independent. They could choose whether to break all connections with the colonial past or remain within the Commonwealth as independent nations. Most of them decided to stay in the organization and only a few British colonies, dependencies and protectorates now remain, and these, such as the Falklands and Gibraltar, are scattered widely across the globe.

During the nineteenth and twentieth centuries, relationships within the UK were also changing and reflected nationalist, anti-colonialist and separatist movements in the rest of the Empire. In Ireland, there had been centuries of continuous rebellions by the indigenous Irish against English/UK colonial, political and military rule. Many Irish MPs in the UK Westminster Parliament after the Act of Union in 1801 demanded more autonomy over their own affairs and called persistently for ‘home rule’ for Ireland (control of internal matters by the Irish through an assembly in Dublin). The home rule question dominated late nineteenth and early twentieth-century British politics and led to periodic outbreaks of conflict.

Eventually in 1921 Ireland was partitioned into two parts as a result of uprisings, violence and later political agreement. The 26 counties of southern Ireland became the Irish Free State and a dominion in the Commonwealth. The six counties in the north became Northern Ireland and remained constitutionally part of the United Kingdom. The Irish Free State remained neutral in the Second World War, and in 1949 its Houses of Parliament passed the Republic of Ireland Act, making the Republic of Ireland official ly independent and the country withdrew from the Commonwealth.

However, under the Act, Irish citizens are treated as though they are Commonwealth citizens and not aliens, giving them for example the right to vote in UK elections if they are resident in Britain. Until 1998, the Republic of Ireland claimed Northern Ireland, but this was rescinded under the Belfast Agreement 1998 through an amendment to the Irish Constitution, which now states an aspiration to peaceful unity. Despite a long history of conflicts, from English military conquests in Ireland to the Irish War of Independence, the UK presently works closely with the government of the Republic of Ireland in areas concerning the peace process in Northern Ireland as well as on many security

issues. The relationship between the two countries has improved significant ly in recent years, symbolized by state visits to Ireland by the Queen and to Britain by the president of Ireland. Since Britain (including Northern Ireland) and Ireland were both members of the EU, there was no ‘hard border’ between the Republic and Northern Ireland and each had free movement of people, goods and services. This became a significant difficult y in coping with Britain’s decision to leave the EU and the status of the border remains problematic.

The present Commonwealth is a voluntary association of some 53 independent states (including Britain). It does not have written laws, an elected Parliament, or one political ruler. In many of the countries there is evidence of past colonial rule, such as their educational and legal systems, although few have kept the British form of parliamentary government. Some have adapted it to their own needs, while others are one-party states or have constitutions based on a wide variety of models, with varying records on civil and human rights.

The Commonwealth has nearly a third of the world’s population and a quarter of the earth’s land mass and comprises peoples of different religions, races and nationalities, most of whom share a colonial history, with struggles for independence from colonialism. Though the Commonwealth is often described as a family of nations, there are occasional wars, tensions and quarrels between these family members. The British monarch is its non-political head and has varying constitutional roles in the different countries. A focal point of identification, the monarch has an important unifying and symbolic function which has often kept the Commonwealth together in times of crisis and conflict.

The prime ministers, or heads of state, in Commonwealth countries normally meet every two years under the auspices of the monarch (or Prince Charles as a representative of the Queen) for Commonwealth meetings or conferences in different parts of the world. Common problems are discussed and sometimes settled, although there seem to have been more arguments than agreements in recent years, with Britain having a minority position on some issues (such as opposing trade sanctions against the former apartheid regime in South Africa). Some countries have been suspended from membership, such as Zimbabwe (which has reapplied) and Pakistan (which has returned).

There is a Commonwealth Secretariat based in London which coordinates policy for the Commonwealth under a Director-General, who is the senior official of the Commonwealth. The Commonwealth Institute in London is the educational and cultural centre of the Commonwealth, through which many Commonwealth societies, institutes, libraries, professional associations and university exchange programmes are organized. Commonwealth citizens travel to Britain as immigrants, students and visitors, while British emigration to Commonwealth countries continues in reduced amounts. English in its many varieties remains the common language of the Commonwealth and the prestigious Commonwealth Games (athletics and other sports) are held every four years (the last in Australia in April 2018). There are many joint British/

PLATE 6.4 Commonwealth Heads of Government meeting, London and Windsor, November 2018, led by the Queen. © Yui Mok-WPA Pool/Getty Images

Commonwealth programmes on both official and voluntary levels in agriculture, engineering, health, education and environmentalism, in which some vestiges of the old relationship between Britain and the Commonwealth are still apparent.

However, British attempts to enter European economic structures from the 1960s reduced the importance to Britain of the organization. There is no longer the old sense of Commonwealth solidarity and purpose, and Britain has little in common with some Commonwealth nations. It is argued that unless member countries feel there are valid reasons for continuing an association which represents historical accident rather than common purpose, the long-term future of the Commonwealth must be in doubt.

A YouGov survey in 2009, commissioned by the Royal Commonwealth Society (RCS) suggested that the Commonwealth’s importance to Britons is in decline and the UK public’s level of knowledge about the Commonwealth is poor. Although many Britons feel warmly towards it, only 35 per cent of 18to 34-year-olds would be sorry if the UK withdrew, although 53 per cent of over55s said they would be appalled or sorry. From another perspective, although 42 per cent said they would be unhappy about the UK withdrawing from the Commonwealth – compared to 4 per cent who would be happy – some 55 per cent said they would not mind or did not know. While the 18to 34-year-olds also had difficult y correctly identifying members of the Commonwealth, where

its headquarters were based and who was its head, some 70 per cent of all respondents correctly identified the Queen as the head of the Commonwealth. Yet only one in five respondents were able to name any activities undertaken by the Commonwealth, and among those the great majority identified the Commonwealth Games. When asked to identify which international bodies were valuable to the UK, 33 per cent opted for the Commonwealth, compared to 44 per cent for NATO, 42 per cent for the UN, 37 per cent for the EU and 35 per cent for the G7 group of nations.

This situation may very well change when Britain leaves the EU and begins to seek more substantial trading arrangements with Commonwealth countries. Britain had preferential trading arrangements with the Commonwealth before it joined the European Economic Community (EEC) in 1973 and the Commonwealth question formed part of the debate on membership. EEC entry was seen as ending the close relationship between Britain and the Commonwealth. Yet economic cooperation and trading between the two have continued, and Britain contributes a considerable amount of its overseas aid to countries in the Commonwealth. However, although Britain has a declining share of the Commonwealth trading market and its economic priorities were previously more with the EU and other world partners, this situation may change considerably following Brexit.

Nevertheless, British governments feel that the Commonwealth is a success and are committed to raising its profile. Indeed, a number of countries wish to join the organization, not all of whom have been previous British colonies (such as Mozambique). However, it is argued that the value of the Commonwealth in the contemporary world should not be as a historical anachronism, but must be based on a concrete and realistic role that is distinct from that of other global organizations. For example, it might function as a worldwide forum or network that emphasizes accountable government, democratic concerns, universal values, anti-corruption reform and civil and human rights.

##### The European Union (EU)

Britain’s relationship with Europe has conditioned the country’s history for many centuries. In 1973 it took a tortuous journey from initially distancing itself from Europe to sceptical membership of the EU, and eventually a vote to leave in 2016. The relationship is central to the identities of both Britain and Europe and has included ideological conflict, political self-interest, class and nationalist perspectives, widespread media coverage and governmental inability to solve problems.

The ideal of a united Europe with strong economic and political institutions became increasingly attractive to European statesmen after the Second World War (1939–45). There was a desire to create a peaceful and prosperous

Europe after the destruction of two world wars and years of antagonism and distrust between European powers. The foundations for a more integrated Europe were established in 1957 when six countries (West Germany, France, Belgium, the Netherlands, Luxembourg and Italy) signed the Treaty of Rome and formed the European Economic Community (EEC). Britain did not join, but instead helped to create the European Free Trade Association (EFTA) in 1959. Not wishing to be restricted by close European connections in the 1950s, it saw its future in trading links with the Commonwealth and a ‘special relationship’ with the USA. An old suspicion of Europe also led many British people to shrink from membership of a supranational European organization, which they thought might lead to a loss of their identity and independence. This tension between ‘ever closer union’ within the EU and the preservation of individual national identity continues.

However, a European commitment grew among sections of British society in the 1960s, which was influenced by the country’s increasing social and economic problems. Yet attempts by Britain to join the EEC were vetoed by the French President, Charles de Gaulle. He was critical of Britain’s relationship with the USA (particularly on nuclear weapons policies), queried the extent of British commitment to Europe and arguably did not want Britain as a rival to the leadership of the EEC.

PLATE 6.5 The European Parliament, Brussels. The Parliament also meets in Strasbourg, France, on a rotating basis. © Etienne Ansotte/Shutterstock

De Gaulle resigned from the French presidency in 1969, and new British negotiations on membership began in 1970 under the pro-European Conservative Prime Minister, Edward Heath. In 1972, Parliament voted in favour of entry, despite widespread doubts and the strong opposition of a politically diverse group of interests among the British people. Britain, together with Denmark and the Republic of Ireland, formally joined the EEC on 1 January 1973, having left EFTA in 1972. However, a Labour government (1974) under Harold Wilson was committed to giving the people a referendum on continued membership. After further renegotiations of the terms of entry, the referendum was held in 1975, the first in British political history. Pro-marketeers won by a margin of 2 to 1 (67.2 per cent in favour, 32.8 per cent against).

The EEC was based initially on economic concerns and instituted harmonization programmes such as common coal, steel, agricultural and fisheries policies, the abolition of trade tariffs between member states and development aid to depressed areas within its borders. Britain’s poorer regions have benefited very considerably from regional funds. In 1986 the member states formed an internal or Single European Market in which goods, services, people and capital could move freely across national frontiers within what was then called the European Community (EC). The EC and Britain prospered and by 2011, 53 per cent of British exports were to the EC, and Britain received 51 per cent of its imports from EC countries.

Some politicians had always hoped that economic integration would lead to political initiatives and a more integrated Europe. The Maastricht Treaty (1992) was a step in this process as a result of which the European Community became the European Union (EU). The treaty provided for the introduction of a common European currency (the euro), a European Bank and common defence, foreign and social policies. Further treaties have also increased the integration momentum.

There are now (2018) 28 EU members with a total population of some

511.8 million people (Britain, Denmark, Germany, Greece, Spain, Belgium, Ireland, Luxembourg, the Netherlands, France, Italy, Portugal, Sweden, Finland, Austria, Cyprus, the Czech Republic, Estonia, Hungary, Latvia, Lithuania, Malta, Poland, Slovakia, Slovenia, Romania, Bulgaria and Croatia. Since 1994, most of the EU single market measures have also been extended to Iceland, Norway and Liechtenstein through the creation of the European Economic Area (EEA). The actual growth of the EU (to potentially include Eastern European nations) has been seen as providing an important political voice in world affairs and a powerful trading area in global economic matters. In 2014, according to EU Commission figures, the EU was the world’s largest economy, with over 50 per cent of the world’s gross domestic product (GDP). According to 2012 figures, it was the world’s largest trading block, accounting for 15 per cent of global trade in goods and 22.5 per cent in services and was also the world’s second-largest investor after the USA.

FIGURE 6.1 The European Union, 2019

The main institutions involved in the running of the EU are the European Council, the Council of Ministers, the European Commission, the European Parliament and the European Court of Justice. In 2009, the Lisbon Treaty reformed many aspects of the EU. It created a President of the European Council and a High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy.

The European Council consists of national government leaders (heads of state or heads of government) who meet several times a year to discuss and

agree on broad areas of policy. It is the supreme political authority and comprises the appointed President of the European Council, the President of the European Commission and one representative from each member state.

The Council of Ministers is the policy-implementing and law-initiating body and is composed of government ministers from the member states who vary according to the policy area that is being addressed. It has legislative functions (with the European Parliament) and also executive powers under the Common Foreign and Security Policy.

The Commission (under a President appointed by the European Council) is the central administrative force of the EU, proposing programmes and policy to the Council of Ministers. It comprises commissioners chosen from member states to hold certain portfolios, such as agriculture or competition policy, for a renewable five-year period. Their interests then become those of the EU and not of their national governments. The most prominent Commissioner is the High Representative of the Union for Foreign Affairs and Security Policy, who also serves as Vice-President of the Commission and is appointed by the European Council. It is argued that the unelected Commission has too much power, favours federalism and should be more democratically accountable. But the EU has a relatively influential voice in world affairs, both politically and economically.

PLATE 6.6 Hall of the European Court of Justice, Luxembourg City, Luxembourg, 2012.

© JULIEN WARNAND/EPA-EFE/Shutterstock

The Court of Justice (ECJ) comprises appointed judges from the member states. It interprets EU laws and treaties, settles disputes concerning EU law and resolves conflicts between member states and the EU. It is a very influential institution and has built up a solid body of EU case law which is directly applicable in the domestic systems of the member states. Critics estimate that 70 per cent of British law originated in the EU, but which has now been transposed directly into British law, preparatory to leaving the EU.

The European Parliament is directly elected for a five-year term on a party political basis from the EU-wide electorate. It advises the Council of Ministers on Commission proposals, determines the EU budget, exerts some control over the Council and the Commission and is the EU’s legislative body (together with the Council of Ministers), responsible for most of the EU’s ordinary legislation. It is argued that the Parliament, as the only directly elected body in the EU, should have more power, and its veto over EU policy has now been extended.

The turnout of British voters in the 2014 EU Parliament election was a low 34.19 per cent and mainstream parties performed badly, particularly the coalition government Conservatives and Liberal Democrats. The UK Independence Party (UKIP), which wanted British withdrawal from the EU and controls on immigration, came first. The election used a PR system of voting with party lists for England, Scotland and Wales and the Single Transferable Vote (STV) for Northern Ireland. Britain had 73 seats (or members of the European Parliament – MEPs) in the Parliament (see Table 6.1), which was reduced from 78 in 2004 because of EU enlargement. Some 29 different parties stood in the election and UKIP’s success was the first time a British political party other

|     |     |     |     |     |

than Labour or Conservative Parties has won the popular vote in a national election since the 1906 general election. It was also the first time a party other than Labour and Conservative had won the largest number of seats in a national election since the December 1910 general election. The Conservatives were pushed into third place and the EU-supporting Lib Dems lost all but one of their seats. It was also the first time since 1984 that the largest opposition party (here Labour) had failed to win the European Parliament election.

The 2014 EU election saw the rise of left and right radical parties. This was seen as a sign of disillusionment with the EU after a period of economic crisis and a long-term fragmentation of traditional Left–Right politics. These parties were also seen as anti-European, who could potentially shift the EU Parliament from its federalist traditions and provoke reform of the EU, allegedly in order to make it more relevant for its citizens.

British membership of the EU continued to be difficult, particularly with the electoral success of UKIP. The UK had complained about its contribution to the EU budget (which although reduced under Margaret Thatcher was again under threat); objected to agricultural and fisheries policies; and opposed movements towards greater political and economic integration. On the one hand, Eurosceptic critics argued that Britain’s sovereignty and independence were threatened by EU developments and wanted the repatriation of some national rights from the EU back to Britain. They tended to see the EU as a free-trade area in which national legal rights and interests could be retained. However, Europhile supporters wanted economic and political integration (possibly on federal lines), arguing that the EU enables national governments to rationalize industrial and agricultural plans and allows European countries to operate effectively in a globalized world by running external policies which are beyond the capacity of a small or medium-sized country. In this view, membership of the EU did not mean a handing over of power to Brussels but a net increase in effective power for EU member states.

The major UK political parties have maintained until recently that they want to be in the EU, but were divided in their opinions about what its ultimate form should be. The Liberal Democrats support an EU federal ideal, to which there is Eurosceptic opposition in the Labour and Conservative parties. The Conservative mainstream has wanted to remain in the EU, but supports the allocation of more power to national parliaments, and insists on reform of EU treaties and structures and the repatriation of major functions to the UK, under the principle of subsidiarity, where some economic and social activities can be carried out at lower levels in individual countries rather than the EU. The Brexiteer wing of the Conservative Party wanted British exit from the EU and global trading activity. The Blair Labour Party had wanted a strong Europe in which Britain can play a central role; supported enlargement of the EU; backed the Lisbon Treaty; was initially in principle in favour of Britain entering the European common currency (the euro); and proposed a common defence and

foreign policy for the EU. But it was against the concept of a federal ‘superstate’, favoured the Council of Ministers as the main decision-making body and was against enhancing the powers of the European Parliament. The present Labour Party, at leadership and member levels, has become more divided and some are Eurosceptic in their attitudes to the EU while others are Europhile.

There are different views in Britain about the performance and efficiency of the EU and the pace and direction of future developments. Feelings about the 2007–09 Lisbon Treaty (a revamped EU constitutional system) were volatile. Among other things, the Treaty gave legal identity and authority to the EU; created a legally binding charter of rights; allowed for a full-time president and foreign minister; and formally recognized the supremacy of EU law over national law. It was finally adopted in 2009 after earlier rejections by France, the Netherlands and the Republic of Ireland. But the UK rejected membership of the euro (EU common currency).

British support for the EU peaked in the 1980s but has since eroded, and Britain has been the least enthusiastic of the EU countries, with a 2003 MORI poll finding that only 30 per cent of British respondents felt that EU membership was a good thing. Some earlier polls suggested that only a small majority of respondents wanted to stay in the EU while a majority wanted a referendum on Britain’s continued membership and believed that Britons had not been given enough information about the arguments for and against membership. A BBC/ ComRes poll in 2009 found that 55 per cent of Britons wanted to leave the EU; 84 per cent wanted a referendum before transferring new powers to the EU; 55 per cent wanted Britain to leave the EU but maintain close trading links (41 per cent disagreed); and 51 per cent disagreed that Britain benefited overall from membership in terms of jobs and trade.

Support for the EU has been changeable, lukewarm and indifferent. Turnout of voters for British EU Parliament elections is low and there is a general ignorance in Britain about the EU, its benefits and its institutions. A Pew Research poll in May 2014 found that British respondents gave limited support to European institutions, such as the European Parliament (36 per cent), the European Commission (34 per cent) and the European Central Bank (30 per cent). An earlier MORI poll in September 2004 found that 32 per cent of respondents felt that the EU needs Britain more than Britain needs the EU; but 32 per cent also felt that Britain and the EU need each other equally.

However, other polls have shown mixed, nuanced and positive responses to the EU, and a wish for EU reform. Some suggest that ‘Europe’ is considered to be relatively more important to Britain than the USA and the Commonwealth. Europeanism (rather than an EU institutional entity) seems to be more easily and naturally accepted by people, particularly the young; large numbers of Britons live and work in EU countries; many Britons take their holidays in Europe; and there is considerable interchange at many levels, such as

university exchanges, between Europeans and the British. However, an _Observer_/ Opinion poll in November 2013 found that only 14 per cent of Britons regarded themselves as Europeans and chose as their closest ally fellow English-speaking nations such as the USA (33 per cent), Australia (31 per cent) and Canada (23 per cent). Yet, underlying all the UK arguments about the EU, there is still a feeling that the country has become so closely tied to Europe in economic and institutional ways that withdrawal would be difficult in practical terms, and counter-productive economically. A significant doubt held by some critics is whether Britain could in fact succeed by itself in a competitive globalized economic environment. British eurosceptics disagree.

Nevertheless, the Conservative Prime Minister, David Cameron, responded to anti-EU feelings and promised that if the Conservatives won the 2015 General Election, a renegotiation of Britain’s relationship with the EU and the repatriation of treaty powers to the UK would be followed by a referendum in 2016–17 on continued British membership.

The polls continued to reflect sharp differences in opinion about Britain and the EU in the run-up to the 2015 general election and two were based on actual voting intention. A YouGov poll for the _Sun_ newspaper in December 2013 suggested that 32 per cent of respondents wanted to stay in the EU and 45 per cent would leave. A Populus poll for the BBC in April 2014 prior to the European Parliament elections also found that the British public were divided. Thirty-five per cent of respondents would remain in the EU, 32 per cent wanted to leave, 27 per cent were undecided and 6 per cent would not vote. Regionally, Scotland, London and the east of England were pro-EU, whereas the north-west of England and the West Midlands were anti-EU. However, the YouGov poll significant ly found that a majority wanted to remain in the EU if David Cameron succeeded in renegotiating substantial treaty changes ahead of a potential 2016–17 referendum. These mixed views were echoed in many polls in the run-up to the 2015 election. An Ipsos MORI survey in March 2014 found that Britons thought that the EU was on the wrong track (67 per cent), expected anti-European parties to do well in European Parliament elections, but preferred staying in the EU and reducing its powers (40 per cent) to leaving outright (28 per cent). However, the renegotiations with the EU did not succeed and the Conservative government held a referendum on 23 June 2016, in which the UK voted to leave the EU (see Table 6.2).

‘Leave’ won the majority of votes in England (53.4 per cent) and Wales (52.5 per cent). ‘Remain’ won majorities in Scotland (62.0 per cent) and Northern Ireland (55.8 per cent). Among geographical regions which voted to leave were the East Midlands, East of England, North-East England, North-West England, South-East England, South-West England, Wales, West Midlands and Yorkshire/Humber. Among those areas who voted to remain were Greater London, Northern Ireland and Scotland.

###### Referendum and withdrawal from EU (Brexit)

The process from the referendum result (2016) to (original) scheduled withdrawal from the EU (29 March 2019) was the most complex, political upheaval in British life since the UK joined the EEC 46 years ago. It was a divisive, confusing, frustrating and indecisive period for many British people, and continues to be so (2019). The EU-sceptic Brexiteers believed that leaving the EU would give Britain access to global trading markets, without restriction from the EU. Remainers rejected such views and preferred the institutions and record of the EU, while still agreeing that they needed reform in order to face the contemporary world more resolutely.

The 2016 referendum in which British voters chose to leave the EU did not automatically lead to the UK’s exit. Article 50 of the Lisbon Treaty, 2007–09 gives any EU member state the right to activate the Article and leave the EU by giving notice. Nine months after the referendum result, Prime Minister Theresa May activated Article 50 by signing a letter which was delivered to the European Council’s president on 29 March 2017. Britain had two years to negotiate an exit deal with the EU. Tortuous negotiations then ensued between the EU and the UK.

If no agreement was reached in two years and no extension was agreed, the UK would automatically leave the EU without a deal. Brexit Day would be Friday 29 March 2019. However, the process of leaving the EU became very complex and this date was not met. The EU and Britain then agreed new procedures, which continued to be influenced mainly by the inability of Britain to decide on effective, agreed action and a solution. The political class (prime minister, executive and legislature) was deadlocked.

Britain could reject an EU deal and leave the EU. Parliament could vote against the deal and send it back to the EU requesting renegotiation of the terms. It could legislate for a second referendum on Brexit or a general election. Or it could vote to stay in the EU by revoking Article 50 unilaterally.

Public and Westminster opinion on what to do about Brexit continued to be divided and the options for movement became more restricted. On 5 April 2019 the EU Council appeared to be willing to grant the UK a one-year extension to enable the British government to find a solution.
