# 7

## The legal system

- Legal history
- Sources of British law
- Court structures in England and Wales
- Civil and criminal procedure in England and Wales
- Law and order
- The legal profession
- Attitudes to the legal system and crime
- _Exercises_
- _Further reading_
- _Websites_

Legal structures have played a central part in British civilization for many centuries. Initially, leaders or monarchs, and then Parliament created the law; lawyers and the courts have serviced it; and all citizens are subject to it. It has been influenced by social conflict, political struggle and constitutional change. This chapter examines its history, the main elements that comprise it today, and the problems that it faces.

Law and order, and the actions of the legal system are of concern to the British people and affect individuals at different levels in their daily lives. Legal issues regularly appear in opinion polls about the state of the country, and include the need for accurate figures of crime offences, anti-social behaviour, violence on the streets, yobbishness, security and terrorism issues, cybercrime and the monitoring of communications systems, and cuts in police forces. Opinions differ on whether the legal system has been able to deal effectively with these concerns.

Britain does not have a common legal system. Instead, there are three separate elements: those of England and Wales, Scotland, and Northern Ireland. These sometimes differ from each other in their laws, procedures and court names. Following devolution, some laws are applicable only to individual devolved nations, although UK Parliament legislation on reserved matters still applies to all of Britain.

To simplify matters, this chapter concentrates on the largest element: that of England and Wales, with comparative references to Scotland and Northern Ireland. The Northern Irish legal system is similar to that of England and Wales. But Scotland has historically had its own independent legal apparatus.

British legal cases are divided into civil and criminal law. Civil law involves non-criminal private rights and settles disputes between individuals or organizations. It deals with claims for compensation, financial or otherwise, by a person (claimant) who has suffered loss or damage (such as a breach of contract or a negligent act) at the hands of another (defendant). Civil cases may be decided by negotiation and settlement before trial or by a judge (and sometimes a jury) after a trial.

Criminal law protects society by punishing those (the accused or defendants) who commit crimes against the state, such as theft or murder. The state usually prosecutes an individual or group at a trial in order to establish guilt. The result may be a fine or imprisonment. Such punishment is supposed to act as a deterrent to potential offenders, as well as stating society’s attitudes on a range of matters.

##### Legal history

The legal system is one of the oldest and most traditional of British institutions. Its authority and influence are historically due to its growing independence from the executive and legislative branches of government. Its role is to interpret and apply the law, serve citizens, control unlawful activities against them and the state, protect civil liberties, and support legitimate government and legislation.

Yet, it has been accused of harshness, of supporting vested and political interests, favouring property rather than human rights, maintaining the isolation and mystique of the law, encouraging the delay and expense of legal actions, and being biased against the poor and disadvantaged. It has been criticized for its resistance to reform and the maintenance of professional privileges which can conflict with the public interest.

Some critics feel that the law today has still not adapted to changing conditions, nor understood the needs of contemporary society. Government law Officers, the legal profession and the police are sometimes criticized for pursuing their own sectional interests. Recent miscarriages of justice have embarrassed the police, government and judiciary (judges) and increased public concern about the quality of criminal justice. There are similar misgivings about the expense, delays and functioning of the civil law.

Britain historically has not been thought of as a litigious society. People usually avoid the difficult y and cost of legal actions if possible and regard the law and lawyers as a last resort in resolving their problems. Yet, recently, more Britons have been using the civil courts to gain satisfaction for alleged wrongs against them and a ‘compensation culture’ has grown, encouraged by specialist lawyers. Large damages may be awarded in libel cases and actions against schools, companies, doctors, hospitals, the criminal justice system, media abuse, personal injuries, phone hacking and medical negligence. The Human Rights Act, 1998 is being increasingly used by individuals to assert their claims and rights and can lead to seemingly opposed actions. For example, a right to privacy can conflict with the right of expression, both of which are contained and protected in the 1998 Act, and which may be abolished by the Conservative government.

The legal system has changed over the centuries in response to changing social circumstances and philosophies. Contemporary consumer demands, professional pressures and government reforms have forced it to develop, sometimes rapidly and sometimes slowly. Most people in the past were unaffected by the law, but it now involves citizens more directly and to a greater extent. Increased demands are made upon it by individuals, the state and corporate bodies. Concern about crime has emphasized the control role of the criminal law, while increased divorce, family breakdown and a more litigious society have led to a heavier workload for the civil law.

To some extent, the structural differences in the UK legal system are due to the events of history and the internal political development of the British state. Generally, however, English (and gradually British) legal history has been conditioned by two basic concerns: first that the law should be administered by the state in national courts and second that the judges should be independent of royal and political control.

State centralization of the law in England meant that the same laws should be applicable to the whole country. This was achieved by Anglo-Norman monarchs as they rationalized the existing common (judge-made or case) law and different legal codes. The early courts were consequently mainly based in London, where they dealt with canon (Church), criminal, civil and commercial law. But there was an increasing need for courts in local areas outside London to apply the national law. By the end of the twelfth century, London judges travelled throughout England and decided cases locally. In 1327, Edward III appointed magistrates (justices of the peace) in each county who could hold alleged criminals in jail until their later trial by a London judge. The powers of the magistrates were gradually extended and they operated a system of local criminal courts with the London judges. But there was no adequate provision for local civil courts. These were not established until 1846 and a more integrated apparatus of local civil and criminal law was only gradually established at a later stage.

Over the centuries, a growing population, an expanding volume of legal work and increased social and economic complexity necessitated more courts and specialization. But the number of local and London courts in this haphazard historical development resulted in diverse procedures and an overlapping of functions in England and Wales, which hindered an adequate implementation of the law. The two periods of major reform to correct this situation were in 1873–5, when there was a complete court revision, and in 1970–71, when further changes produced the present court system in England and Wales. Similar developments also occurred in Scotland and Ireland.

The second concern was that the judiciary should be independent of the executive and legislative branches of government. Monarchs were responsible for the law in earlier centuries, often interfered in the legal process and could dismiss unsympathetic judges. Judicial independence was achieved in 1701, when the Act of Settlement made judges virtually irremovable from Office. In future, all judges will resign at 70 and can be dismissed before that for good cause. In recent decades under both Labour and Conservative governments, there have been conflicts between judges and politicians, as the judiciary maintains its traditional independence from the executive, its function of interpreting the law and its sentencing powers in cases, such as those involving murder.

##### Sources of British law

The three main sources of _English/Welsh law_ are the common law, statute law and, until Brexit, European Union law. The oldest is the _common law_, based on the varied local customs of early settlers and invaders. After the Norman Conquest, it slowly became a uniform body of rules, principles and law which was decided and written down by judges in court cases. The same rules still guide judges in their interpretation of statutes and the expansion of the common law (also known as case law or judge-made law).

Common law decisions form precedents from which judges can determine the principles of law to be applied to current cases. Normally today, the creation of new precedents in England and Wales lies with the Supreme Court, as the highest court of appeal from most courts in the United Kingdom. Its rulings state the present law to be applied by all courts. The tradition of following precedent maintains consistency and continuity, but it can result in conservative law and fail to take account of social change.

_Statute law_ was originally made in various forms by the monarch, but the Westminster Parliament gradually became the legislating authority because of its growing power against the monarch. Statutes (Acts of Parliament which create new law) multiplied in the nineteenth and twentieth centuries because rules were needed for a changing, more complex and larger society. Much British law today is in statute form and shows the influence of the state in citizens’ lives. Acts of the Westminster Parliament are applicable to England, Wales and sometimes to the UK as a whole and formerly were supreme over most other forms of law (except for EU law).

_European Union law_ became part of English (British) law following Britain’s entry into the European Economic Community in 1973. EU law took precedence over British domestic law in many areas and British judges had to apply EU law when there was a conflict with Acts of Parliament. EU law and British domestic law therefore coexisted and some 70 per cent of British law originated in the EU. However, when the UK tried to withdraw from the EU, relevant law was transferred into British law.

Another form of law applicable in Britain stems from Britain’s membership of the Council of Europe’s European Convention on Human Rights. The European Court of Human Rights in Strasbourg is not part of the EU legal structures, but rules on appeals made to it by British citizens on human rights issues which have been rejected in the British courts. There is continuing conflict at present between the British courts (especially the Supreme Court) and the European Court of Human Rights as to whether Britain must abide by the latter’s decisions, or whether British courts are sovereign in some matters, such as whether convicted prisoners in British jails have a right to vote in British elections, which they have traditionally not possessed.

_Scottish law_ derives from legal principles and rules modelled on both Roman and English law. The sources of Scots law are judge-made law, authoritative legal treatises, EU law (pre-Brexit) and legislation. The first two constitute the common law of Scotland and are similar to the English common law. Legislation consists of relevant Westminster Acts of Parliament and Scottish Parliament Acts on devolved matters in Scotland.

_Northern Irish law_ has a similar common law tradition to England and Wales. In addition to UK statutes affecting Northern Ireland, the Northern Ireland Assembly has legislative and executive authority for devolved matters and can thus make laws in Northern Ireland.

##### Court structures in England and Wales

The court system is divided into criminal and civil courts (see Figure 7.1) under the central direction of the Ministry of Justice. Increasing numbers of British people have dealings with the different courts at various levels. These are not,

FIGURE 7.1 Civil and Criminal courts in England and Wales

therefore, abstract structures remote from the daily realities of British life and their activities are widely reported and commented upon.

###### Criminal courts

There are two levels of criminal courts. The lower and busier is the magistrates’ court, which deals with summary (less serious) cases and handles over 95 per cent of all criminal matters. The more serious (indictable) criminal offences, such as murder, are tried by the higher court, the crown court. _Magistrates’ courts_ serve local areas in England and Wales. Two types of official s try cases in these courts: magistrates or Justices of the Peace (JPs) and District Judges.

Most magistrates’ courts are presided over by 16,125 lay magistrates (JPs), who may be appointed from the age of 18 and retire at 70. They are part-time judicial official s chosen from the general public, hear cases without a jury, receive no salary for their services (only expenses), and have some mandatory legal training. Magistrates may be motivated by the desire to perform a public service or the supposed prestige of the position. They sit daily in big cities and less frequently in rural areas, in 330 courts in England and Wales. Their Office dates from 1327 and illustrates a legal system in which the ordinary person is judged by other citizens, rather than by professionals. They deal with crimes in the adult court and, on gaining experience, may be involved with defendants aged between 10 and 18 in the youth court, with the welfare of children in the family court, or with a judge in the crown court when an appeal is heard against a sentence passed in the magistrates’ court. Media reports of youth and family cases must not normally identify the parties involved. Youth courts play a central role, particularly at a time when many crimes are committed by young people aged under 16, and family courts are increasingly busy in civil cases at a time of family problems. Magistrates’ courts also handle road traffic offences.

Magistrates are now formally appointed by the Lord Chief Justice (head of the judiciary in England and Wales), who will have been given suitable recommendations by local advisory committees and who delegates the function to a senior presiding judge. In the past, magistrates were mainly white middle or upper-class males who were prominent in the local community, such as landowners, doctors, retired military Officers and businessmen. But they are now recruited after a rigorous selection process from wider and more representative social, ethnic and gender backgrounds. However, the government has reduced the number of magistrates and magistrates’ courts to cut costs. Both are now struggling to produce a basic service in inadequate conditions and subject to long delays.

The magistrates’ court has an average of three JPs when hearing cases, usually composed of both men and women. They decide a case on the facts and

pronounce the punishment, if any is to be given. For centuries they were advised by their clerk, but this central position has now been replaced by ‘a designated Office r for the court’, who is a legally qualified, full-time professional element in the system, but should not be involved in the magistrates’ decision-making, although now able to handle minor judicial tasks.

Each person accused of a criminal offence (defendant) must usually appear first before a magistrates’ court. The court can itself try summary offences and some indictable/summary offences (‘either-way offences’). The magistrates decide whether a person should be sent for trial at the higher crown court in serious cases. Magistrates have limited powers of punishment. They may impose fines up to 75,000 for each offence, or send people to prison for six months on each offence up to a maximum of one year.

There is a need for uniform punishments in magistrates’ courts. However, sentences vary in different parts of the country and at local courts. This factor, in addition to alleged bias and the amateur status of JPs, has led to criticism of the system and proposals to replace magistrates with legal experts. However, these suggestions are criticized by those who oppose the full professionalization of the legal process and who argue that such changes would not necessarily result in greater competence or justice.

Some 140 _district judges_ in the magistrates’ court are qualified lawyers and full-time official s, are paid by the state, usually sit alone to hear and decide cases and work mainly in the large cities. Since the magistrates’ system is therefore divided between amateur JPs and professional district judges, it is sometimes argued that the latter should be used to replace the former on a national basis. But this proposal has been resisted by those who wish to retain the civilian element in the magistrates’ courts.

The higher _crown courts_, such as the Central Criminal Court in London (popularly known as the Old Bailey), are situated in about 92 locations in England and Wales and are centrally administered by the Ministry of Justice.

The crown court has jurisdiction over all indictable criminal offences and innocence or guilt after a trial is decided by a jury of 12 citizens. After it has reached its decision on the facts of the case, sentence is passed by the judge who is in charge of proceedings throughout the trial. Some crown courts can also be used for civil cases.

In _Scotland_, minor criminal cases are tried summarily by lay justices of the peace in district courts (equivalent to English magistrates’ courts). Sheriffs’ courts deal with more serious offences where the sheriff sits alone to hear summary offences and is helped by a jury (15 members) for indictable cases. The most serious cases (such as murder and rape) are handled by the High Court of Justiciary in major urban centres and are heard by a judge and a jury of 15 lay people. _Northern Irish_ criminal courts follow the system in England and Wales with lower magistrates’ courts and higher crown courts (the latter generally with a judge and jury).

PLATE 7.1 The Old Bailey central criminal court in the City of London has been the scene of many famous trials in British legal history and is now a crown court centre. © Kevin Foy/Shutterstock

PLATE 7.2 Interior of Kingston-upon-Thames crown court showing computer equipment installed for trial; court contains many traditional features such as the judge’s chair and bench, jury seats (left) and prosecution and defence benches (centre). © Kevin Wheal/Shutterstock

###### Criminal appeal courts

The appeal structure (see Figure 7.1) is supposed to be a safeguard against mistakes and miscarriages of justice, but the number of such cases has increased, resulting in much publicity and concern. They have been caused by police tampering with or withholding evidence, police pressure to induce confessions, and the unreliability of some forensic evidence. Criminal courts are criticized for their decisions and appeal courts for their handling of some appeals, and an independent authority (the Criminal Cases Review Commission) was created in 1995. It reviews possible miscarriages of justice in England, Wales and Northern Ireland and can send cases back to the appeal courts, which may effectively quash convictions as unsafe or order new trials.

Appeals to a higher court can be expensive and difficult and permission must usually be granted by a lower court. Appeals may be made against conviction or sentence and can be brought on grounds of fact and law. If successful, the higher court may quash the conviction, reduce the sentence or order a new trial. The prosecution can also appeal against a lenient punishment and a heavier sentence may be substituted.

PLATE 7.3 The UK Supreme Court, 2009 in Parliament Square, London, which took over the role of the House of Lords as the supreme court of appeal for many purposes for most parts of the UK. © Ian Macpherson London/Alamy Stock Photo

Crown courts hear appeals from magistrates’ courts and both may appeal on matters of law to a divisional court of the Queen’s Bench Division. Appeals from the crown court are made to the Criminal Division of the Court of Appeal. Appeals may then go to the Supreme Court in London as the highest court in England and Wales (and for civil cases in Scotland and criminal and civil cases in Northern Ireland). But permission is only granted if a point of law of public importance is involved. Usually, five to nine Supreme Court Justices (out of twelve) may hear a case and their decision represents the current state of the law.

In _Scotland_, the High Court of Justiciary acts in some large and smaller towns in Scotland as a criminal court and in Edinburgh as the supreme court for criminal appeals. The Supreme Court in London is the final court of appeal in the UK for civil cases, and also for criminal cases from _England_, _Wales_ and _Northern Ireland_.

###### Civil courts

Civil law proceedings in England and Wales, if not settled by negotiation out of court, are generally brought either in the county court (which deals with 90 per cent of civil cases) or in the High Court (see Figure 7.1). More straightforward and less expensive actions are dealt with in the county court, rather than the High Court, and most civil disputes do not reach court at all. Since 2014, England and Wales have been divided into some 92 county courts, which handle a range of money, property, contract, divorce and family matters and a district judge usually sits alone when hearing and deciding cases. Various types of tribunals, such as those dealing with employment and discrimination matters, may also be used by the civil law. The magistrates court, crown court and family court also deal with some civil matters.

The _High Court of Justice_ has its main centre in London, with courts throughout England and Wales where High Court proceedings may be issued and heard. It is divided into three divisions which specialize in specific matters. The _Queen’s Bench Division_ has a wide jurisdiction, including contract and negligence cases; the _Chancery Court Division_ is concerned with commercial, financial and succession matters; and the _Family Division_ deals with domestic issues such as marriage, divorce, property and the custody of children. After much controversy there were changes to family justice in 2014, which are supposed to put the welfare of children at the centre of the system. The measures include a single Family Court to replace the previous three-tier system, and new time limits for cases where children are taken into care.

In _Scotland_, the sheriff court deals with most civil actions, because its jurisdiction is not financially limited, although the higher Court of Session may also be used for some cases. The _Northern Irish_ High Court handles most civil cases.

PLATE 7.4 The Royal Courts of Justice in the Strand, London, which contain the High Court and the Courts of Appeal (civil, and criminal). © Alex Segre/Shutterstock

###### Civil appeal courts

The High Court hears appeals from magistrates’ courts and county courts. However, the main avenue of appeal is to the Court of Appeal (Civil Division), which deals with appeals from all lower civil courts on questions of law and fact. It can reverse or amend decisions, or sometimes order a new trial.

Appeals from the Court of Appeal may be made to the Supreme Court. The appellant must normally have obtained permission either from the Court of Appeal or the Supreme Court. Appeals are usually restricted to points of law where an important legal issue is at stake. In _Scotland_ at present, civil appeals are made first to a sheriff-principal, then to the Court of Session and finally to the Supreme Court in London. The _Northern Irish_ Court of Appeal hears appeal cases and further appeal is to the UK Supreme Court.

##### Civil and criminal procedure in England and Wales

Many features of civil and criminal procedure in England and Wales are similar to those in Scotland and Northern Ireland.

###### Civil procedure

A civil action in the county court or the High Court begins when the claimant serves papers with details of a claim on the defendant. If the defendant defends the action, documents are circulated to all parties and the case proceeds to trial and judgment. A decision in civil cases is reached on the balance of probabilities. The court also decides the expenses and damages of the action, which may be considerable. Civil law procedures have been reorganized and simplified since 1999 because of concern about the efficiency of the system, with its delays and expense. Much of the High Court’s work has been transferred to the county court. Procedural rules between the two courts have been unified. Active court management has been adapted, with judges setting the pace of litigation, and cheaper, quicker forms of settlement in other courts have been implemented, such as those dealing with matters in small claims courts. Nevertheless, it is advisable that disputes be settled by negotiation out of court rather than by a court trial in order to avoid high costs and any uncertainty about a result.

###### Criminal procedure

Crimes are offences against the laws of the state, and it is the state which usually brings a person to trial. Prior to 1985, the police in England and Wales were responsible for prosecuting criminal cases. The Crown Prosecution Service (CPS) now performs this role. It is independent of the police, financed by the state and staffed by state lawyers, but there has been criticism of its performance, which has suffered from understaffing and underfunding. The CPS and its head (the Director of Public Prosecutions – DPP) have the final word in deciding whether to proceed with cases. In _Scotland_, prosecution duties rest with the Crown Office and Procurator Fiscal Service and in _Northern Ireland_ with the police and the DPP.

Arrests for most criminal offences are made by the police, although any citizen can make a ‘citizen’s arrest’. After criticism of the police for their arrest, questioning and charging practices, they now operate under codes of practice (PACE), which lay down strict procedures for the protection of suspects. The police cannot usually interrogate people, or detain them at a police station if they have not been arrested. Once persons have been arrested, had their rights read to them and been charged with an offence, they must be brought before a magistrates’ court, normally within 24 hours. In serious cases and after arrest, a person can be held for up to 96 hours. After this period, the suspect must be released if no charges are brought.

When a person appears before a magistrates’ court prior to a trial, the magistrates can grant or refuse bail (freedom from custody). If bail is refused, a person is kept in custody in a remand centre or in prison. If bail is granted,

FIGURE 7.2 A typical magistrates’ court in action

the individual is freed until a later court appearance. The court may require assurances from the accused about conduct while on bail, such as residence in a specific area and reporting to a police station.

Application for bail is a legal right, since the accused has not yet been found guilty of a crime, and there should be strong reasons for refusing it. There is concern that those who are refused bail are, at their later trial, either found not guilty or are punished only by a fine. The system thus holds alleged criminals on remand to await trial, which increases overcrowding in prisons. But there is also public concern about accused persons who commit further serious offences while free on lengthy bail.

Criminal trials in the magistrates’ and crown courts are usually open to the public. However, the media can only report the court proceedings and must not comment upon them while the trial is in progress (the _sub judice_ or ‘pending litigation’ rule).

The accused enters the dock, the charge is read and they plead ‘guilty’ or ‘not guilty’. On a ‘guilty’ plea, the person may be sentenced after a statement of the facts by the prosecution. However, sentencing may be deferred to a later date for further reports. On a ‘not guilty’ plea, the trial proceeds in order to establish the person’s innocence or guilt. An individual is innocent until proved guilty and it is the responsibility of the prosecution to prove guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. If proof is not achieved, a ‘not guilty’ verdict is returned by magistrates in the magistrates’ court or by the jury in the crown court. In _Scotland_, there is an additional possible verdict of ‘not proven’ (lack of evidence to convict, but judge or jury unconvinced of the innocence of the defendant).

The prosecution and defence of the accused are usually performed by solicitors in magistrates’ courts and by barristers and solicitor-advocates in crown courts, but it is possible to defend oneself. British trials are adversarial contests between defence and prosecution. Both sides call witnesses in support of their case, who may be questioned by the other side. The rules of evidence and procedure in this contest are complicated and must be strictly observed. The accused may remain silent at arrest, charge and trial and need not give evidence. However, the right to silence has now been limited, which means that the police must warn arrestees that their silence may adversely affect their later defence. The prosecution and judge at the trial may comment on silence and it may influence the decision of juries and magistrates.

It is argued that the adversarial nature of criminal trials can result either in the conviction of innocent people or in the guilty escaping conviction. It is suggested that the inquisitorial system of some other European countries would be better. This allows the prior questioning of suspects and establishing of facts to be carried out by professional impartial interrogators (or judges) rather than the police.

The judge in the crown court and the magistrates in the magistrates’ court are controlling influences in the battle between defence and prosecution. They apply the rules of the court and give directions on procedure and evidence, but they should not interfere too actively, or show bias. After the prosecution and

FIGURE 7.3 A typical crown court in action

FIGURE 7.4 Criminal procedure

the defence have concluded their cases, the magistrates decide both the verdict and sentence. In the crown court, the jury delivers the verdict after the judge has given a summing-up and the judge then pronounces sentence (which may be deferred).

The use of cameras has historically not been allowed in British courts. Although they were allowed in Scotland in 1992, restrictions had prevented their use in all circumstances. The Court of Appeal in England and Wales has now allowed cameras into their proceedings from October 2013 on limited occasions.

###### The jury

Trial by jury is an ancient and important feature of British justice. It has declined in civil cases (except for libel and fraud), but is the main element in criminal trials in the crown court for indictable offences. Most British residents are obliged to undertake jury service when summoned.

Before the start of a criminal trial in the crown court, 12 jurors are chosen from a list of 30 names randomly selected from electoral registers. They listen to the evidence at the trial and give their verdict on the facts, after having been isolated in a room for their deliberations. If a jury cannot reach a decision, it will be discharged and a new one sworn in. The accused can thus be tried twice

(double jeopardy) for the same offence (as in appeals that order a new trial or in the re-hearing of cases in the interests of justice where new and compelling evidence appears). Such results are an exception to the principle that a person can only be tried once for the same offence. In most cases, the jury reaches a decision. The judge accepts a majority (rather than a unanimous) verdict after the jury has deliberated for more than two hours, if there are no more than two dissenters (ten to two). The jury does not decide the punishment or sentence, except in some civil cases, where it awards damages.

The jury system is the citizen’s link with the legal process. It is supposed to safeguard individual liberty and justice because a common-sense decision on the facts either to punish or acquit is taken by fellow citizens. Yet the system has been criticized because of high acquittal rates; allegedly unsuitable or subjective jurors; intimidation of and threats against jurors; and the need to save time and expense. Some critics wish to replace the jury with ‘experts’; the Labour government reduced the right to jury trial in some ‘either-way’ offences; and other trials in which jury intimidation is alleged may now be heard and decided by a single judge without a jury. Although criticism continues, it seems that the jury system, as an essential feature of British justice, will continue for most relevant cases.

###### Legal aid and access to legal services

The British legal aid system was created in 1949 and was the second of its type in the world. It was intended to help eligible persons who could not afford legal representation and advice in criminal and civil matters, to have their bills paid by the state. Since then, eligibility for legal aid has been reformed and reduced in order to cut costs. Recipients of civil and criminal legal aid may be asked to make contributions and pay charges depending on their financial resources and on whether they are found guilty or not guilty.

The changes in legal aid mean that some cases are no longer eligible for public aid funds, including divorce, child contact and access, welfare benefits, employment, clinical negligence and housing law except in limited circumstances. Aid in these areas was once largely given to the poorest and most underprivileged in society. Other examples, such as domestic violence, must have medical evidence of abuse, which is expensive to obtain. The coalition government (2010–15) argued that cuts were necessary, genuine cases of domestic violence would be covered and that the reductions in civil legal aid have not had the serious effects that critics predicted. Some lawyers argue that more people will in fact represent themselves in court and slow down the court system. Other organizations, such as charities and housing associations, are using other ways to cover the gap left by the removal of civil aid by developing new funding models. However, a final assessment of the impact of the cuts remains to be made, although it currently seems to be substantial.

Proposed reductions in criminal legal aid will add to the pressures created by those already made. These include cutting lawyers’ fees in complex high-cost

cases, and in other crown court work by up to 18 per cent. Lawyers argue that this will prevent people having proper representation in court, will seriously affect the quality of justice and diminish the criminal bar with many junior lawyers leaving the profession. The government maintained that lawyers would be fairly paid for their work after the reforms and that efficiencies are necessary to ensure that legal aid remains sustainable.

Critics argue that the reduction of legal aid has already resulted in many legal firms (and barristers) withdrawing from the system because it is unprofitable. In this view, access to legal services is restricted to the poorest members of society (for whom reduced legal aid may still be available without contribution) and the richest minority (who are able to pay their own legal fees). Most people on medium incomes will usually have to pay their own civil and criminal expenses in one form or another if they are involved in legal actions.

Since fewer people are now eligible for state help at a time when demand is rising, it is argued that the provision of legal aid should be made more efficient and effective. But increased demand results in a more expensive legal aid system (which cost 72 billion in 2014), and is both one of the most expensive and generous in the world. Recent governments have increased ‘means-testing’ of legal aid so that any help is effectively tied to income. This practice resulted in a decreased expenditure on legal aid of 71.5 billion in 2016–17. Polls by Ipsos MORI showed that between 2013 and 2014 the number of respondents supporting the government’s cuts in legal aid had fallen from 34 per cent to 23 per cent; those disagreeing rose from 44 per cent to 49 per cent; and those neither agreeing or disagreeing rose from 18 per cent to 25 per cent.

It was hoped that a recent reform would help those people who wish to start personal injury civil actions, but who cannot afford the cost. Clients can enter into conditional agreements with lawyers, in which payment of legal fees on a percentage basis is only made if the client wins (‘no win–no fee’). This scheme could be extended to most civil disputes that involve money or damages (and possibly criminal matters). But critics argue that such work only appeals to lawyers if there is a reasonable chance of winning; it will not solve the problem of insuring against the cost of losing; lawyers may impose large fees; and it encourages a compensation culture.

##### Law and order

###### Crime and punishment

There has been considerable debate as to whether there have been recent decreases or increases in overall crime in the UK. Law and order are of wide concern to people, and political parties promise action. Government analyses indicate that Britain spends more than other industrialized countries on public

order and safety (at about 2.5 per cent of GDP). But EU statistics suggest that it also has more violent crime than other European countries. Evaluations of the extent of crime in Britain are influenced by significant non-reporting of offences such as assaults, burglary and rape; the level of unsolved crime remains high; and despite improvements in recording offences by the police and other bodies there are still conflicting sets of crime statistics and interpretations.

In October 2017, the Office for National Statistics (ONS) reported that the police in England and Wales recorded 5.0 million criminal offences, more than the 4.6 million in 2016. There were increases of 19 per cent in violent crime, assault with injury, robbery, attempted murder, threats to kill, rape and sexual assault (including stalking and harassment). There were also increases in theft, criminal damage, shoplifting (stealing from shops), fraud and public order offences. These rises were arguably due to improved reporting and police recording, and a surge of sex complaints.

On the other hand, the British Crime Survey in England and Wales (CSEW), which is based on replies from respondents who experience crime, estimated that there were 10.8 million crimes against households and resident adults (aged 16 years and over) in 2017. This figure was a 9 per cent reduction compared with 2016. The reduction of crime measured by the CSEW was reportedly influenced by decreases in a range of crime types, such as theft offences, violence and criminal damage.

However, critics point out that the Survey focuses on household responses, which may inflate figures, and yet does not cover, for example, the homeless, students in halls of residence and business crime. It also surveys people face-to face, which allegedly results in its figures differing from the number of crimes reported by police. The definition of crime in the CSEW also differs somewhat from how the police record it, and police-recorded statistics are contested.

Nevertheless, ONS figures in 2017 also revealed that many more people in England and Wales worry about becoming a victim of crime than will actually experience it. The risk of being a victim of crime is relatively low. Additionally, there has been better reporting of low-level crime, domestic violence and sexual offences, and the number of crimes that affect most people, such as burglary, robbery and vehicle theft, have decreased. However, street crime, such as knife attacks, is a politically high profile issue and 40 per cent of this type of crime occurs in London.

Research surveys (like the Offending, Crime and Justice survey up to 2006) suggest that about 10 per cent of offenders are responsible for 60 per cent of offences and that serious and prolific offenders amount to around 1 per cent of the population. It is also estimated that only one in 50 crimes results in a conviction and that only some 23 per cent of all crimes are recorded by the police. Opinion polls still regularly suggest that 50 per cent of victims do not report incidents to the police because they lack confidence that criminals would be caught.

Disturbing aspects of these statistics are the increasing use of knives, firearms and acid in criminal acts, particularly those connected to gang violence and serious organized crime (leading to demands that all police Officers should be armed); the increased amount of drug and alcohol-related crime and violence; and the number of offences committed by young people. Britain has a problem with young offenders, the peak age for committing crime is 15 and 10 per cent of reported criminal offences are committed by teenagers under 16. There is a widespread membership of gangs in this age group and adherence to gang culture (allegedly encouraged by rap music lyrics) in which the carrying and use of guns and knives are prevalent. But the amount of knife-carrying is also reportedly due to self-protection from attack. Binge-drinking in town and city centres has also increased and led to more violence on the streets (although such behaviour declined in 2017). Critics argue that the previous Labour government’s decision to extend drinking hours in pubs, clubs and restaurants in 2005, in an attempt to copy Continental cafe culture, only further contributed to the problem.

Respondents to polls think that the causes of crime in Britain are lack of parental discipline and male role models; the breakdown of family and community structures; drugs; alcohol; lenient sentencing by the courts; gangs; unemployment; a lack of school discipline; poverty; television; poor policing; teenage boredom; and lack of free-time facilities for young people. Governments have tried to ease public concern by promising to confront crime by introducing stricter punishment (particularly for young people), curfews and restrictions on persistent offenders, longer jail terms and greater protection for the public. But polls continue to show that people do not think that governments have delivered on their law and order promises.

A person found guilty of a first criminal offence may, depending on its severity, either receive a suspended prison sentence, or be placed on non-custodial probation for a period under the supervision of probation Officers, despite a serious reduction in the staffing of the probation service. Other punishments for adults are fines or imprisonment (for those over 21), which vary according to the severity of the offence and any previous convictions. Stricter sentencing will lead to more prisoners and convicted persons are imprisoned at a higher rate than in other western European countries. In 2018, there were 93,000 people in prisons in the UK (83,430 in England and Wales, 7,595 in Scotland and 1,475 in Northern Ireland). The prison population is expected to grow by 500–1,000 prisoners each year into the 2020s. Prison overcrowding is a serious problem and prison warders lack control over inmates in many institutions, which suffer from internal violence and abuse, drug traffic king and widespread drug usage.

Alternatives to prison are community rehabilitation orders (serving the community in some capacity for a number of hours over a number of months) and suspended prison sentences (dependent upon no further offences being committed for a specified period). A recent experiment is ‘tagging’ (arm or

leg bracelets on offenders and connected electronically to local police stations) whereby offenders are confined to a specific area and have to observe a curfew. The tag is activated if these conditions are broken. However, tagging has not proved to be a complete success and the level of re-offending after and during tagging is very high. A recent attempt to control yobbish behaviour is the anti-social behaviour order (ASBO), which bans offenders from particular areas or prohibits various forms of thuggish activity. The number of ASBOs reached its height in 2005 before declining, and then started to increase again in 2009. In 2012, the ASBO was replaced by Criminal Behaviour Orders (CBOs) and CPIs (Crime Prevention Injunctions).

Young people may be punished by fines (under 17), taken into local authority care, confined in a young offenders’ institution (Youth Prison) for those between 17 and 20, or undergo supervision in the community. Re-offending among young people after a custodial sentence is high, but supervision outside institutions leads less often to re-offending. There have been suggestions to abolish custodial sentences for relatively minor offences, but this idea has not been implemented.

The death penalty by hanging for murder was ended in 1965 and legally abolished under the Human Rights Act in 1998. The House of Commons has since voted by substantial majorities on several occasions against its reimposition. However, an Ipsos MORI poll in July 2010 revealed that 70 per cent of respondents thought that the UK should still have the death penalty as the maximum possible penalty for at least one crime, such as child murder (62 per cent). A YouGov poll in November 2010 reported that 74 per cent supported the death penalty for murder in some circumstances, but only 16 per cent supported it for all murders. These polls suggest that respondents are able (and willing) to distinguish between different categories of murder and other offences. But Ipsos MORI and British Social Attitudes (BSA) used tracking of attitudes to the death penalty across the generations which concluded that support for the death penalty fell by 20 per cent from 74 per cent in 1993 to 54 per cent in 2012. This decline in support indicated that most of the population has no immediate media experience of death penalty hanging and is distanced from it. A BSA report in March 2015 found that support for the death penalty had dropped below 50 per cent (48 per cent) for the first time since 1983.

Nevertheless, despite these developments, the public do support harsh treatment of criminal offenders, particularly in child abuse categories, and argue that more sympathy and aid should be given to victims of crime. Governments have supported victims with financial compensation, but its programmes have not satisfied critics.

Many British people feel that the penalties for criminal offences are inadequate as deterrents against crime. But most prisons are overcrowded, old and decayed, lack humane facilities, are unfitted for a modern penal system and are understaffed, with personnel being overworked. Prison conditions have

resulted in serious disorder and riots in recent years, low morale among prisoners and prison staff, more suicides by inmates and terrorist indoctrination of prisoners. Debates about punishment as opposed to the rehabilitation of offenders continue. Yet proposals to improve the situation encounter the problem of expense, although the government is building more prisons. Some prisons and prison services (such as escorting prisoners to court) have also been privatized.

A majority of prisoners are not reformed by their sentences. Some 60 per cent are reconvicted for later offences, and fear of prison or punishment does not seem to act as a deterrent. Critics suggest that jail terms should be cut (with weekend-only prisoners) and that institutions should be humanized and prisoners given a sense of purpose. Alternatives to custodial sentences, such as supervised housing, probation hostels and supervised work projects are also advocated. But others argue that the function of the criminal system is punishment and not rehabilitation. For example, the coalition government argued that prisoners should not have the right to vote in elections or to challenge the validity of full life sentences, despite opposition from the European Court of Human Rights.

###### Law enforcement and the police

The armed forces in Britain are subordinate to the civilian government and are used only occasionally for defence and emergency situations. An exception was the deployment of the army in Northern Ireland during the Troubles, where they supported the Royal Ulster Constabulary (now the Police Service of Northern Ireland). But proposals have been made that the military and some civilian agencies could help the police to counter organized crime involving drugs, illegal immigration, fraud and computer hacking.

The maintenance of law and order rests mainly with the civilian police. The oldest police force is the Metropolitan Police, founded in 1829 by Sir Robert Peel to fight crime in London, and from which the modern forces grew. Today there is no single national police force, but instead 45 independent forces (43 in England and Wales, one in Scotland and one in Northern Ireland) which undertake law enforcement in county or regional areas, with the Metropolitan Police being responsible for policing London. Regional forces are under the political control of local government committees. Daily authority rests with the head of each regional force (chief constable), who has organizational independence and responsibility for the actions of the force. There are proposals to reduce or merge the regional forces, and to centralize their functions, administration and operations in a national unit.

A recent innovation to improve police performance and their relationship with the public was the creation of Police and Crime Commissioners in 2013. They are elected by local populations in regional police force areas,

PLATE 7.5 Clashes between police and Occupy London anti-capitalist protestors, Fetter Lane,

London, 12 May 2012. © Matthew Chattle/Alamy Stock Photo

represent the public, act on public views in policing matters, produce police and crime plans with proposals for local priorities, are supported by a budget, employ their own administrators, and hold the Chief Constable to account for police performance on behalf of the public. The Commissioners do not have a party political position and are given freedom to develop their own methods appropriate to their area. This service has had an uncertain start, with controversy, conflicts and lack of direction, but now appears to have become more settled.

According to the House of Commons Library, there were 123,142 fulltime policemen and women in the 43 police forces of England and Wales in 2017, a decrease of 0.7 per cent or 924 since 2016. Police forces are being cut under the government’s austerity measures and many areas of the country, both urban and rural, have lost their local police. Local populations are very concerned and critics argue that the forces are understaffed and should be increased. Only a disproportionately small number of police are from nonwhite ethnic communities (6 per cent or an increase of 1 per cent in 1989 of the total force). The proportion of female police Officers increased from 7 per cent in 1997 to 29 per cent in 2017. Many in the ethnic communities are hostile to or sceptical of the police, although there have been attempts to recruit more of them to the forces, with varying degrees of success. Community Support Officers (14,205, a decrease of 188 or 1.3 per cent since 2012) provide

supplementary help to the regular police in security duties and give a greater street presence. There are also part-time special constables (19,010, a decrease of 1,332 or 6.6 per cent since 2012) who form a voluntary police resource for at least four hours per week.

The police are not allowed to join trade unions or strike. But they do have staff associations or federations to represent their interests. They are subject to the law, and can be sued or prosecuted for any wrongdoing in the course of their work. Individuals and organizations can complain about police actions to an Independent Police Complaints Commission (IPCC) which, it is hoped, might provide a stronger role than previous models. However, critics still argue that complaints procedures are unsatisfactory and that democratic control of the police should be strengthened.

The police, with their distinctive helmets and lack of firearms, are often regarded as a typical British institution. They used to embody a presence in the local community by ‘walking the beat’ and personified fairness, stolidity, friendliness, helpfulness and incorruptibility. These virtues still exist to a degree and the traditional view is that the police should control the community by consent rather than force and that they should be visible in local areas.

However, in recent years, the police have been taken off foot patrols; put into cars to increase effectiveness and mobility; and more are now armed and trained in riot-control programmes. They have been accused of institutional racism, corruption, brutality, excessive use of force, perverting the course of justice, tampering with evidence in criminal trials, and inappropriate behaviour. Some of these accusations have been proved. They have lowered the image of the police as well as their morale and have contributed to a loss of public confidence and trust. There are still public concerns, and governments and senior police Officers have attempted to reform the police forces.

The police tread a thin line in community activities, strikes and demonstrations. They are in the middle of opposing forces, much is expected of them and uncertain law and politically correct attitudes sometimes hinder their effectiveness. The problems of violent crime, organized criminal gangs, relations with ethnic communities and an increasingly complex society have made their job more difficult. The police are trying to find ways of adequately and fairly controlling a changing society. They are concerned about their image, but insist that their primary duty is to maintain law and order.

In an attempt to counter a more complex crime scene, a National Crime Agency (NCA) of some 4,200 Officers was formed in 2013, with powers applicable to England, Wales and Scotland. It combats major organized crime such as drugs, people traffic king and child exploitation, as well as complex international fraud, such as online cybercrime that cuts across regional and international borders. The NCA can also instruct regional police and other agencies to carry out specific tasks or operations.

##### The legal profession

The legal profession in England and Wales is divided into two principal types of lawyer: barristers and solicitors. Each branch has its own vested interests and jurisdiction and fiercely protects its position. This system is criticized because of a duplication of services, delay and expense. But legal services have been reformed to benefit consumers, promote competition and give easier access to the law. Legislation in 2007 made further inroads into the profession’s traditional self-regulatory role and exclusive representative status by opening up legal services and firms to commercial ownership and the sharing of functions with other professionals. An independent, lay-dominated Legal Services Board was also established, which oversees the regulation of lawyers and their organizations in England and Wales. It controls their general accountability to clients and society; acts in the interests of the consumers of legal services; and reforms and modernizes the legal services marketplace. Arguably, there is now better provision for the public to make consumer complaints against legal professionals.

There were 142,286 _solicitors_ in England and Wales in 2018, who practise mainly in private firms, but also in local and central government, legal centres and industry. They are represented by their professional body, the Law Society, but regulation is performed by the independent Solicitors Regulation Authority (SRA), which examines the conduct of solicitors and their relationship with clients and the public. The free Legal Ombudsman service (from 2010) also examines consumer complaints by the public against legal professionals.

Although the solicitors’ profession is historically middle-class, it is attracting members from wider class, ethnic and gender spectrums of society. Solicitors deal with general legal work, although many now specialize in one area of the law. Their firms (or partnerships) offer services such as conveyancing (the buying and selling of property); probate (wills and succession after death); family matters; criminal and civil litigation; commercial cases; and tax and financial affairs. Large firms may be very successful, but small firms are currently affected by cuts in Legal Aid and some face closure.

The client with a legal problem will first approach a solicitor, who can often deal with all aspects of the case. Solicitors originally could only appear (rights of audience) for their clients in the lower county and magistrates’ courts, and cases in higher courts had to be handed to a barrister. This expensive practice has now been reformed and solicitor-advocates can appear in higher courts.

In order to become a solicitor, it is usual to have a university degree, not necessarily in law. After passing further professional examinations organized by law colleges, the student serves a practical apprenticeship as a trainee solicitor with an established solicitor for some two years. After a total period of about six years’ education and training, the new solicitor can practise the law.

PLATE 7.6 The Middle Temple, one of the four Inns of Court in London, where student barristers become members and often work in chambers there on becoming fully qualified. © Frank Monaco/Shutterstock

There were about 17,000 _barristers_ in private practice in 2018, who have the right to appear before any court in England and Wales. They belong to the Bar, which is an ancient professional legal institution whose Bar Council provides representation, support and services for barristers in England and Wales. Barristers are also members of one of four Inns of Court in London (Gray’s Inn, Lincoln’s Inn, Middle Temple and the Inner Temple), where many barristers work from chambers or offices. The Bar Standards Board is an independent body of the Bar Council which regulates barristers and their professional conduct in the public interest through a Code of Practice, although the public can now complain about barristers to the Legal Ombudsman service (2010).

Barristers have two functions: to give specialized advice on legal matters and to act as advocates in the courts. Historically, the general public could not approach a barrister directly, but must have been introduced by a solicitor. This regulation has now been relaxed for some clients.

In order to become a barrister, one must usually have a university degree, pass professional examinations and become a member of an Inn of Court. The student must dine in the Inn for a number of terms before being ‘called to the Bar’ (accepted as a barrister). They must then serve for a one-year period (pupillage) under a practising barrister and there is great competition to gain a place. After this five-year training period, the new barrister can practise law.

Barristers are self-employed individuals who practise from chambers (or offices), together with other barristers. The barrister’s career starts as a ‘junior’ handling minor briefs (or cases). They may have difficult y in earning a living or in becoming established in the early years of practice, with the result that many drop out and enter other fields. Should the barrister persist and build up a successful practice as a junior, they may ‘take silk’ and become a Queen’s Counsel (QC). A QC is a senior barrister who can charge higher fees for their work, but who is then excluded from appearing in lesser cases. Appointment as a QC may lead to a future position as a judge and it is regarded as a necessary step for the ambitious. The legal professions in _Scotland_ and _Northern Ireland_ are also divided.

Scotland has some 462 practising advocates (barristers) and 11,000 solicitors.

Advocates practise as individuals and are independent of each other. Scottish solicitors usually operate in partnership with other solicitors. Northern Ireland has some 2,300 solicitors and about 700 barristers.

The _judges_ constitute the judiciary, or independent third branch of the constitutional system. There are a relatively small number of judges at various levels of seniority, who are located in most large cities and the higher courts in London. They are chosen from the ranks of senior barristers, although solicitors are now eligible. Judgeships are advertised for open competition and appointments are made by an independent Judicial Appointments Commission. But there is a current problem with filling vacant judicial posts.

Judges are now required to retire at 70 years of age, although those appointed before 31 March 1995 may wait to retire at 75. All judges may be dismissed for good reason before their retirement ages, although such a step is historically unusual. Given the difficult y of recruitment to the judiciary and later retirement ages in society, it is suggested that judges should also be able to serve at least until 75.

Judges are regarded by many as socially and educationally elitist and remote from ordinary life. They are overwhelmingly male and are seen as people who will not cause embarrassment to the establishment and tend to support the accepted wisdom and status quo. However, they may in fact rule against government policies. They increasingly argue publicly in the media and elsewhere and have been frequently in dispute with governments about their jurisdiction in areas such as life sentences for murder. The judiciary is changing to admit more women, ethnic minorities and people with lower-class and educationally diverse backgrounds. But, although over half of law students are female, there are few women judges (or QCs and senior partners in solicitors’ firms ).

The judiciary tends to be old in years because judgeships are normally awarded to senior practising lawyers and there is no career structure that people may join early in life. A lawyer’s income may be greatly reduced on accepting a judgeship, but the honour and added security are supposed to be some compensation. There are promotional steps within the judiciary from recorder to circuit judge to High Court judge, and thence to the Court of Appeal and the Supreme Court.

##### Attitudes to the legal system and crime

An Ipsos MORI poll in June 2018 reported that concerns among respondents about crime and law and order in the UK had risen to fourth place at 20 per cent in a list of the most important issues facing Britain, after Brexit and the NHS.

British attitudes to the criminal justice system (CJS) in England and Wales, which covers crime, criminal institutions, court procedures, legal personnel, the police and criminal statistics, vary over time and can be contradictory. Crime statistics derive from two main sources. The first is crime figures recorded by the police on the basis of offences reported to them by the public (recordable crimes). The second is the Crime Survey for England and Wales (CSEW). This is regarded as an accurate survey of criminal statistics because it asks households in face-to-face interviews whether they have experienced any crime in the past year. But since it registers offences that have not been reported to the police, and because it does not cover all groups (such as students living in university/ college accommodation or individual children under 18), the Survey’s results are often different to police-recorded crime, and attitudes to crime and the CJS. The CSEW in 2017 (see earlier section on Law and order) reported that there were 10.8 million offences, which was a 9 per cent reduction on the previous year. But ONS figures of crimes recorded by the police in 2017 had exceeded 5 million for the first time in ten years, and risen by 13 per cent from

4.6 million offences in 2016.

When respondents were asked in the 2017 CSEW whether they thought that crime had increased or decreased in recent years, 60 per cent said that it had gone up (as opposed to 57 per cent in 2016) and 83.5 per cent thought that crime was a big problem; 72 per cent thought that crime in their local area was similar to or higher than crime nationally; had formed opinions about crime in their local areas from their own or other local people’s experience; but 67 per cent said that their main source of information about crime was from the media (TV/radio news).

The CSEW’s 2017 finding of a reduction in crime was reflected in a decline (15.2 per cent in 2017) of people who felt a likelihood of being a victim of crime. There was also a downward trend of adults who worried about specific crime types, such as burglary, car crime and violence. As opposed to these CSEW declines, police-reported crime showed a 22 per cent increase in offences involving knives or other sharp instruments and an increase in firearms offences, which attract media comment and public concern. It is argued that such crimes, while relevant as individual increases, do not suggest significant trends. Most people do not experience crime, and violent offences tend to be localized in certain areas in the big cities.

Arguably, the CSEW surveys and ONS police-reported offences illustrate that different polls, based on particular research methods, give varying pictures

of overall trends in British crime. There are spikes in new types of violence, which attract media and public attention, such as acid and moped attacks on individuals, that often focus on particular groups or individuals. But it is argued that fear of crime in Britain has been historically higher than its actuality. The reasons why some people think that crime has increased in England and Wales may also depend on where they obtain their information, such as television, radio, newspapers or local hearsay.

The police are at the forefront of the fight against crime, but their numbers have fallen by 19,000 Officers since 2010 due to government financial cuts. Attitudes towards the police have varied in recent years. According to a Police Federation General Public Online Omnibus poll in 2014, respondents were asked whether they had confidence in the police in local areas to keep them safe (12 per cent replied ‘very confident’, 59 per cent ‘fairly confident’) and to tell the truth (15 per cent ‘very confident’, 51 per cent ‘fairly confident’).

An Ipsos MORI poll in 2014 also suggested that trust in the police at local levels had increased. Sixty-five per cent of respondents said they would trust the police to tell the truth and 63 per cent disagreed that local police abuse their power. Seventy-three per cent agreed that the police could be trusted not to exceed their authority and 66 per cent of respondents disagreed that the police think they are above the law. There have been times when trust in the police at national level was relatively high and long periods in the 1970s–90s when it was low. In the last 30 years the police have been held to higher standards and their actions have been more closely examined. But despite more positive statistics, the former Independent Police Complaints Commission (IPCC) in England and Wales in 2015 reported that complaints about police conduct had risen by 15 per cent to a record high. Many of these dealt with assaults by a police Office r or unlawful or unnecessary detention. Following recent anger and mistrust over investigations into the deaths of several men in police custody, a new IPCC was formed in 2018. The aim was to ensure greater accountability to the public by allowing the new body to pursue investigations independently of the police forces.

However, there are opposed views about the overall performance of the Criminal Justice System (CJS) in England and Wales. According to the Public Accounts Committee of MPs in 2016, it was described as failing victims of crime and court witnesses and was close to collapse. BBC News also reported (2016) that it was weakened by long-standing poor performance, delays and inefficiencies. The report warned that a 26 per cent decline and a 27 per cent cut in Crown Prosecution lawyers affected the courts’ ability to deliver justice. Two-thirds of trials in the crown courts were delayed or did not go ahead at all. There was a list of cases and long waiting times to move from magistrate courts to the crown courts. Witnesses experienced long waits to give evidence in court. The system was described as ineffective and putting criminal court justice at risk. The Ministry of Justice in 2016 reported that there were only 33 per cent

of effective trials in 2014–15; the estimated cost for cases that did not go to trial was 721.5 million; and the backlog of cases in the crown court since 2013 was 34 per cent.
