# 9

## Social services

- Social services history
- Household and demographic structures
- Social security (pensions and welfare benefits)
- The National Health Service (NHS)
- The personal social services/social care
- Housing
- Attitudes to the social services
- _Exercises_
- _Further reading_
- _Websites_

The term ‘social services’ covers health care, social care (personal assistance), social security (pensions and welfare benefits) and housing in the UK. This chapter examines the public, private and voluntary provision of these services.

State (public) sector social services are now very much taken for granted by many Britons. They also feature prominently in lists of people’s concerns, directly affect the lives of individuals of all ages and are a much-debated part of government policy. But it was not until the 1940s that the state accepted overall responsibility for providing basic social and medical help for all its citizens. Previously, there had been few such facilities and it was felt that the state was not obliged to supply them.

Until recently, the UK government in London organized state health, social security, housing and social care for the sick, retired, disabled, elderly, needy and unemployed, which were delivered by agencies and local councils throughout Britain. This system changed from 1998. The individual countries of the UK now have devolved powers over most of their own affairs through their elected parliament and assemblies. The UK government distributes central funds to the devolved countries to spend according to their local plans.

Consequently, Scotland has devolved powers in health, social housing and social care; Wales in health, social welfare, and social housing; and Northern Ireland in health and social services. The UK government organizes these services in England, but also has reserved powers over work, pensions and social security benefits in the UK through the state Department for Work and Pensions.

State systems are primarily funded through UK central taxation and National Insurance (NI). This means that although many state social services, such as health care, are provided free at the point of need, most people will have contributed to the cost during their working lives through payment of income tax and NI. But some social care is means-tested or based upon ability to pay for services.

In the private sector, social and health services are financed by personal insurance schemes, company plans and by individuals who choose to pay for such facilities out of their own income or capital. Most of these people are also eligible for state care because of their state contributions. In the voluntary sector, there are long-established organizations, which continue the tradition of charitable help for the needy and depend for their funding mainly upon donations from the public.

The state social services have been under financial pressure for many years. For example, Conservative governments (1979–97 and 2010–15) tried

to reform the National Health Service and social security in order to reduce expenditure, improve efficiency, encourage more self-provision and target benefits to those genuinely in need. These have been widely criticized, and it is argued that they are based on an economic free market orientation.

A previous Labour government from 1997 also tried to promote change in the expensive welfare state by encouraging people to insure themselves against unemployment and sickness and to provide for their own pensions and care in old age. Such reforms have tried to help families, reduce poverty and exclusion, and made efforts to return the unemployed to work. But it is argued that the reforms have not been successful and successive governments have had to increase state spending in social services to prevent their decay and possible collapse.

This situation suggests that the UK and its devolved countries in future may be unable (or unwilling) to meet the financial costs of state social services without increases in personal income tax, alternative funding schemes or cuts in aid plans. Government’s role in the future may lie in directing aid rather than funding it. The Labour government has tried to involve the private sector in the creation and management of public services (such as hospitals). But there is public and trade union opposition to this policy, which is often perceived as privatization of ‘free’ social services. This illustrates the difficult y of reconciling state demand with a national ‘free market economy’ system and of deciding how much dependence there should be upon the state. A recurrent issue in general election campaigns has been how to pay for the National Health Service (NHS). Governments are often forced to provide budget increases for the NHS, but have not solved its problems. Critics argue that without large increases in income tax, alternative funding and improved management, the social services will collapse. For example, the Conservative government in January 2019 introduced a ten-year plan for the NHS based on tax funding, but was also struggling to implement a new state social security system.

##### Social services history

Historically, state social services were non-existent for most of the British population. Churches, charities, the rural feudal system and town guilds (organizations of skilled craftsmen) did give some protection against poverty, illness and unemployment. But this help was limited in its application and effect. Most people were thrown upon their own, often minimal, resources in order to survive.

In Elizabeth I’s reign (1558–1603), a Poor Law was established in England, by which the state took over the organization of charity provisions. Similar schemes existed in Wales, Scotland and Ireland. They operated at the local parish level and were responsible for their poor, sick and unemployed, by providing

housing, help and work relief. The Poor Law was the start of state social help in Britain, but it was grudging, was limited in its effects and discouraged people from relying on it. Poverty and need were considered to be the result of an unwillingness to work and provide for oneself, a criticism which is still directed at welfare recipients and the unemployed in Britain today. The state was not supposed to have extensive responsibility for social services.

These attitudes persisted, although urban and rural poverty and need continued. Conditions worsened in the eighteenth and nineteenth centuries as industrial revolutions expanded and the population rapidly increased. The urban workforce had to work long hours, often in bad conditions, in low-quality factories for low wages. Families frequently inhabited slums of overcrowded, back-to-back dwellings which lacked adequate sewerage, heating or ventilation, in which some people also had to work in cramped and unhealthy surroundings. The situation of many rural agricultural workers was just as bad.

Public health became a concern and the poor conditions resulted in infectious epidemics in the nineteenth century, such as measles, diphtheria, typhoid, smallpox and tuberculosis. Some diseases remained endemic in the population in the twentieth century because of bad housing, and the lack of adequate health and social facilities.

The old Poor Law was replaced by the Poor Law Amendment Act of 1834 in England and Wales (later in Scotland). It was designed to prevent the alleged abuse of parish social relief and to reduce the taxes needed to service the system. It created a system of workhouses in which the destitute and needy could work and live. However, these were unpleasant places and people were discouraged from relying upon them. They were dreaded by the poor and accepted only as a last resort. Since nineteenth-century Britain experienced economic slumps and unemployment, the workhouse system often resulted in misery and the separation of families.

Successive governments until the nineteenth century refused to allow workers to organize themselves into trade unions, through which they might agitate against their working and living conditions. This forced groups of workers into establishing their own social and self-help clubs in order to provide basic protection. Although some employers were more benevolent than others and provided good housing and health facilities for their workers, such examples were few and life continued to be harsh for many in both the towns and the countryside.

The social misery of the nineteenth century persuaded some towns to establish local boards to control public health and initiate health schemes, though a public health apparatus was not created until 1848 and an effective national system was not in place until 1875. Legislation was passed to clean up slum areas, but large-scale clearance was not achieved until the mid-twentieth century. Reforms in housing, health, factory and mine conditions, sanitation and sewerage, town-planning and trade unionism were implemented in the

nineteenth century. But they were limited in their effects and were seen as paternalistic in their intention.

The social welfare problems of the nineteenth century were considerable and the state’s failure to provide adequate help against illness, unemployment and poverty made the situation worse. Social reformers, who promoted legislation offering some relief from the negative effects of industrialization, had to struggle against the apathy and hostility of vested interests in Parliament and the country.

However, small victories had been won and in the early twentieth century it was slowly, if not universally, admitted that the state had social responsibility for the whole of society. Progressive Liberal governments between 1905 and 1922 introduced reform programmes on old age pensions, national insurance, health, employment and trade unionism. These formed the basic structures of the future welfare state. But they affected only a minority of people, and the state was unwilling or unable to introduce further provisions in the early twentieth century. The financial and physical exhaustion resulting from the 1914–18 World War and the economic crises of the 1920s and 1930s halted social services expansion.

The underlying need for more state help continued as the population rapidly increased. The model for a welfare state appeared in the Beveridge Report of 1942. This recommended that a comprehensive system of social security and health care for all should be established to overcome suffering and need ‘from the cradle to the grave’. It was intended that the system would be largely financed by a national insurance (NI) scheme, to which workers would contribute, and out of which they and their families would receive benefits when required. Although Conservative governments passed some of the legislation to implement these proposals, it was the 1945–51 Labour government that radically altered the social and health systems and created the present welfare state. It was also gradually realized that most of the cost of the system would have to be provided for by general taxation, which would share the financial burden with National Insurance, and in some cases means-testing.

##### Household and family structures

The provision of contemporary social services, in both the state and the private sectors, is conditioned by changes in family and household structures, demographic factors (such as birth rates and increases in life expectancy), governmental responses to social needs, the cost of services and individuals’ personal financial resources.

It is argued that the traditional British household type (two parents and children living together) is fragmenting into new units. These significant changes may incur substantial financial burdens for the family groups and British society.

In 2017, there were 27.2 million households in Great Britain (see Table 9.1), but a trend towards smaller units resulted in the average household size falling to 2.4 people. The most common type was the one-family household consisting of a couple, with or without children (15.7 million), which has seen a small decline. The next most common, and increasing, household type was the one-person household at 7.7 million, with one person living alone. The third most common type was the one-family household of a lone parent with dependent children in 2.7 million units.

People have also combined with two or more unrelated adults to form a group-sharing household category (0.8 million). Multi-family households with two or more families or relations were the fastest growing household type (0.3 million). Members may be unrelated or related, such as a married couple living with their son and his girlfriend. This type has increased recently due to young people being unemployed or unable to afford a property of their own.

It is predicted that traditional opposite-sex married couples will in future be outnumbered by those people who choose not to marry. The proportion of unmarried men is expected to increase more than that of unmarried women and the rise in cohabiting couples (couples of the same or different genders living together outside marriage) may not compensate for the decrease in married couples.

These predictions are complicated because the definition of marriage as between male and female changed in 2013 to allow people of the same sex/gender to be legally married. Additionally, in 2004 same-sex couples in England and Wales could obtain legal recognition of their relationships by registering as civil partners, and were able to convert their relationship to marriage from 2014. By 2011, some 53,417 civil partnerships were registered, and there were 1,409 same-sex marriages by 2014 (56 per cent being female and 44 per cent male). It is argued that civil partnerships will decline in popularity and be overtaken by marriage. Household and family groupings have produced their own sex/gender identities in response to changing views and legislation (see Table 9.2).

The status of same-sex marriage and other gender questions in Britain are debated. An ONS survey in 2016 reported that 2.0 per cent of UK adults aged

16 and over gave their sexual identity as lesbian, gay or bisexual (LGB), 1.2 per cent as gay or lesbian, and 0.8 as bisexual. Males were twice as likely as females to reveal their sexual identity as gay or lesbian. However, transgender persons did not appear in these figures, although transgender persons have had the right to change their legal gender since 2005 (LGBT).

More adults will be living alone in the future. There has been a significant increase over the past 20 years in one-person households with no children. These are people of all ages who may be single by choice, divorced, separated, widows or widowers and the largest number in 2017 were women.

While marriage is the most common form of partnership for men and women, it is becoming less popular. ONS figures show that there were 239,020 marriages between opposite-sex couples in 2015 in England and Wales, a 3.4 per cent decrease from 2014. Some 30 per cent were religious marriages, 70 per cent were civil marriages, usually in a register Office, and 15 per cent were remarriages for both parties. More people delay marriage for career and other reasons with the average age in 2015 being 37.5 years for men and 35.1 for women.

The average British marriage which ends in divorce lasts 11 years and six months. The rate of divorce in England and Wales fluctuates and has recently been declining. Remarriages are at greater risk than first marriages and people who marry under 21 are the most susceptible to divorce. The rate for remarriages among men is double that of women and the most common divorce ages for both women and men are between 25 to 29 years. Divorce affects a considerable number of children under 16. The trauma is increased by the confrontational nature of the divorce system, with conflicts over property, financial support and custody of children.

A recent decline in divorce rates has arguably coincided with an increase in cohabitation (same-sex couples, opposite-sex couples and civil partners living together outside marriage). In 2013, there were 2.9 million opposite-sex cohabiting families and 89,000 same-sex cohabiting families in England and Wales and the number may rise over the next 25 years (see Table 9.2). Some of these relationships are stable and long-term and eight out of ten resulting births to couples are registered by both parents, rather than one as previously. Adoption of children by civil partners and same-sex couples, and surrogacy are also increasing.

ONS figures reported that the UK population grew to an estimated

65.1 million in 2015, and marked an increase of half a million people since 2014. In 2017, there were 766,000 people in the UK aged 18, but this number has been decreasing. The population is becoming older with 18 per cent aged 65 and over and 2.4 per cent aged 85 and over. It is projected that the population will reach over 70 million by 2050, although there was a 2.5 per cent decrease in births and fertility rates in England and Wales in 2017.

Average life expectancy in 2014 was 80.42 years, with men at 78.26 years and women at 82.69 years, and there are more women among the elderly. But the number of older people in the population is expected to grow less quickly in future than in recent years.

Although the population is growing, child-bearing is being delayed, with women in Britain having their first child on average at 28, seven years older than in 1971. Some women are delaying child-bearing even longer for educational and career reasons, and there has been an increase in the number of single women and married/unmarried couples who choose to remain childless, or to limit their families.

There has been a growth in the number of one-parent families with dependent children since 1961. In 2017, some 3 million children were being raised in 2.8 million one-parent families. Some 91 per cent of these units are headed by a mother and 9 per cent by a father. Of the women bringing up one-parent families 16 per cent are single, 34 per cent are divorced, 22 per cent are separated and 17 per cent are widowed. Lone fathers tend to be either divorced or widowers. Some of these families (with the highest proportion being in inner London) often have reduced living standards and some are dependent upon social security benefits.

The proportion of working-age women in employment is now some 70 per cent. More women are returning to work more quickly after the birth of a child and women make up 45 per cent of the workforce. But although Britain has a high percentage of working mothers and wives, provisions for maternity leave and child care are low in European terms.

The various household units have to cope with increased demands upon them, which may entail personal sacrifice. They carry out most of the caring roles in British society, rather than state professionals. Only 6 per cent of people

over 65 and 7 per cent of disabled adults live in state or private institutions. Most disabled children and adults are cared for by their families and most of the elderly are either cared for by families or live alone supported by the social services (Social Care). These figures represent a saving to the state, without which the cost of state health and social care would rise to very demanding levels. However, the burden upon families will grow as the population becomes more elderly, state provision is reduced and the numbers of disabled (currently 6 million adults) and disadvantaged increase. There are proposals that more social care state aid should be given to carers, families and local authorities to lighten their burden and to enable the elderly to stay in their own homes rather than in expensive care homes or hospitals.

The picture that emerges from these statistics is one of smaller households; more people living alone; an increase in one-parent units; a declining divorce rate; more individuals living longer and contributing to an ageing population; more working mothers and wives; more cohabiting couples; new structures such as civil partnerships; gender classifications; and a decline in opposite-sex marriage. These features influence the contemporary state and private provisions for social security, health, social care and housing. They are very different to previous generations.

##### Social security (pensions and welfare benefits)

A new social security system was introduced by Conservative governments in 2014–17. It provides pension and welfare payments to individuals and their dependent families. It is organized by Department for Work and Pensions (DWP) offices in England and by devolved agencies in Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland.

Eligible British people have since the mid-twentieth century received state contributory and non-contributory benefits. Contributory payments are those where workers contribute to the National Insurance Fund (NI) and income tax system during their working lives and receive benefits on retirement or when necessary. Non-contributory benefits are awarded to claimants who lack the necessary contributions and are disabled, ill or in need. Some benefits may involve means-testing of personal resources or discretionary considerations before aid is granted.

The contributory system has provided state retirement pensions for employed persons at 65, rising to 66–67 and possibly to 68 by 2050; maternity pay for partners who take leave from work to care for new children; sick pay for people who are absent from work because of illness or who become incapable of work; and allowances for those who become unemployed. A wide range of non-contributory aid has been historically provided to the elderly, one-parent families and the long-term sick in the form of, for example,

Housing Benefit, Disability Allowance, Child Benefit, dental treatment, eye testing and children’s school meals.

Under the old system, there were some 30 million people in the UK who received income and assistance from social security. There were a very large number of different payments and at 29 per cent of total government expenditure, social security represented the largest single area of state spending. Public retirement pensions were the most expensive item, accounting for some 37 per cent of total expenditure and it was argued that the pension could become unsustainable without further reform and raised qualifying ages. The other main categories of benefit recipients are generally families with children (18.4 per cent), unemployed people (2.5 per cent), people on low incomes (20.8 per cent), elderly people (42.3 per cent), sick and disabled people (15.53 per cent) and bereaved people (0.31 per cent).

Social security benefits do provide a degree of security for many British people. They are supposed to be a safety net against need, but this does not prevent relative hardship. Some 13.5 million people with 1.8 million children live in households below the low-income threshold (7279 a week for a couple with two dependent children under 14). Other studies suggest that a quarter of the population live on the poverty line, which is sometimes measured as 60 per cent of the average annual national income. But accurate poverty figures are difficult to gather because there are different definitions of what constitutes poverty.

Social security benefits are expensive and will become more so as the numbers of the sick, disadvantaged, elderly, poor, disabled and unemployed persist. The number of benefits has grown and can be difficult to understand. It is alleged that they encourage a dependency and welfare culture in which successive generations of some families have never worked.

Governments have tried to reform the welfare system by attacking abuse, cutting expense and reducing benefits while still preserving the safety net commitment, targeting those people with the greatest needs, and cutting poverty and exclusion, while encouraging people into employment and away from a lifetime on benefits. Governments argue that the cost of social security is unsustainable and try to encourage self-provision through work. But it is difficult to limit the numbers and to create a simple and fair system that protects the genuine needy and persuades people to become more self-reliant and independent.

Governments are concerned that people should look after themselves without automatic recourse to the state for help. Political parties agree that it is unacceptable that those who are capable of work should reject it for a life on benefits. The creation of jobs, an embrace of the work ethic and greater personal responsibility are seen as essential in the face of a potentially difficult economic future, social uncertainties, a growing elderly population and pension difficulties. For example, it is now generally accepted that workers should work longer before claiming their state pensions (65 years at present,

but moving to 66–68), and save to supplement their pensions while in work. More people are also now accepting the chance to work longer, and workplace ageism is decreasing.

The coalition government from 2010 began to reform welfare benefits, and argued that too many people were trapped on benefits. After delays, changes were gradually phased in in different parts of the country from 2014. They were designed to make work pay rather than people seeing their income drop when they moved off benefits. It has tried to simplify and reform the system by merging former working-age benefits and tax credits into a single payment, called Universal Credit (UC).

UC was supposed to be paid monthly in arrears and is based on people trying to move into work. But it can take up to five weeks to receive the first UC payment after a successful claim, leaving some recipients without interim finance. The amount of UC received is gradually reduced as more money is earned. Claims are made online, and different systems apply in Northern Ireland, Wales and Scotland.

In England, UC is a single monthly payment for people in or out of work, and will replace single benefits and tax credits. It comprises a standard allowance to cover housing, duties of carers, child-care costs, disabled children and ill or disabled adults. The maximum UC award comprises one allowance for a household and any family circumstances. An applicant will receive the maximum amount if the household has no other earnings, savings or capital of 76,000 or less. Other household earnings will be taken into account when calculating the UC payment.

A person on UC can receive paid work and qualify for a work allowance, or the amount of money one is allowed to earn before the UC payment is affected. This work allowance depends upon whether one is responsible for dependent children or is unable to work because of illness or disability. The UC payment will be reduced if earnings exceed the work allowance (earnings taper). Earned or paid benefits, such as Maternity, Paternity and Sick Pay are treated as earnings and will also affect the taper. Some unearned income, such as Jobseeker’s Allowance and Pension income will also affect the UC payment. But other unearned income (Child Benefit, Maintenance payments and Disability Living Allowance) will not be deducted from UC payment.

The previous welfare system was seen as complex, difficult to understand, faulty, allowed people who could work to remain on welfare benefits and gave them insufficient incentives to start working again. But it is argued that applying for the new UC can also be stressful.

The introduction of UC from 2013–14 was meant to improve the previous system by ensuring that any benefits that a person received would in future be paid in one lump sum. The idea was to encourage people to find paid work, increase their working hours, and move into a new working environment. The new system is supposed to be simpler, so that those who find jobs do not have

to worry about increased poverty. The changes were also intended to reduce error and fraud.

However, a study in 2016 by the Fabian Society argued that the new system was inadequate, most low-income working households would be worse off by 2020, and families on out-of-work benefits face losing a ffth of their income because of successive government cuts and squeezes in public spending. Without an overhaul of UC and social security policy, the crisis in living standards for poorer families will become worse as their incomes deteriorate, and child poverty and inequality will rise sharply. The Labour Party argued that what was needed was a system that takes account of contemporary social realities, such as low pay, high housing costs, insecure work, longer working lives and caring responsibilities. Otherwise, social security spending under current policy is likely to halve in the next few years.

Critics concluded that the roll-out of the UC scheme should be halted because it was flawed in execution, unjustly varied in operation, and was as complex as its predecessor. The Conservative government in 2019 was forced to delay the implementation of people moving on to UC because of the problems associated with it, such as delays in payments and correct assessment of individual cases.

##### The National Health Service (NHS)

The National Health Service (NHS) was created by a Labour government in 1946 and was implemented across the UK in 1948. It was based on the Beveridge Report recommendations and replaced a private system of payment for health care by one of free health treatment for all at the point of need. Some in the medical profession wished to retain private medicine and opposed the establishment of the NHS, but this was countered by the government.

The NHS was originally intended to be free for those needing medical help, irrespective of income. This ideal, despite continuing problems, has been largely achieved, and the system is centrally funded through the income taxes and National Insurance contributions of people while in work. Hospital and most medical treatment under the NHS are free for British citizens, those foreign citizens legally resident in Britain and EU visitors to Britain under the European Health Insurance Card (EHIC). The NHS provides a comprehensive range of medical and dental services based on hospitals and medical practices, doctors, dentists, nurses, midwives, ambulance services, blood transfusion units and other health facilities.

However, some health services and prescriptions (written notes from a doctor enabling patients to obtain drugs from a chemist or pharmacist), dental work and eye tests have to be paid for (with exceptions in Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland). Payments are dependent upon employment status, age and

income. Children under 16, people on social security benefits and old age pensioners receive free prescriptions, eye tests and subsidized dentistry. Coverage of NHS dental treatment is limited and many dentists have left the NHS for private practice.

The complicated health structures in the UK originated in legislation from 1946–69. Devolution in 1998 completed a separation process, but while the four devolved systems in England, Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland are mostly independent from each other and handle their own health matters, some functions are shared. The term NHS usually refers to the UK collectively, the UK government funds the NHS for the whole UK, and has control over the English NHS through the Department of Health and Social Care.

The individual systems are National Health Service NHS (England), Health and Social Care in Northern Ireland, NHS Scotland and NHS Wales. NHS England also underwent reorganization after the passing of the Health and Social Care Act 2012. Since each devolved country has its own political structures, divergent programmes have developed despite basic similarities. For example, payments (such as prescription charges) exist in England, but not in the other systems.

The UK Parliament sets the overall budget available to the NHS in England and allocates a block grant to each devolved government, which decides how much to spend on its regional health-care needs. Health expenditure in England for 2017–18 was 7122 billion, 713.2 billion in Scotland, 77.3 billion in Wales and 75 billion in Northern Ireland. The NHS is the biggest single employer of labour in Western Europe. However, compared with other G7 countries, GDP expenditure is relatively low and lower than other major Western countries which have a higher combination of the public and the private in their health spending. UK funding derives from UK taxation and National Insurance contributions. Other sources of income include charging non-EU overseas visitors and insurers for the cost of NHS treatment, prescriptions, dental charges and hospital car parking.

There has been much criticized restructuring of NHS England by successive governments. In 2013, the existing 152 Primary Care Trusts (PCTs), which controlled local spending on dentists, hospital operations, tests and medicines were organized mainly by health managers and accounted for 80 per cent of NHS spending. Financial and health commissioning duties (of primary care) were given to 200 Clinical Commissioning Groups (CCGs) led by General Practitioners (GPs or doctors). Every GP surgery had to belong to a CCG. A Clinical Commissioning Board was created which took charge of overseeing the NHS from the Department of Health. Hospitals had Trust or Foundation status (referred or secondary care based on referrals from GPs) and would be independent of Whitehall control. The Conservative government promised to cut bureaucratic costs, but this exercise in reform and the resulting structures have been subject to widespread criticism.

###### Doctors

Most people in Britain who require health care will first consult an NHS-funded doctor, who is a GP or non-specialist general practitioner, of whom, for example, there were in 2016 about 41,985 in England in 7,613 practices and 2,887 in Wales in 454 practices. There are difficulties with losses, retention and recruitment of doctors. The majority of GPs are now members of group practices, where they share larger premises, services and equipment, which also allow for minor surgery. A patient may be on the panel of one named doctor, although appointments with overstretched GPs can be difficult to make. Alternatives are an NHS drop-in centre or Minor Care Unit, or in urgent cases the Accident and Emergency Department at a local hospital.

###### Hospitals

If patients require further treatment or examination, the GP refers them to specialists and consultants, normally at local NHS Trust hospitals or Foundation hospitals. Both types of hospitals are ‘self-governing’ to a large extent, run their own independent budgets and services, and provide medical, dental, nursing and midwifery staff. It is argued that although NHS England’s number of hospital beds has fallen from 299,000 to 142,000 in the last 30 years, the number of total patients being treated in hospital has increased because of greater input– output efficiency. Britain has some modern hospitals and facilities and more hospitals are being constructed, but it also has many buildings that were built in the nineteenth century and are in need of modernization and repair. There is a shortage of beds in some hospitals, yet wards and hospitals are being closed. Long waiting times for consultancy visits and admission to hospital for operations as well as for treatment in accident and emergency departments are still a source of concern, despite a large infusion of government funds into the system and improvement in some areas.

###### The state of the NHS

The NHS has an ambivalent position in the public mind. On one hand, it is praised for its work as a free service and its achievements. It is considered a success in terms of consumer demand. Today people are in general receiving help when they need it and many who would previously have died or suffered are surviving and being cared for. Standards of living and medicine have risen; better diets have been devised and there is greater health awareness in the population at large.

On the other hand, the NHS is criticized by some for its alleged inefficiency, waste, cost, variation in treatment throughout the country, medical scandals and bureaucracy. Its objectives are considered too ambitious for the amount of

PLATE 9.1 Doctors’ waiting room at busy GPs’ practice, National Health Service. © age fotostock/Alamy Stock Photo

PLATE 9.2 NHS operating theatre in action, Chelsea and Westminster Hospital, London.

© Photofusion/Shutterstock

PLATE 9.3 University College Hospital, London, one of the new NHS hospitals being constructed in many parts of Britain. © Jeff Blackler/Shutterstock

money spent on it. The media constantly draw attention to shortcomings (such as hygiene problems, breakdown, infectious disease outbreaks in hospitals and lack of care and concern for patients). Workers in the NHS, such as doctors, nurses and non-medical staff, complain about low pay, long hours, management weaknesses, levels of staffing (with a shortage of doctors and nurses), and cuts in services. In the past, it was suggested that such problems could be solved simply by injecting more money into the NHS, but increased spending by government has not eradicated what many critics see as a managerial inability to organize the funds competently at the points where they are needed. Alleged inefficiency and delays at general practice level lead people to see Accident and Emergency Departments in hospitals as the only point of entry for help, which leads to overcrowding and near-breakdown of the A&E system. The demands upon the NHS have grown hugely, piling further pressures upon staff. In the 2017–18 winter, the NHS had great difficult y in meeting demand.

Rising costs and increased demand provoke cries for more finance and resources. The NHS is in many ways a victim of its own success and of the demands that the British place upon it as of right. It is inevitable that a free, consumer-driven service will require increasing levels of expenditure, better

management of existing resources, more political competence from government, greater understanding from the public (including their use of the GP appointments system) or alternative funding. Yet despite problems, such as increasing physical assaults upon health professionals in hospitals and surgeries, an upsurge in waiting times in GP surgeries and A&E, and for hospital appointments and operations, together with other undoubted pressures, it is generally accepted that much of the NHS works well, gives value for the money spent on it and attracts popular support.

There are many suggestions as to how the NHS can be improved, but each can have unfortunate results. Increased government spending on the NHS may require increased taxation, although research for the NHS Confederation in 2018 found that 77 per cent of respondents to a poll supported a Conservative government plan for a 4 per cent per year rise in health spending over five years, amounting to 720.5 billion yearly, and there was some backing for a 3.9 per cent rise in spending for social care. The increases were those deemed necessary for the NHS to make improvements for health and social care to meet demands, according to the Institute for Fiscal Studies and the Health Foundation. Forty-two per cent of the public agreed that this would mean income tax rises for taxpayers and 45 per cent that it involved increased national insurance.

Charges could be made for some services, but this would go against the principle of free health care, although polls show that many respondents are prepared to pay for some NHS care if it meant an overall better service. More efficient management of existing funds, or the replacement of allegedly underperforming managers, might make some efficiency savings, but possibly not enough. Combining a public service with private insurance as in other countries might not include poorer people, who would still be dependent upon a free NHS.

Previous Labour governments had involved the private sector more closely in the running of the NHS (for example, through private finance initiatives or PFIs to build new hospitals) and paying for patient care in private hospitals to reduce strain on the NHS. But many people regard these schemes as privatization of the NHS and some private contracts for building projects and services have not been successful and led to the near-bankruptcy of some hospitals, clinics and care homes.

Governments have tried to raise NHS spending, reduce management costs, transfer money to medical care and staff, and reorganize NHS administration. But the need for more money continues. The public, according to polls, have seen little actual improvement, are apparently disillusioned with attempts to revitalize the NHS and are very concerned about health facilities. But they still continue to support the NHS in polls. A survey of eight European countries, Canada, the USA and Australia in June 2014 by the Commonwealth Fund showed that the UK ranked first in overall performances, such as quality of care, access, efficiency, cost, equity of service and other indicators such as infant

mortality rates. However, it lagged on health outcomes in tenth place and was in third position for timelines of care.

###### The private medical sector

It is argued that health care should not be a question of who can pay for it, but a responsibility of the state. However, attempts have been made to involve the private sector in providing health care.

Governments have encouraged the growth of private health institutions, private medical insurance and commercial partnerships between the public and private sectors. For example, the controversial private finance initiative (PFI) allows new health facilities, such as hospitals to be built and maintained by the private sector. These are then leased to the NHS, which provides clinical services and controls planning and clinical decisions. However, PFI arrangements have run into problems and NHS hospital Trusts have found it difficult to service and pay for their loans and leases. In theory, the private sector could be seen as complementary to the NHS. It can release pressure on state funds; gives choice to patients; allows the sharing of medical resources; provides flexible services; results in cost-effective cooperation with the NHS; and allows treatment of NHS patients at public expense in the private sector. However, PFI arrangements have been problematic and NHS hospital Trusts have found it difficult to service and pay for their loans and leases.

But agreements are made with other private health-care providers to enable the NHS to make better use of facilities in private hospitals. Some NHS hospitals also share expensive equipment with private hospitals, and NHS patients are treated (at public expense) in the private sector when it represents value for money. However, the scale of private medical practice in relation to the NHS is small. Much private treatment is confined to relatively minor medical cases and expensive, long-term high-technology care is still carried out mainly by the NHS. About a quarter of all operations and health care in Britain are paid for privately by patients out of their income, capital or insurance policies. Some

6.9 million individuals and 4.8 million people in company plans are covered by private medical insurance taken out with businesses such as the British United Provident Association (BUPA). Concern about waiting lists and alleged standards of health care in the NHS persuade many to take out such insurance. The insurance policy pays for private care either in private hospitals and clinics, or in NHS hospitals which provide ‘pay-beds’ (beds for the use of paying patients). These still exist in NHS hospitals and were a concession in 1946 to those doctors who agreed to join the NHS but who wished to keep a number of private patients. Opinion polls report that a significant minority of people sometimes consider going private to avoid lengthy NHS queues, although in practice it is estimated that the majority of Britons do not have private medical insurance. Many people today are likely to be deterred by the rising costs of insurance premiums and private treatment.

##### The personal social services/social care

###### The state sector

State social care services provide facilities by local government and other agencies in the local community which assist people, such as the elderly, the disabled, the mentally ill, families, children and young people. Unlike the NHS, which is funded directly from the government, social care is funded by local councils and clients may be means-tested on the cost of the services provided to them. Trained staff, such as district visitors, nurses, social workers and care assistants cater for these personal needs. The services are organized by local government and devolved authorities with UK government funding. While it is argued that state social services need extra public money to address problems, local and devolved authorities are selling or effectively privatizing parts of the sector to avoid costs and to maintain basic services. For example, mental health services are seeing more people seeking help, but local government spends only

1.4 per cent of their public health budgets on mental health. A crucial question is whether the inclusion of Social Care in the Department of Health will improve the overall provision of social care. At present, there are funding problems for local government and its constituent parts.

Increasing pressure is being put upon the social services, families and carers as the elderly population grows and the ranks of the disadvantaged rise. For example, the number of public residential care and nursing homes for the elderly is insufficient for the demand. Some have been sold off to save costs or increased their fees, while some private homes close because of expense and reduced profit. In both cases, elderly people may be forced to sell their own homes to cover some or all of their expenses in the remaining, but decreasing, public and private nursing or care homes. However, in Scotland residential and medical care is free under devolved legislation. Care services for the elderly and infirm in both private and state sectors face severe staff shortage unless higher pay and better training for workers are introduced and the system operates on a better foundation.

Abuse of vulnerable patients and residents in care homes continues to be a problem, particularly in the 17,000 homes in England, of which 1,200 have been warned about the quality of residents’ welfare treatment. The low pay and inadequate training of care assistants is a concern and there are difficulties in contributions that local councils are supposed to pay at a time of budget cuts.

Various ‘Care in the Community’ programmes have been introduced by Conservative and Labour governments with limited success. The aim was to give financial and material support to families and carers looking after elderly or disabled relatives in the latter’s own homes or for children and adults with disabilities in the family home. It also allowed psychiatric patients who do not need constant care to be moved to the community under social services supervision

and for elderly and disabled people to be cared for in their own homes by social services. The aim was to prevent the institutionalization of people and to give them independence.

The scheme had difficulties, such as mentally ill and disabled patients becoming homeless, being housed in inadequate temporary accommodation and neglecting their medication, while elderly people receive poor or insufficient attention and help by hard-pressed official carers. It is argued that local authorities need more support, that helpers and carers should receive better pay and conditions, and that it should be recognized that home supervision of the elderly is not only cheaper than hospital, but what they wish for.

The personal social services also cater for people with learning disabilities, give help to families and provide day-care facilities for children. Children in need or suffering from family breakdown and dysfunctional parents are also supposed to be protected in residential care accommodation and local authorities facilitate fostering and adoption services. But there have been a number of serious cases in recent years which have drawn attention to the physical and sexual abuse in children’s care homes and grooming gangs in the community which prey on young people, as well as child deaths in family homes from neglect and abuse. Local government social services, which are often hard pressed, have been heavily criticized.

In 2018, actual spending on social care was 9 per cent lower per person than ten years ago, despite extra government funding and councils trying to protect services by switching money from other budgets. According to the Institute for Fiscal Studies, this meant that local authority spending on adult social care dropped by 10 per cent in real terms between 2009 and 2015.

Other services (excluding adult social care) provided by local authorities have been cut more heavily, with average cuts per person of 32 per cent although there were big variations with councils in poorer areas making larger cuts. In 2018, councils in the 30 most deprived areas of England cut social care budgets overall by an average of 17 per cent, compared with 3 per cent in the 30 least deprived.

The Conservative election manifesto in 2017 proposed to reform social care, arguing that better-off older people should pay more for their social care, including care at home. Suggestions were also made to make the health and care systems work more closely together. Adult social care faced fundamental changes in order to provide a system that worked for everyone in society.

###### The private social care services and voluntary sectors

While there were improvements in state social care services in the twentieth century, there is still a shortage of finance, resources and effective policies to support all the needy and disadvantaged in a comprehensive fashion and their numbers continue to rise. The private sector supplies various care facilities, but

these are also declining because of costs. It is therefore important that voluntary charities and agencies have remained and continued their work. These are a complementary welfare service to the state and private facilities and provide an essential element in the total aid pattern. The state system would be unable to cover all needs without them.

Most of the voluntary agencies have charitable status, which means that they gain tax concessions on their income, but receive no (or very little) financial support from the state. However, some groups, such as those dealing with drug and alcohol addiction and released prisoners, do receive financial grants from central and local government. There are many thousands of voluntary organizations in Britain, operating at national and local levels and varying considerably in size. Some are small and collect limited amounts of money or donations from the public. Others are very large, have professional staffs and receive millions of pounds from many different sources. Some groups, such as Oxfam (for the relief of famine) and the Save the Children Fund, have now become international organizations, although Oxfam and others suffered from destabilizing accusations of sexual abuse in their foreign operations in 2017–18.

The following are examples of voluntary agencies in Britain. Barnardo’s provides help for needy children; the Church of England Children’s Society cares for neglected children and is Britain’s largest adoption agency; the Cancer Research Fund gathers finance and carries out research into cures for cancer; the People’s Dispensary for Sick Animals (PDSA) provides free veterinary aid for people’s pets; the Samaritans give telephone help to the suicidal; women’s groups have founded refuges for abused women; and Help the Aged campaigns for the elderly.

##### Housing

Housing in the UK is divided between the public and private sectors. These are overseen by the Ministry of Housing, Communities and Local Government in England and by devolved bodies in Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland. There is at present a serious lack of affordable properties to buy or rent in Britain, and to simplify matters this section will refer only to the housing situation in England.

According to ONS statistics in 2017, there were 23.9 million domestic dwellings in England (a slight increase since 2016). The majority consisted of

15.1 million owner-occupied (private) dwellings, 4.8 million private dwellings rented from a private landlord or letting agency and 4.0 million social and affordable rented dwellings (Private Registered Providers and Local Authority). The private rented stock decreased from 2016, the social and affordable rented stock had increased and public sector stock had decreased. These figures illustrate the very variable state of the housing market in recent years, such as the

increase in private ownership, the decrease in private rented properties owned by ‘buy-to-let’ landlords, and increased renting from Private Registered Providers (such as Housing Associations and other social housing landlords), despite the fall in Local Authority properties.

There were 605,891 vacant dwellings in England in 2017, an increase of

2.7 per cent from 2016, and vacant dwellings were 2.5 per cent of the housing stock. Long-term vacant dwellings had increased by 2.6 per cent and such buildings are 0.9 per cent of the housing stock.

Social housing is in the public, subsidized sector and is rented by low income tenants from local government authorities or housing associations (non-profit-making bodies which manage and build homes for rent and sale with the aid of government grants), and housing cooperatives or charitable trusts. These figures for England (as for the rest of the UK) show a small decline in home ownership, but a rise in private and social housing renting, after a long period from 1979 when private home ownership grew steadily.

Some 91 per cent of the population live in houses or bungalows (single storey houses) and the remainder in flats and maisonettes (about 9 per cent). Houses are traditionally divided into detached (22 per cent), semi-detached (32 per cent) and terraced housing (27 per cent), with the greater prices and prestige being given to detached property.

PLATE 9.4 Detached house on a new housing estate, Surrey, England. Detached houses command the highest prices on the housing market, particularly as here in southern England. But note the relatively small-sized house, small windows and high-density plot. © Shutterstock

PLATE 9.5 Terraced housing, Kensal Rise, London. Terraced housing, where houses are joined together in a row, varies in quality and type throughout Britain. These are popular and can be expensive, depending on location and condition. © Ray Tang/Shutterstock

Much social, public or ‘council housing’ has historically been provided by local councils with finance from local councils and central government. Although the provision and organization of such properties by local government has declined in recent years and more has been taken over by housing associations, some local councils are increasingly building properties for rent by local tenants.

Conservative governments (1979–97) encouraged the growth of private home ownership in the housing market, as part of its programme to create a property and share-owning democracy in Britain. In the public sector, the Conservatives were critical of local government housing policies. They wanted local authorities to divest themselves of housing management and instead, work with housing associations and the private sector to increase the supply of low-cost housing for rent without building it themselves. The Conservatives also controversially introduced (1980) a right-to-buy policy by which local government sells off council housing to sitting tenants at below-market prices. This policy has increased the number of home-owners by over 1 million and relieved local authorities of the expense of decoration, upkeep and repair. The Conservatives in their 2015 Election Manifesto also announced that they would allow Housing Associations to sell their houses at discount rates to existing tenants.

The Labour Party, after initially opposing the right-to-buy policy, accepted it, mainly because it proved attractive to tenants. Later Labour governments transferred back the revenue from council sales into local government (which previously had not been able to spend it) so that it can provide more low-cost social housing. Labour therefore returned some control over housing policies to local government and was sceptical of tenants who bought council properties to quickly sell them on for profit on the open market. But Conservative government spending on social housing was cut to 71.5 billion of total spending in the 2014–15 budget.

The construction of new publicly funded houses has declined considerably in real terms and the private sector is not building enough low-cost or affordable properties to satisfy the need for housing. Critics argue that Conservative government housing policies contributed to a serious shortage of cheap rented accommodation in towns and rural areas for low-income groups, single people and the unemployed, at a time when demand was (and is) growing. The biggest increase in this demand is expected to be in the number of one-person households, which are projected to grow to 8.5 million by 2021. However, property prices have been increasing and there is at present a serious lack of affordable properties to buy or rent, which particularly affects young people.

PLATE 9.6 Southmere housing estate, Thamesmead, London. Council or social housing built by local government authorities for rent to low-income tenants. Many of these concrete tower blocks have now been demolished. © Cecilia Colussi/Alamy Stock Photo

The normal procedure when buying a house or flat is to save for a deposit and then take out a loan on the security of the property (a mortgage) from a building society, bank or other financial institution. The amount of money advanced on a loan depends mainly on the borrower’s salary and it is usual to borrow three times one’s gross annual salary. This long-term loan is normally paid off over a 25-year period and includes interest. Currently many people cannot afford a deposit, obtain a mortgage, or, if lucky, pay off the debt. At present rates and prices, many in the younger generation may never be able to afford to buy a property of their own.

House prices can vary considerably throughout Britain, with London and south-east England having the highest prices and northern England, Scotland and Wales having the lowest. Prices increased dramatically at the beginning of the 1970s and much property speculation occurred. Increases then stabilized for some years. But there was a price boom from 1986 to 1988, followed by high interest rates and an increase in mortgage foreclosures. This means that, when people cannot afford to continue their repayments on the loan, the lending institution takes over the property (repossession) and the occupier becomes homeless. There was also a fall in house prices, a property slump and a growth in negative equity (where the loan exceeds the value of the property), which was only slowly reversed from 1994 as interest rates were reduced and the property market recovered. House prices then increased again throughout Britain, and properties were arguably overvalued. Interest rates increased to counter the property boom. Lenders often gave high loans to borrowers who could not repay them. A credit crunch arrived in 2007 bringing a collapse in the housing market and recession due to high mortgage lending by institutions, the accumulation of personal debt by borrowers and an over-supply of new properties (such as buy-to-let flats accumulated by private landlords). Foreclosures increased. After a period from 2008 when the housing market was close to collapse, there was a government-aided growth in sales and prices by 2010. But critics in 2014 were again warning about a potential housing bubble and an overheated property market with high prices, particularly in London. On the other hand, by 2018 there was a slowdown in the buy-to-let property market and speculators were squeezed.

British homes still have variable construction standards. Many older types are damp and cold; are frequently badly built; and lack central heating, double-glazed windows and adequate insulation. Yet there has been some improvement in housing quality in recent years and most new houses have a reasonable percentage of the basic amenities. Greater attention has been paid to insulation and energy saving, aided by government grants for older properties. However, as building costs rise and available land becomes scarce, the trend in some new property construction has been towards flats and smaller, more cramped rooms in property generally.

Nevertheless, there are still districts, particularly in the centres of the big cities, where living conditions are bad and the equivalent of contemporary slums. Nearly half of the property in the inner-city areas was built before 1919 and, in spite of large-scale slum clearance in the 1950s and 1960s, much existing housing here is in barely habitable shape. Some high-rise (tower) blocks of council flats and estates in the public sector from the 1970s and 1980s have had to be demolished because of defective and dangerous structures. According to the _Independent_ newspaper in 2017, 1.4 million households containing several million people were living in unsuitable rented accommodation.

Twentieth-century town renovation and slum clearance policies from the 1930s attempted to remove the populations of large city centres to new towns, usually located in the countryside, or to new council estates in the suburbs. Some of the new towns, such as Crawley, Hemel Hempstead and Stevenage, have been seen as successes, although they initially had their share of social and planning problems. However, many other council estates have tended to degenerate very quickly. The bad design of some estates, their social deprivation and lack of upkeep are often blamed for the crime and vandalism which affect many of them. Some local councils have tried to modernize decaying housing stock, rather than spending on new development, in an attempt to preserve local communities, although others have demolished old properties, particularly in northern England and the Midlands. But renovation work is also being done by housing associations (with government grants) and by private builders.

The provision of sufficient affordable and varied housing in Britain, such as one-bedroom properties for young and single persons, has been a problem for many years. People on low (and even medium) wages, whether married or single, are often unable to afford the cost of a deposit and mortgage for suitable, but expensive, private property in the current market. Even young professionals and university graduates with substantial salaries are unable to save for a deposit and to obtain mortgages, and many live with their parents. One of the factors (in addition to high property prices and travel costs) causing difficult y for young people in buying first homes, particularly in rural areas within commuting distance of London, is the desire of affluent people for homes – or second homes – in the country. It is also difficult for young people to obtain council housing (even if available) because of long waiting lists, which contain applicants with priority over them. The right-to-buy policy has also reduced the number of appropriate council houses and flats for low-income groups and the unemployed.

In a limited housing market, it is argued that Britons should give up their obsession with exclusive home-ownership and investigate new alternative ways to meet housing demand, such as shared rentals, partor shared-ownership arrangements, and negotiated board with parents.

Conservative governments have tried to encourage landlords and other agencies to provide more privately rented accommodation by lessening the

effects of rent legislation and introducing new lease structures. But the relaxations have led to accusations of exploitation of tenants by landlords. Some landlords do not make repairs to their properties which can create dangerous living conditions. They may try to evict tenants or lose their properties in a difficult housing market, leaving their tenants homeless. The previous Labour government wanted a healthy private rented sector. It tried to improve the rights of leaseholders to purchase their freehold and protect themselves against abuse by unscrupulous landlords. At the same time, there was a growth in individuals purchasing ‘buy-to-rent’ properties which proved to be attractive investments, but which could collapse in an economic downturn, leaving the tenant homeless. The recession led to the virtual collapse of the ‘buy-to-rent’ market as landlords over-extended themselves. Currently, the housing market is at a low ebb for those without deposits and a reasonable salary.

There are many suggestions for house-building schemes in an attempt to cope with the demand for housing. However, these frequently meet objections such as building on flood plains with the consequent threat of flooding. Others could cause environmental damage, increased traffic congestion and loss of Green Belt land, although governments have permitted councils to allow high-density housing in urban areas and in some Green Belts. There have been protests about inadequate government house development, and local support which allegedly supports builders and planners. Yet, a growing population will need more and different properties.

###### The homeless and rough sleepers

Poor housing provision, poverty, joblessness, dysfunctional families and mental health problems have contributed to the number of homeless people, particularly in London and other large cities, which has led to increased social problems. Accurate numbers for the homeless are notoriously difficult to find. Official ly, government figures in 2013 reported that 112,070 people in England declared themselves as homeless to their local councils, who are legally obliged to house some categories of the homeless in temporary accommodation, such as bed and breakfast hotels, which is usually inadequate, especially for families. Charities such as Crisis, Chain, Shelter and Centrepoint give a realistic homeless total for all age groups at about 380,000 (including about 130,000 children).

In addition to the homeless, there are those sleeping rough on the streets or at other locations. According to the _Guardian_ newspaper in 2014, the number of people sleeping in the open in London grew by 75 per cent to 6,437. Other sources suggest that, on any night there might be some 2,414 people sleeping rough in England as a whole. However, figures from the Department for Communities and Local Government gave lower figures of 498 rough sleepers, with 248 in London. Some of them are visible on the streets and doorways of Britain’s large cities. Others live in squats (unoccupied houses) and temporary accommodation.

PLATE 9.7 A homeless man begging, Cromwell Road, London. The previous Labour government claimed that the number of beggars, homeless people and ‘rough sleepers’ had been reduced during its period of office. But estimated levels continue to vary considerably.

© Denis Cameron/Shutterstock

The charity, Shelter, in 2017 used government data, freedom of information returns from local authorities to estimate that 307,000 people in Great Britain are sleeping rough or accommodated (with children) in temporary housing, bed and breakfast rooms, hostels or social services housing, which marked an increase of 13,000 since 2016. London is the centre of homelessness followed by Scotland and Wales, the South-east and the West Midlands. This figure includes 4,447 people who were sleeping rough.

The reasons for homelessness and rough sleeping figures are varied, ranging from poverty to mental health, lack of affordable homes, housing benefit cuts, and inadequate state help and finance. Meanwhile the problem expands to commuter belts outside London and large provincial cities. Shelter warns ‘that more than a million households are at risk of becoming homeless by 2020’.

Homelessness is complex and affects all age groups and types of people, but it is suggested that the problem could be better handled. For example, there are some 700,000 homes (mainly in the Midlands and the north of England but increasingly in southern England) in both the private and public sectors which remain empty and unoccupied for various reasons. Critics argue that these could eradicate the problem of homelessness and the housing shortage if they were properly refurbished, utilized and managed.

Charities such as Shelter and religious organizations like the Salvation Army provide accommodation for the homeless for limited periods and campaign on their behalf. Local organizations, such as Housing Advice Centres and Housing Aid Centres, also provide help. But the problem of housing in Britain is still a major one and a focus of public concern. The high prices of many private houses, the inadequacies of the public sector market and the difficulties of some renting suggest that the problem will remain. Although the number of new starts for house construction improved in 2013–14 following the 2007–10 recession, there was a decrease towards the end of 2014.

##### Attitudes to the social services

Opinion polls indicate that British people are concerned about the social services (NHS, social care, housing, pensions and social security). An _Economist_ Ipsos MORI poll in September 2017 reported that the NHS/hospitals/health care were in second place at 40 per cent after Brexit among the most important issues/problems facing Britain. Housing was in sixth place at 16 per cent, poverty and inequality were in eighth place at 15 per cent and an ageing population and social care were in tenth place at 12 per cent. This section will be mainly concerned with the NHS.

An Ipsos MORI Political Monitor poll in June 2018 addressed the issues faced by the NHS and political attitudes about health care. When respondents were asked which political party was the best at managing the NHS and has the best policies on health care, 40 per cent said Labour, 22 per cent said Conservative, 5 per cent said Lib Dem and 1 per cent said UKIP. But 7 per cent said ‘other’, 9 per cent said ‘none’ and 17 ‘don’t know’.

There were many arguments about insufficient financing of the NHS and its potential collapse in 2017–18. The Conservative government had pledged to provide an extra 720 billion a year to the NHS for the next five years. Ipsos MORI Political Monitor asked respondents whether this extra funding was enough to generate an improvement in NHS services. Only 14 per cent said it was, and 49 per cent thought that it would only maintain current NHS performance levels.

The Monitor asked respondents which methods they would support to pay for the increased funding for the NHS announced by the government. Fifty-five per cent supported increasing individual taxes; 55 per cent favoured more charges for people on high income; 38 per cent wanted more charges for everyone; 21 per cent supported higher government spending; 20 per cent wanted reduced spending on other public services; and 5 per cent favoured other means. When the Monitor asked for respondents’ expectations of the NHS, 54 per cent thought that it would get worse and only 22 per cent thought that it would get better.

Fears about the future of the NHS are widely held. For example, the NHS Federation in 2018 argued that the annual NHS winter crisis now lasts all year. It maintained that:

Without new ways of delivering services and sustained investment, NHS and care services will simply not be able to cope. We are not currently doing enough for the old, the sick and the vulnerable, and as things stand it will get worse.

The NatCen Social Research’s British Social Attitudes (BSA) survey (2018) asked the public about their views on the NHS and health issues generally. It found that satisfaction with the NHS overall was 53 per cent (a 3 per cent drop from 2017). This was the lowest level since 2007, but still better than the low of 34 per cent in 1997. In particular, satisfaction with GPs was at the lowest ever of 63 per cent. The main reasons for satisfaction with the NHS were quality of care; it was free at the point of use; the attitudes and behaviour of NHS staff; and the range of services and treatments available. The main reasons for dissatisfaction were staff shortages, long waiting times, lack of funding and government reforms.

Surveys show that the NHS remains a respected national institution with public support for its founding principles and the prioritization of the health service for extra government funding above other public services such as education and welfare. Yet the public’s satisfaction with the NHS fluctuates in terms of waiting times, patient experience, annual fears of imminent collapse, funding and political attitudes to the NHS.

The reasons for a decline in satisfaction include alleged deterioration of services, negative media reporting, public expectations of the NHS, and opposition to government and its NHS policies. Debate and concern continue and it may be that the public are supporting an iconic state institution under threat from perceived privatization, irrespective of its actual performance and satisfaction levels. The political parties admitted in the 2015 General Election campaign that the NHS would run an annual deficit in the future, which must be covered. Yet there is no long-term programme for meeting the deficit and no alternative plan for how Britain can best generate the wealth to fund its public services, apart from tax revenues.

In spite of such concerns, the 2010 BSA survey found that 81 per cent of respondents thought that there should be more/or much more spending on health. But only 39 per cent said that government should increase personal taxes in order to improve health and social services and 50 per cent thought that taxes and spending should remain at the same earlier levels.

The problem of financing state services remains, and in Britain is linked to the budget deficit following the credit crisis in 2007. Poll respondents differ on how to reduce the budget deficit, short of increased taxation. A majority

think that public sector spending in fact needs to be cut. Others feel that making the public services more efficient could save money to pay off the deficit without damaging the services or raising income taxes. Some believe that reducing NHS waste and management would give considerable savings in the NHS budget. Others argue that the coalition government’s reforms of the NHS to give doctors and nurses control over their own budgets and work procedures would improve the costs and organization of the NHS, reduce bureaucracy and increase local control. Yet it appears that the various attempts at NHS structural reforms had not been successful.

It is doubted whether efficiency savings would in fact be sufficient to make deep inroads into budget deficits. Public social services are at present demand led and must be serviced largely by taxation revenues despite the fact that costs rise annually. Most of the suggested solutions to improve quality and productivity such as better pay and conditions for public sector employees; more such workers; and more investment on new buildings and equipment have to be largely paid for from tax revenues. Both working and middle classes have been squeezed by higher taxes, and the higher paid have become the preferred target group.

Respondents to polls often do not believe that there had been a great improvement in social services generally despite increased government spending and cuts. They do not consider that state institutions are well run and there is growing support for more comprehensive, better-funded and more efficient, less wasteful state services. Yet despite considerable recent criticism of the NHS and social services, the personal experiences of many individuals in their dealings with the system are usually very positive. Doctors and nurses frequently head the lists of professionals with whom Britons are most satisfied, despite recent scandals about failing hospitals, medical negligence, delayed appointments, patient abuse, lack of caring treatment and overworked GPs.

It is argued that alternatives might consist of better, more efficient funding of the NHS such as upfront charges for itemized services subject to annual maxima (as in other countries) and supplementary insurance like the apparently successful social insurance schemes in France and Australia, in addition to proportional taxation and National Insurance contributions. However, the importation of private sector models into the NHS, such as PFI and private sector re-styling of hospitals and doctors’ surgeries, have not proved successful or popular with the public. Nevertheless, there is already a degree of privatization in the public health services and it is possible that hospitals and doctors’ surgeries could become more competent and competitive market structures free from excessive government and management interference.

The problems of NHS funding are conditioned by history and cultural traditions, reliance on state funding as a reaction to past suffering, and an assumption that the present systems should be able, without drastic alteration, to solve the problems of an ageing population and increasing amounts of chronic long-term

illness. So much of government policy and legislation therefore tends to be working at the edges of monolithic structures without fundamental and effective reform. Opinion polls show that a majority of the British people support the idea of a welfare state and public social services that are funded by taxation. Only a minority seem to believe that privatization policies would improve the effectiveness of public services. All British political parties have, to varying degrees, committed themselves to support a public NHS and front-line social welfare services.

Write short essays on the following topics:

1.  Describe the structure and condition of the National Health Service.

1.  Does the social security system provide a comprehensive service for the needy in Britain?

1.  Discuss the different types of housing in Britain and the method of buying property. What are some of the problems that affect property buying today?

Briefly explain and examine the following terms, commenting on their significance in British life:
