# 10

## Education

- School history
- The 1944 Education Act
- The contemporary state school sector
- The contemporary independent (fee-paying) school sector
- School organization and examinations
- Higher education
- Further, adult and lifelong education
- Attitudes to education
- _Exercises_
- _Further reading_
- _Websites_

British education today operates on three main levels: schools, further/adult education and higher education. Schools are divided into state (maintained from public funds) and independent (privately financed) sectors (the latter mainly in England). But there is no common educational organization for the UK, and England, Wales, Northern Ireland and Scotland have somewhat different school systems. This chapter concentrates on the largest school unit, that of England, with comparative references to Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland. Further/adult and higher education have similar structures throughout Britain and are mostly state-funded. The four individual countries of the United Kingdom have significant degrees of either national or devolved self-government in educational matters.

The quality of contemporary British state school education is of concern to many parents and teachers and attracts criticism because of teacher and funding shortages. An _Economist/_Ipsos MORI poll in 2017 placed education/schools in fourth place at 21 per cent in a list of the most important issues/problems facing Britain. In recent years, international comparisons by organizations such as the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development’s (OECD) Pisa tests have suggested that Britain does not rank highly in global terms for the quality of its school system (defined by good passes in national examinations in reading, maths and science by 15-year-olds). Results for 2016, for example, showed that Britain had dropped to 27th in maths and 22nd in reading, but climbed to 15th place in science. England had the strongest results in the UK, but performance had not improved globally. The highest international ratings were consistently achieved by Asian educational systems and by some Scandinavian countries. However, a Pearson/_Economist_ international league table in 2014 based on wider school and university results showed that the UK had achieved a good performance in sixth and second places in Europe behind Finland. But statistics vary over time, cover different areas, and raise questions about how educational systems are, or should be, measured.

British pre-school and primary education sometimes give poor results, allegedly because of low-quality nursery schools, unqualified and underpaid staff, and poor working conditions at some primary schools. Figures from Ofsted (the Office for Standards in Education) frequently report considerable numbers of children leaving state primary schools without basic skills in reading, writing and arithmetic. Critics argue that this results in some functionally illiterate pupils passing on to the secondary level of education, where they may have learning and associated behavioural problems. It is also estimated that 7 million adults (one in five) in Britain are illiterate. Low standards of literacy and

numeracy arguably stem in large part from decades of inadequate state school education and current difficulties. Ofsted inspectors have particularly criticized school standards in English, mathematics, technology and writing/reading skills. However, the _Times Higher Education_ World University Rankings 2018 listed British universities as leading the top 1,000 universities in the world, with the University of Oxford in first place, the University of Cambridge (2), Imperial College London (8), University College London (16) and the London School of Economics and Political Science (25). But the ranking suggested that British universities had experienced a year of ‘stagnation and modest decline’ in which some of them had lost ground and others had slipped from the top 200. Britain had also fallen from the second most represented nation after the USA in the rankings and there are concerns that a bad Brexit result would further

hurt the UK’s reputation and research capacity.

In 2001, Britain had the highest proportion (35.6 per cent) of university graduates aged 21, largely because of short (three-year) degree courses in most institutions. It is argued that courses should be cut to two years in order to increase quality and productivity and reduce costs. But, today, there is also criticism of degree standards, teaching performance, inadequate content of courses, varying outcome levels, and an allegedly declining quality of students entering university. However, British education does have positive elements. Governments have prioritized education, improved its overall quality and made it a life-long learning experience. Some primary school literacy has improved and reforms are being encouraged in secondary schools. National school examinations have improved in recent years and tests have been revised to avoid grade inflation. Significant numbers of schools and students in the state and independent sectors and universities produce excellent work. It is the failing and underperforming state and independent schools, and some universities which attract the

media headlines.

Nevertheless, education still appears in some critical opinion polls as a concern because of declining quality, inadequate structures, political and bureaucratic interference in schools and universities, a targets and test culture, a league table mentality, and confusing attempts to reform educational systems.

##### School history

The complicated nature of British schools and current educational arguments have their roots in school history. State involvement in education came late and the first attempt to establish a unified system of state-funded elementary schools was made in 1870 for England and Wales (1872 for Scotland and 1923 for Northern Ireland). It was not until 1944 that the state provided a comprehensive and national apparatus for both primary and secondary state schools, which was free and compulsory.

However, some church schools have long existed. After England, Scotland, Ireland and Wales were gradually converted to Christianity by the ffth and sixth centuries, the church’s position in society enabled it to create the first schools. These initially prepared boys for the priesthood, but the church then developed a wider educational role and its structures influenced the later state system.

Other schools were also periodically established by rich individuals or monarchs. These were independent, privately financed institutions and were variously known as high, grammar and public schools. They were later associated with both the modern independent and state educational sectors. But originally such schools were largely confined to the sons of the rich, aristocratic and influential. Most people received no formal schooling and remained illiterate and innumerate for life.

In later centuries, more children benefited as the church created new schools; local areas developed secular schools; charity schools were provided by wealthy industrialists and philanthropists for working-class boys and girls; and some other poor children attended a variety of schools organized by voluntary societies, women (dames), workhouses and the Ragged School Union. But the children attending such institutions were a minority and received only a very basic instruction in reading, writing and arithmetic. The majority of children received no adequate education.

By the nineteenth century, Britain (except for Scotland with its quality education) had a haphazard school structure. Protestant churches had lost their monopoly of education and competed with the Roman Catholic Church and other faiths. Church schools guarded their independence from state and secular interference and provided much of the available schooling. The ancient high, grammar and public schools continued to train the sons of the middle and upper classes for professional and leadership roles in society. Yet, at a time when the industrial revolutions were proceeding rapidly and the population was growing strongly, the state did not provide a school system which could educate the workforce. Most of the working class received no formal or sufficient education.

However, local and central government did begin to show some regard for education in the early nineteenth century. Grants were made to local authorities for school use in their areas and in 1833 Parliament funded the construction of school buildings. But it was only in 1870 that the state became more actively involved. An Education Act (the Forster Act) created local school boards in England and Wales, which financed and built elementary schools in their areas. Such state schools supplied non-denominational training and the existing religious voluntary (or Church) schools served denominational needs.

By 1880 the state system was providing free and compulsory elementary schooling in most parts of Britain for children between the ages of 5 and 10 (12 in 1899). The Balfour Act (1902) abolished the school boards, made local

government responsible for state education, established some new secondary and technical schools and funded voluntary schools. But, although state schools provided education for children up to age 14 by 1918, this was still limited to basic skills.

Adequate secondary school education remained largely the province of the independent sector and a few state schools. But generally, parents had to pay for these services. After a period when the old public (private) schools had declined in quality, they revived in the nineteenth century. Their weaknesses, such as the narrow curriculum and indiscipline, had been reformed by progressive headmasters such as Thomas Arnold of Rugby and their reputations improved. The private grammar and high schools, which imitated the classics-based education of the public schools, also expanded. These schools drew their pupils from the sons of the middle and upper classes and were the training grounds for the established elite and the professions.

State secondary school education in the early twentieth century was marginally extended to children whose parents could not afford school fees. Scholarships (financial grants) for clever poor children became available, some state funding was provided and more schools were created. But this state help did not appreciably expand secondary education, often because many working-class children were required to work because of family poverty rather than go to school. By 1920, only 9.2 per cent of 14-year-old children in England and Wales were able to enter secondary schools on a non-fee-paying basis. The school system in the early twentieth century was still inadequate for the demands of society; working and lower middle-class children lacked extensive education; and hard-pressed governments avoided any further large-scale involvement until 1944.

##### The 1944 Education Act

In 1944, an Education Act (the Butler Act) reorganized state primary and secondary schools in England and Wales (1947 in Scotland and Northern Ireland) and greatly influenced future generations of schoolchildren. State schooling became free and compulsory up to the age of 15 and was divided into three stages: primary schools (5–11 years), secondary schools (11–15) and post-15 education. A Ministry of Education drew up policy guidelines and local education authorities (LEAs) decided which forms of schooling would be used in their areas.

Two types of state school resulted from the Act: county and voluntary. County schools were created by LEAs in each county and divided into primary and secondary schools. Voluntary schools were mainly those elementary schools which had been founded by religious and other groups and which were now partially financed or maintained by LEAs, although many retained a particular

religious affiliation. Non-denominational schools thus coexisted with voluntary schools.

Following the 1944 Act, most state secondary schools in England, Wales and Northern Ireland were effectively divided into grammar schools, secondary modern schools and technical schools. Some grammar schools were new and state-funded, while others were old foundations, which received direct state funding and were known as grant-maintained schools. The placement of pupils/ students in this secondary system depended upon an examination result. The eleven-plus examination was adopted by most LEAs; consisted of intelligence tests which covered linguistic, mathematical and general knowledge; and was taken in the last year of primary school at the age of 11. The object was to differentiate between academic and non-academic children, and it introduced the notion of ‘selection’ based on ability. Those who passed the eleven-plus went to the grammar school, while those who failed went to the secondary modern and technical schools.

Although all schools were supposed to be equal in their educational aims, the grammar schools were equated with a better (more academic) education and a socially respectable role, and qualified children (through national examinations) for good jobs and entry into higher education and the professions. Secondary modern schools emphasized basic schooling, initially without national examinations. The third type of school (technical school) educated more vocationally inclined pupils and apprentices.

The intention of the 1944 Act was to provide universal and free state primary and secondary education. Day-release training at local colleges was also introduced for employed people who wanted further education after 15, and local authority grants were given to students who wished to enter what was then essentially free higher education for many students. It was hoped that such equality of opportunity would expand the educational market, lead to a better-educated society, encourage more working-class children to enter university and achieve greater social mobility.

However, in the 1950s it was felt that these aims were not being achieved under the selective secondary school system. Education became a party political battlefield. The Labour Party and other critics maintained that the eleven-plus examination was wrong in principle, was socially divisive, had educational and testing weaknesses, resulted in middle-class children predominating in grammar schools and higher education, and thus perpetuated the class system.

Labour governments from 1964 were committed to abolishing the eleven-plus, selection and the secondary school divisions. These would be replaced by non-selective ‘comprehensive schools’ to which all children were automatically transferred after primary school. They provided schooling for children of all ability levels and from all social backgrounds in a local area on one school campus.

The battle for the comprehensive and selective systems was fierce and still continues. Although more schools became comprehensive under the Conservative government from 1970, it decided against legislative compulsion. Instead, LEAs were able to choose the secondary education which was best suited to local needs. Some decided for comprehensives, others retained selection and grammar schools, while most chose a mixture of the two systems.

But the Labour government in 1976 intended to establish comprehensive schools nationwide. Before this policy could be implemented, the Conservatives came to power in 1979. Thus, the state secondary school sector today still remains divided between the selective and non-selective options since a minority of LEAs in England do not have comprehensives and there are some 164 grammar schools left. Grammar schools are allowed to expand on one site, but there are increasing demands from parents and politicians that new grammar schools should be created to widen school choice in more parts of the country. Schools in Scotland and Wales are comprehensive, although there are some independent establishments. Northern Irish schools are at present divided into grammars and secondary moderns, but the eleven-plus and selective tests for the grammar schools are scheduled for abolition.

The comprehensive/selection debate continues in different forms. School education is still subject to party political and ideological conflict and in the last few years has become a very diverse marketplace. Opinion polls suggest that only a minority of parents support the original comprehensive model while a majority favour a diverse system of schools (including grammars), with entry for their children being based on choice, assessment or examination selection. However, government reforms to the state school system are still being made with the introduction of academies and free schools (see following section). It is often argued that the long-running debates about the relative merits of different types of schooling in Britain have not benefited schoolchildren or the educational system. There is also dissatisfaction with school performances and standards, which tend to be linked to arguments about the various educational models, and a concern that lack of quality and discipline leads to further social problems and inequality.

##### The contemporary state school sector

Children in _England_ between the ages of 3 and 5 are entitled to a number of hours per year of optional, state-funded, pre-school education, such as ‘playgroups’, nurseries, community child-care centres or nursery classes in schools. Although ‘home training’ by parents is legal, state education is official ly free and compulsory for children between the ages of 5 and 18. Pupils remain in education or training until their 18th birthday, when they reach the educational ‘leaving age’.

The education system is divided into stages based upon age. Early Years Foundation Stage (ages 3–5); primary education (ages 5–11), subdivided into Key Stage 1 (KS1), Infants (ages 5–7) and Key Stage 2 (KS2) Juniors (ages 7–11); secondary education (ages 11–16), subdivided into Key Stage 3 (KS3; ages 11–14) and Key Stage 4 (KS4; ages 14–16); Key Stage 5 is post-16 education (ages 16–18); and tertiary education (for ages 18+).

At age 16 the students mainly take exams for the General Certificate of Secondary Education (GCSE) or similar level qualifications. While education is compulsory until 18, schooling is only obligatory until 16, and thus post-16 education can take a number of forms, and may be academic or vocational. This can involve continued schooling, known as sixth form or college, leading (after two years of study) to A-level qualifications, or a number of alternative level qualifications such as Business and Technology Education Council (BTEC), the International Baccalaureate (IB), Cambridge Pre-U, WJEC or Eduqas. It can also include work-based apprenticeships, traineeships, or volunteering.

Sixteen-to-eighteen student options are therefore full-time education at a school or college, an apprenticeship, traineeship, part-time education or training, as well as being employed, self-employed or volunteering for 20 hours or more a week. Such students can apply for 16–19-year-old funding schemes to help with education costs. Schools are mainly mixed-sex, although some are

PLATE 10.1 Pupils in a primary school class, Tooting, London. Ethnic minority children can now form a majority in state schools in areas of minority group concentration. © Ilpo Musto/ Shutterstock

single-sex. Some 93–94 per cent of British children receive free education in state primary and secondary schools, but the state system has remnants of the 1944 Act and a diversity of school types throughout the country.

In England, the Department for Education initiates overall policy (with Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland having devolved responsibility for their educational systems). State schools consist mainly of funded, non-denominational schools and voluntary (faith) schools which are usually state-aided and self-run, with some being connected to religious groups. LEAs in England organize some state schools in their areas and may be formally responsible for employing staff and school admission procedures. But LEA control has declined considerably as new types of schools have been established.

###### Schools

State schooling before the age of five is not compulsory in England and there is no statutory requirement on LEAs to provide education for under-5 children. But more parents in England (particularly those at work) are seeking school provisions for younger children and there is concern about the lack of opportunities and the expense of child care beyond the basic amount provided by government. Pupils attend state primary school (divided usually into infants and junior levels) from the age of 5. Most then move to state secondary school level normally at 11 until the age of 16 or 18.

Over 87 per cent of secondary pupils in England attend comprehensive schools or new alternatives such as ‘free schools’ and academies. There are only a small number of grammar (164) and secondary modern schools left in the state system from 1944. The continued existence of these latter schools depends partly upon local government decisions, partly upon parent power and partly upon central government policy. Although the Labour Party is ideologically against increasing the number of selective grammar schools, many critics and parents argue for their retention and expansion on new sites. Many such schools achieve high national examination results and provide a disciplined academic background.

Comprehensive school pupils are often of mixed abilities and come from a variety of social backgrounds in the local area. There is still much argument about the quality and performance of this system. Some critics argue that disadvantaged students from poor homes receive a poor education. Others maintain that bright academic children suffer, although ‘setting’ (formerly called ‘streaming’) divides pupils into different ability and interest classes, and examination results can be excellent. Arguably, therefore, forms of ‘selection’ continue within comprehensives. There are some very good comprehensive schools, which are not confined to privileged and affluent areas. But there are also some weak and failing ones, which suffer from a variety of social, economic and educational problems and are sometimes (if not always) associated with deprived inner cities.

FIGURE 10.1 The state school sector in England and Wales

In an attempt to encourage diversity in the state comprehensive system, Conservative governments established secondary level, state-funded, privately sponsored City Technology Colleges specializing in science, technology and mathematics. The Labour government from 1999 also promoted school diversity. It created a system of publicly funded, part privately sponsored, self-governing academies independent of LEA control that replaced failing and underperforming schools and were intended to revitalize deprived areas. Specialist Schools were formed which concentrated on the sciences, modern foreign languages, sports or the arts and allowed best performing schools to mentor local struggling schools. University technical colleges were also introduced as part of the academies programme. They offer technically oriented courses of study, which are sponsored and supported academically by a university, are funded by the state, non-selective, free, and not controlled by a local authority. It seems that currently these colleges are struggling for students and support. Labour also tried to increase the number of voluntary schools organized by faiths (for example, Church of England, Roman Catholic, Methodist, Jewish, Muslim and Sikh). The coalition government (2010–15) expanded these models. It allowed all existing secondary and primary state schools to apply for academy status with state budgets and to opt out of LEA control. It also enabled parents, charities and other bodies to apply to set up ‘free schools’ independent of LEAs with state funding for each pupil they attract. While some of these schools have experienced difficulties during the set-up stage, they were the top performing type of school at GCSE examination level in 2017. However, critics argue that such reforms create a two-tier secondary school system, in which state academies and free schools become self-governing independent trusts, while comprehensive schools remain largely state-funded and LEA-controlled. In 2018, two-thirds of state secondary schools were academies and more than 680 free

schools had opened or been approved.

_Scotland_ has its own ancient educational system, with schools, colleges and universities which are among the oldest in Europe. Its state school system is now comprehensive and non-selective. Children transfer from primary to secondary education at 12 and continue to 18. Scottish ‘public schools’ are state and not private institutions (although some eminent independent schools do exist).

PLATE 10.2 Nottingham Bluecoat Academy illustrates the historic stages that some English schools have passed through. Created in 1706 as a charity school with a Church of England identification in central Nottingham, it moved premises before becoming a Technology College and in 2012 a voluntary aided Academy funded directly by the government. This building was established in 2006 and houses specialist teaching. © Martine Hamilton Knight/ Builtvision

In _Northern Ireland_ the state schools are mostly divided on religious grounds into Catholic and Protestant and are often single-sex, although there are some tentative movements towards integrated co-educational schools. The comprehensive principle had not been widely adopted and a selective system with entry tests at 11 gives entrance to grammar schools, which 40 per cent of the age group attend. The future of entry tests and selective schools is still debated. Student performances at selective schools are generally superior to those in England, although examination results in the comprehensive schools are comparatively poor.

_Wales_ tends to follow the state school and examination system in England, although there are relatively few independent and single-sex schools.

The academic organization of most English schools has been traditionally largely left to head teachers and staff. Following Conservative and Labour reforms, head teachers in some schools now have greater financial responsibility for school budgets, management, academic organization, appointment of teachers and student admissions. Trust bodies of schools, such as academies, and school governors (drawn from among local citizens and parents) have greater

powers of decision-making and parents are supposed to have a voice in running schools. These changes have meant a shift from educational to management roles within state schools and involve increased burdens of time and administration. Parents also have a legal right to choose a particular school for their children (not always successfully achieved).

##### The contemporary independent (fee-paying) school sector

The are some 2,500 independent schools in the UK, which cater for about 615,000 children. This is 6.5 per cent of all UK children, but 7 per cent of those in England. The independent sector operates mainly in England, is separate from the state school structure and caters for children from the ages of 4 to 18 at different levels. Some 1,289 schools today are members of the Independent Schools Council, which accounts for 80 per cent of the pupils in the UK independent education sector.

The sector is defined by payment for school education. Its financing derives from investments and the fees paid by the pupils’ parents for their education, which vary considerably between schools and can amount to many thousands of pounds a year. The independent sector is dependent upon its charitable and tax-exempt status to survive. This means that the schools are not taxed on their income if it is used only for educational purposes. A minority of children are scholarship holders, whose expenses are covered by their schools. Given the increasing fees, some parents are apparently moving their children to free schools where they may potentially have good-quality schools without paying fees. Yet there have recently been increased numbers of pupils in independent schools caused by overseas applications and British parents moving teenagers from state schools.

So-called public schools (private, not state), such as Eton, Harrow, The King’s School, Canterbury and Winchester, are the more famous of the

FIGURE 10.2 The independent school sector

independent schools and are among the 264 leading schools that belong to the Headmasters’ and Headmistresses’ Conference. Loretto, Fettes College and George Watson’s College are among well-known private schools in Scotland, which in total have about 31,000 pupils. Many independent schools were originally created (often by monarchs) to provide education for the sons of the rich and aristocratic although some were under public management and offered free schooling to the public. Today, such schools are often boarding establishments, where the pupils live and are educated during term time, although many now take day pupils (who do not board).

Independent schools play a significant role in English education and many leading figures have been educated at them. Entry today is competitive, often by an entrance examination, and is not solely restricted to social class, connections or wealth, although the ability to pay a child’s fees is obviously important for most parents. Independent preparatory schools (at the primary/junior level) prepare their pupils for secondary school entrance, and parents who decide to send their children to an independent school will often give them a ‘prep school’ education first.

Independent schools can vary considerably in quality and reputation. The sector has grown and has an attraction despite its small size and increasing school fees. Insurance schemes for the payment of fees give opportunities for

PLATE 10.3 Pupils at Eton College, a prestigious independent (public) school near Windsor, England. © David Hartley/Shutterstock

independent education to the less affluent. But some parents make great financial sacrifices so that their children can be independently educated. Opinion polls often suggest that many parents would send their children to an independent school if they could afford it because of the quality of many of the schools and because such schooling may give social advantages in later life. However, some schools have had to close in recent years because of poor recruitment, bad finances, recessionary factors, competition, teaching quality and legal demands from government.

The independent sector is criticized for being elitist, socially divisive and based on the ability to pay for education. In this view it perpetuates the class system. The Labour Party has long argued for the abolition of independent schools and the removal of their tax and charitable status. Recent governments’ evaluation of the schools concluded that the sector should continue but that its charitable position could be more rigorously defined to include aid to neighbouring state schools, such as the sharing of teaching and sporting facilities. Short of outright government abolition, the independent schools seem firmly established, despite the economic and political pressures upon them, and provide choice in what would otherwise be largely a state monopoly on school education.

##### School organization and examinations

The school day in English state and independent schools runs at variable times from about 9.00 a.m. until 4 p.m., with possible voluntary or extra-curricular activities after school hours. The school year is divided into three terms (autumn, spring and summer), although there have been proposals to reorganize this system by adding another term and cutting holiday times. Classes in English schools used to be called ‘forms’ and in secondary schools were numbered from one to six, but most schools have now adopted year numbers from one (e.g. Year 2), which include a two-year ‘sixth form’ for advanced examination work. An increase in the birth rate from 2010 led to larger numbers of schoolchildren, particularly in ethnic minority communities, and a need for more school buildings and facilities in difficult economic times. OECD reported in 2014 that the average UK class size for state primary schools was 26 pupils, the largest in Europe and secondary schools had average classes of 21.3 pupils. Independent

schools have smaller class sizes and a better pupil–teacher ratio.

Most school teachers are trained at universities and other colleges to degree level. There has long been a shortage of teachers in some subjects, especially in mathematics, technology, physics and foreign languages, and there is an increasing use of teaching assistants to take some of the burden from specialist classroom teachers. Some practising teachers increasingly see the profession as unattractive and leave for better-paid jobs or retire early. Teachers (and their

PLATE 10.4 Pupils and teacher at a secondary (comprehensive) school practise a chemistry experiment. There is a shortage of girls studying science subjects in schools and at university and there are attempts to attract more of them to the field. © Simon Townsley/Shutterstock

trade unions) at present are suffering from low morale and recruitment difficulties after battles with the government over pay, conditions, government targets, cuts and educational reforms, and from what they think is the low status afforded them by government and the general public. The teaching profession has become very stressful and subject to greater pressures, such as physical and abusive assaults upon teachers by pupils, increased bureaucracy, lack of resources, pupil indiscipline and an alleged lack of support for teachers. The quality of teaching in state schools has attracted criticism and governments say that they are committed to raising standards, removing underperforming teachers and closing ‘failing schools’. However, some successful recruitment campaigns are now attracting high-quality and committed young graduates into teaching.

The effect of government spending or austerity cuts on education has been considerable. These have prevented new school building and the repair and modernization of existing schools; resulted in reduced services and a shortage of books and learning equipment for pupils and teachers; and libraries have also closed in many local areas. However, governments have tried to protect schools in real terms and the budget for 2015–16 was 753 billion. Money is being spent for example on the creation of more free schools in England. The aim is to produce a fairer funding formula for schools to

remove inequalities between different areas. Nevertheless, LEAs and schools argue that there have still been cuts in their budgets earmarked for school improvements and educational funds have been cut because of recession and austerity measures.

The educational testing of children in state schools has been vigorously debated and tied to Key Stages in the National Curriculum in England. Governments have introduced tests such as SATs (standard assessment tests) to establish what children have learned between the ages of 5 and 11 and the progress of children is measured by national standards. School results are shown in ‘league tables’ which allow parents, schools and public bodies to assess school performances. The subjects under examination are often changed and reassessed, but the usual areas are maths, English and science, which may be marked by SATs testing or teacher assessment.

Such tests and assessments have been criticized and are controversial. Ofsted argues that an over-reliance on exam results encourages schools to take advantage of the system. Many critics are opposed to them on principle, object to the stress and extra work involved for teachers and children, have doubted the validity of the tests, and objected to the competitive league tables. Some teachers have boycotted the tests, and argue that they detract from time that should be spent in teaching. Governments and other critics believe that they are valuable in countering poor primary school performance and contribute to serious and structured learning.

There has also been considerable effort to rationalize the school system and revitalize school subjects by the creation of a National Curriculum in England in 1988 (with similar moves in Northern Ireland and Wales). A state curriculum for primary and secondary schools was established, which was standardized, centrally devised and appropriate to the needs of contemporary life. It is not applicable to Scotland, independent schools and academies, although the latter must follow some of the subject areas such as English, maths, science and religious education.

The curriculum covers what subjects are to be taught in schools and specifies the standards children should reach in each subject. It has been vigorously criticized and consequently changed over the years as politicians and educationalists debate what should be included and represented. It is structured into Key Stages 1–4 covering ages 3 to 16. The age group between 16 and 18 includes students taking higher academic examinations and categories in further education and training.

The compulsory subjects studied in the National Curriculum are English, maths, science, history, geography, modern foreign languages, design and technology, art and design, music, physical education, citizenship, and information and communication technology (ICT). Schools may develop their own ICT study programmes and must provide religious education (RE) and sex education, although parents may ask that their children be excused from the

last two. These compulsory subjects are adapted in different programmes of study throughout the two years of the course and schools have some choice in selection.

The National Curriculum is tied to a system of national examinations at the secondary level in England and may also be taken in Wales and Northern Ireland. The structures and content of courses and examinations have recently been reformed and the first teaching started in 2015. The two main national examinations are the General Certificate of Secondary Education (GCSE); and the General Certificate of Education at Advanced Level (A-level). Results in all exams at all levels tend to be better in single-sex girls’ schools.

During Key Stage 4 (aged 14–16) most students work towards national qualifications, usually GCSEs taken at age 16. Students choose from the core compulsory subjects of English, maths and science and foundation subjects such as information and communication technology (ICT), physical education and citizenship. Schools must offer at least one subject from the arts, design and technology, humanities and foreign languages. They must also provide Religious Education (RE) and sex education. Pupils do not have to take exams in RE, but schools must provide one course where pupils can obtain a recognized RE qualification. Critics currently argue that the present RE syllabus should be replaced by a new subject (Religion and Worldviews) to include Humanism and Atheism.

The choice of some subjects, the alleged downgrading of modern foreign languages, debates about the content of history courses, and the place of religious and sex education in the curriculum have generated controversy, difficulties of implementation and problems about the content and scope of some GCSE courses.

The questions and marking of GCSE examinations are undertaken by independent examination boards, after a written examination in the second year. It can be taken in any subject(s) according to individual choice. But many candidates will attempt six or seven subjects and the basic subjects required for jobs and further education are English, maths and science. The GCSE was originally intended to give employers some idea of a candidate’s ability. But, although standards continue to improve, a third of students do not achieve high passes and 8 per cent do not pass a single subject. The minimum standard aimed at is five GCSEs on a new grading system from 9 to 1, with 9 being the top grade.

The new A-level two-year course, whose content has been revised and updated, was taught from 2015 and has a final written examination at the end of the second year. It is associated with more academic children, who are aiming at entry to higher education or the professions and who spend two years on their studies in a school sixth form or at a sixth-form college. Good passes are now essential because the competition for both popular and demanding courses at the universities and other colleges has become stiffer. Pupils may mix arts and science.

The standards achieved and examination results continue to rise, although grades have recently declined after criticism of grade inflation. There is continuing discussion about the format and content of A-levels and the system has been criticized for over-examining students, leaving less time for other school activities and leading to teacher overwork.

Alternative examinations are vocational GCSEs which are taken by young people between the ages of 16 and 19 and provide a broad-based preparation for a range of occupations and higher education; and National Vocational Qualifications (NVQs and SVQs), Higher National Diplomas (HNDs), and a number of other technical and vocational courses which are job-specific examinations. A new English Baccalaureate (EBac) is an alternative to GCSEs, in which students take examinations in English, maths, two sciences, a language and history or geography.

Scotland does not have a statutory national curriculum and pupils take the National Qualification (NQ) at the age of 16. Those between 16 and 18 take the reformed Scottish Highers (Higher and Advanced Higher).

##### Higher education

Should pupils obtain the required examination results at Aor equivalent levels, and be successful at any required interviews, they may gain a place at an institution of higher education (HE), such as a university or college. The student, after a prescribed period of study and after passing examinations, will receive a degree and become a graduate of that institution. In the past, only a small proportion of the age group in Britain proceeded to what was an elite higher education, in contrast to the higher rates in other major nations such as the USA.

The Higher Education Statistics Agency (HESA) reported in 2017–18 that student enrolment at British HE institutions was 2.34 million, an increase from 2016–17, with 81 per cent of all students coming from the UK, 6 per cent from EU countries, and 13 per cent from countries outside the EU. However, the situation for UK students coincided with the effect of increased tuition fees; uncertainty over whether students would choose employment possibilities over HE; British students studying in Europe, the USA and other English-speaking countries; and part-time and mature students struggling financially. Although numbers increased in 2017–18 there was again a decrease in British applicants in 2019.

Among undergraduate students from outside the UK, the highest proportion in 2017–18 came from Asia (201,580). Others came from Africa, the Middle East and North America. Over half of all postgraduate students were from outside the UK, and 42 per cent of full-time postgraduates came from outside the EU. But EU student numbers in future may be negatively affected by Brexit.

###### The universities

There were 23 British universities in 1960. After a period of expansion in the 1960s and reforms in 1992 when existing institutions such as polytechnics were given university status, there are now some 123 universities (including the publicly funded Open University, the independent, privately funded University of Buckingham, 1973, and the privately funded New College of the Humanities, 2012) out of a total of 161 institutions of higher education.

The universities can be broadly classified into four types. The ancient universities of Oxford and Cambridge, composed of their many colleges, date from the thirteenth century, and until the nineteenth century they were virtually the only English universities and offered no places to women. However, other old universities were founded in Scotland, such as St Andrews (1411), Glasgow (1450), Aberdeen (1494) and Edinburgh (1583). A second group comprises the ‘redbrick’ or civic universities such as Leeds, Liverpool and Manchester, which were created between 1850 and 1930. The third group consists of universities founded after the Second World War and in the 1960s. Many of the latter, for example Sussex, York and East Anglia, were built in semi-rural areas. The fourth group are the ‘new universities’ created in 1992 when polytechnics and some other colleges were granted university status. Since 1992, other universities have been created, either based on existing colleges or as new institutions.

PLATE 10.5 Balliol College, one of the oldest (1263) and largest colleges of the University of Oxford. It admits undergraduate and postgraduate students and has a high academic reputation. © Andrew Drysdale/Shutterstock

PLATE 10.6 Leeds University in Yorkshire is one of the redbrick or civic universities established in the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries. Founded in 1904, its origins go back to the nineteenth century and it is now a major teaching and research institution. The Parkinson Building, shown, contains the Brotherton Library, one of the largest university libraries in the UK. © Mark Campbell/Shutterstock

Competition to enter universities has recently been strong in both popular and prestigious subjects, and students who do not do well at A-level or equivalent may be unable to find a place. Even students with very high grades may not succeed in gaining their preferred place. An average of some 17 per cent of students drop out of higher education because of work, financial or other problems, and the percentage is much higher in some newer institutions. However, the majority aim for a solid degree in order to obtain a good job, or to continue in higher education by doing further study or research (Master’s degrees and doctorates). The bachelor’s degree (Bachelor of Arts, BA, or Bachelor of Science, BSc) is usually taken in final examinations at the end of the third year of study, although some degree courses do vary in length in different parts of Britain (such as Scotland with a four-year MA Honours degree). The degree is divided into first-, second and third-class honours. Some degrees are awarded entirely on the basis of examination results, while others may include some continuous assessment over the period of study. Grade inflation (increased number of high passes), particularly for ‘firsts’, has encouraged calls to dispense with these classifications and to move possibly to percentage results.

Universities are supposed to have uniform standards, although there are centres of excellence in certain fields and there has been recent criticism about low performance levels in some universities and particular subjects. Students can choose from a large number of subject areas and teaching is mainly by the lecture system, supported by tutorials (small groups), seminars and web based courses. The student–lecturer ratio at British universities has increased to a high 25:1, which has resulted in student protest about the quality of teaching and courses because of higher student numbers and the cutting of government money for teaching jobs and staff positions. Students live either on campus in university accommodation or in rented property outside the university. Until recently few British students chose universities near their parents’ homes and many seemed to prefer those in the south of England, but financial costs now persuade some students to live at home or locally.

The 12 most subscribed-to first degree courses in 2017–18 according to HESA were, in decreasing order of popularity, business and administrative studies, subjects allied to medicine, biological sciences, social studies, creative arts and design, engineering and technology, education, computer science, languages and law.

Universities are independent institutions created by Royal Charter, enjoy academic freedom, appoint their staff, award their own degrees and decide which students to admit. But they are in practice dependent upon income from student fees and government money. The latter derives from finance (according to the number of students recruited and research performance) given by government to Universities Funding Councils for distribution to the universities through university vice-chancellors, who are the chief executive Officers of the universities.

Both Conservative and Labour governments have been concerned to make the universities more accountable in the national interest; have controlled their budgets; and have encouraged them to seek alternative private sources of finance from business, entrepreneurial activity, fund raising and industry. The universities have lost staff and research money; have had to adopt more effective management and accounting procedures; must market their resources more efficiently; must attract and recruit students in order to obtain government finance and student fees; should pay greater attention to teaching and research performance, but have to cope with increased bureaucracy; and must justify their positions financially and educationally. Many might be prepared to become private institutions outside state control, but this would mean the loss of government finance in a competitive market in which they would have to depend on their own initiatives and financial resources.

Government consequently intervenes more closely in the running of higher education than in the past, for example by implementing the closure of uneconomic or failing departments. These policies have provoked opposition from the universities, which argue that the recent large expansion of student numbers

has not seen an equivalent rise in funding, salaries or new staff appointments. Yet they have to adapt rather than continue to lose staff, finance and degree programmes. It is also argued that expansion has led to universities taking poorly qualified students to fill quotas, who then drop out because of work and other pressures. Some educationalists feel that British universities will decline in quality if they are not better funded by the state. Others argue that capable universities should break away from the state, market their own resources and attract their own finance.

###### Other higher education colleges and professional training

The 1970s saw the creation of colleges (or institutes) of higher education, often by merging existing colleges with redundant teachers’ training colleges or by establishing new institutions. They offered degree, diploma, Certificate and professional courses in both science and the arts. They were originally controlled by their local authorities, but Conservative governments gradually granted them independence and some have since achieved university or alternative status.

The traditional professional training of teachers was undertaken by the universities or by specialist teacher training colleges. Today, graduates who already have a degree complete a postgraduate Certificate in education (PGCE) at a university or college and work with school partnerships on school experience placements as part of the training. A second pathway is for students to gain qualified teacher status (QTS) by school-led training based in schools where they learn ‘on the job’ and may later achieve other professional qualifications, such as the PGCE.

A variety of other institutions also provide higher education courses. Some, like the Royal College of Art, the Cranfield Institute of Technology and business schools, have university status, while others, such as agricultural, drama and art colleges such the Royal Academy of Dramatic Arts (RADA) and the Royal College of Music, provide comparable courses. All these institutions usually have a strong vocational aspect to their programmes and fill a specialized role in higher education.

###### Student finance

There are differences between student finance systems in England, Wales, Northern Ireland and Scotland. This section deals mainly with England.

After the 1944 Education Act, English students who gained a place at an institution of higher education were awarded a grant from their local education authorities (LEAs). The grant was in two parts: it paid the tuition fees of a first degree (directly to the university), and also paid after means-testing of parents’ income, maintenance and residence expenses of the student such as the costs

of travel away from home, rent, food and books for a course during term time. This grant system meant that higher education was free for many students, except those whose parents were expected to contribute means-tested amounts to their children.

In 1998, a Labour government replaced the student grant with tuition fees and maintenance loans during their study period. The cost of these schemes has increased significant ly over the intervening years.

The present situation for most students consists of two components. First, English and EU students studying in England have to pay annual tuition fees for their courses (79,250 in 2018). These students apply to Student Finance England for a loan to cover their tuition fees. This is paid directly to the institution and the student has to start paying it back after graduation when they reach a prescribed level of income. Second, full-time English students may apply for a maintenance loan from the Student Finance England, which is intended to cover living costs. The non-means-tested loan in 2018 depended on whether the student lived at home (up to an annual 77,324), away from home outside London (78,700) or away from home in London (711,354). The loan is paid into an individual’s bank account at the beginning of term. Part-time English students in financial or social difficulties may receive assistance from a maintenance grant, but this may reduce their maintenance loan. Further help may be available from bursaries and university/college aid.

Students start to pay back their tuition fees and loans after graduation when they are employed and reach a salary level of 725,000 (2017). Consequently, most students now have to finance their own higher education without free state grants, some have financial difficulties, and may have to take part-time employment. However, apart from a decrease in applications to university after 2010, particularly from part-time and mature students, these changes have not resulted in a great reduction in English students. It is estimated that 45 per cent of them will never pay back their loans in full, and any amount still owing 30 years after starting repayments will be cancelled.

Scottish students living in Scotland receive free tuition from the Scottish government. However, students from England, Wales and Northern Ireland studying in Scotland are charged tuition fees by Scottish universities, which are similar to those charged in their own countries. Currently, Welsh and Northern Irish tuition fees are somewhat lower than those charged by English universities to English students, who protest about this situation and the size of their tuition fees. However, students are now consumers of education and feel freer to complain about what they see as the inadequacies of courses, teachers and their teaching.

The Labour Party in February 2015 said that it would cut tuition fees by a third to 76,000 if it won the general election and considered that tuition fees ‘had been a disaster’. This plan was supported by the National Union of Students (NUS), but the universities said they would lose income for teaching

and other resources if it were adopted. In the event, the Conservatives won the 2015 and 2017 elections and in 2018 announced that they would cut fees to 76,000.

###### The Open University (OU)

The Labour Party broached the idea of the Open University in the 1960s. It would be a non-residential system of ‘distance learning’, which initially used BBC television, radio, specially produced books, audio/video cassettes and correspondence courses to bring degree-level courses to a range of students. It was intended to give opportunities (or a ‘second chance’) to adults who had been unable to attend traditional campus-based higher education. It was hoped that OU courses might appeal to working-class students who had left school at the official school-leaving age and wished to broaden their horizons, as well as those with health disabilities.

The Open University opened in 1969, and its first courses started in 1971. It now caters for undergraduate, postgraduate and research students in a wide range of subjects. About 7,000 students of all ages and from very different walks of life receive degrees from the Open University each year. First degrees (bachelor’s degrees) are awarded on a system of credits for each course completed and now include students from the European Union, Gibraltar and Switzerland. Postgraduate degrees have been introduced and the OU Business School is the largest such school in Europe. Some 2.0 million students have taken OU courses since 1971.

As the Open University developed, dedication, stamina and perseverance were necessary to complete its long, part-time courses. The first OU students, who were often employed, followed their lessons and lectures at home. Parttime tutors in local areas marked the students’ written work and met them regularly to discuss their progress. There were also special weekend and refresher courses throughout the year at universities and colleges to enable students to take part in intensive study. The television programmes, websites, books, printed and digital course materials, and software associated with the OU programmes were widely used throughout the world. The OU was generally considered to be a cost-effective success, has provided valuable alternative educational opportunities for many people and has served as a model for distance learning across the world.

The OU has also utilized computer and digital developments which have improved communications, conferencing techniques and web-based education. The old television offerings have been reduced. But OU/BBC television programmes still appear at peak time viewing, and have reached a global audience. In 2009, the OU adopted a new system of higher education funding and technological developments. Governments had made tuition fee loans available

to part-time learners for the first time, which helped OU students. In 2015–16, over 174,739 students were studying with the OU, including 34 per cent under 25 and over 7,430 overseas. Many students access course materials on smartphones and tablets at convenient and flexible times. According to a National Student Survey in 2016, 89 per cent of respondents gave an overall satisfaction rating with the quality of their course and the OU was in joint 28th place out of the top universities in the UK for student satisfaction.

##### Further, adult and lifelong education

An important aspect of British education is the provision of further and adult education, whether by colleges, universities, voluntary bodies, trade unions, the Workers’ Educational Association (WEA), evening institutes, local societies, clubs and other institutions. Courses may be vocational (for employment) or recreational (for pleasure), and cover a variety of activities and programmes.

Some of these organizations originated in a thirst for knowledge which was felt by working-class people in the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, particularly after the arrival of elementary state education and growing literacy. Today a wide range of educational opportunities is provided by self-governing state-funded colleges of further education (CFEs), and other types of public and private institutions. These may offer vocational and academic subjects for part and full-time students and may specialize in a wide variety of skills, such as art and design, catering, engineering and building trades, or business and finance. Some part-time students over sixteen, such as apprentices, may study in the evenings or on day-release from their employers and their studies are often work-related, include government training programmes and have close ties with local commerce, companies and industry. Full and part-time students of varying ages are taking a wide spectrum of further education courses; some colleges design courses especially for students from other countries; and the UK offers work-based training for students seeking to build careers in specific industries. Private training companies cooperate with colleges and employers to provide practical work and qualifications in subjects such as engineering, construction, ICT and social care.

For example, National Vocational Qualifications (NVQs) in England and Scottish Vocational Qualifications (SVQs) are gained through assessment of performance and knowledge at work and college. They are a valuable alternative to academic qualifications and NVQs 1–5 are the equivalent of GCSEs, A-levels, degrees and some postgraduate courses. Candidates may work towards an NVQ that reflects their employment role in a paid or voluntary position, such as someone in an administrative Office role taking an NVQ in Business and Administration.

In the past, a relatively low percentage of the 16–24 age group in Britain were in further and higher education, compared to the much larger percentages in Japan, the USA and Germany. The figures have now improved considerably, with more 16to 18-year-olds in Britain remaining in full-time education, either in school or in further education colleges. Yet the previous Labour government felt that even more people should be educated or trained after the age of 16, with a proposed 50 per cent passing through university. This view is particularly relevant at a time when there is an increasing shortage of well-qualified people for the future workforce, especially in the vocational, trade and technical fields. Today, all pupils must remain in some form of education or training until the age of 18.

There has been a recent expansion of continuing-education projects and a range of programmes specific ally designed for employment purposes and to provide people with access qualifications for further training. The previous Labour government for example saw further and adult education as part of a lifelong learning process, which it wanted to prioritize. The aim was to encourage the continuous development of people’s skills, knowledge and understanding as well as creating skills and improving employment prospects in a changing labour market.

##### Attitudes to education

Concerns about the quality of British schools, universities and educational policy frequently appear in opinion polls. Respondents argue that state schools are not performing well, that the educational system is confused, that more money should be spent on education, and that parents’ views about their children’s schools are not taken seriously by politicians. However, both Labour and Conservative governments have given more funding to the school system and created new types of schools, as well as reforming examination structures and the National Curriculum.

But dissatisfaction is still felt. For example, an _Economist_ Ipsos MORI poll in September 2017 placed education/schools in fourth place at 21 per cent in a list of the most important issues/problems facing Britain, following Brexit, the NHS and immigration.

According to budget figures in 2017, spending on education as a share of public spending continued to shrink compared with other big government departments, and was 7102 billion in 2018–19, a 12.7 per cent fall from 2017–

18\. This was largely attributed to austerity cuts following the credit crash of 2007. A central question for many critics is whether the available funds are in fact spent adequately. They query whether governments are committed enough to raising quality and performance, and whether education has the same political priority as other areas of state expenditure.

Many UK schools, for example, face resource and infrastructure difficulties, such as poor buildings and difficulties in filling teacher vacancies. Education was in third place for public sector spending at 7102 billion for 2018–19 or 4.4 per cent of GDP, but could drop to 3.8 per cent – the lowest budget share since the early 1960s.

Financial restraints and cuts were reflected in a 2017 survey by _Guardian_ Teacher Network where 81 per cent of teachers said that their school had made cutbacks or was planning to do so. Schools were not replacing leaving teachers, support staff were being made redundant and budgets for supply staff were being reduced. Budgets for IT equipment, books, extra-curricular activities and special education were lacking. Workloads were excessive, curriculum changes and cuts were a concern, classes were larger and preparation time was short, and demands for more school cutbacks were made by local education authorities. Such conditions are serious for those teachers who are trying to build their school assets.

A poll conducted by The Key Educational Consultancy and Ipsos MORI in 2015 found that school head teachers were undecided about which political party was best equipped to improve the education system in England. Many were dissatisfied with the educational policies of the political parties prior to the 2015 general election. Teacher morale, workload and recruitment were seen as major problems, as was government and LEA interference in education.

Yet despite these contemporary images, there is also a history of British education trying to provide a competent national service. There have been continuous debates about the performance and goals of British education at all levels since the 1960s. Traditionalist critics, who wanted disciplined learning programmes, felt that state comprehensive schools and ‘creative/progressive’ methods of child-centred teaching were not producing the kind of pupils needed for contemporary society. It was argued that they lacked the basic skills of numeracy and literacy and were unprepared for employment and the realities of the outside world. These arguments continue today and employers frequently criticize both schools and higher education for the quality of their students. The allegedly low quality of British education is often given by Britons as the reason for poor performance in many areas of contemporary life.

Conservative governments’ reforms from 1986 were based on centralized and consumer-choice policies. They attempted to rectify alleged educational weakness and aimed at producing accountability, improved standards and skills in schools and higher education through more formal learning programmes. Attempts were made to reform the teaching profession, improve pupil performance, emphasize science and modern languages and increase parental choice.

A Labour government from 1997 continued this functional reform process in schools by stressing compulsory homework, contracts with parents, ‘literacy hours’, concentration on the ‘3 Rs’ (reading, writing and arithmetic) and grouping children by ability (‘setting’). Progressive ‘child-centred’ methods

were dismissed and funds were provided for school repairs and computers in every school. There was also a move away from having only one type of state secondary school (comprehensive) to embrace diversity through the expansion of faith schools, specialist technology colleges, academies and ‘free schools’. The government also wanted greater numbers of poorer, disadvantaged and working-class students to enter university. But it is still maintained that ethnic and disadvantaged students are not gaining entry to competent schools in the inner cities or winning university places in sufficient numbers, particularly to elite institutions.

The arguments continue from both the left and the right of the political spectrum, arguably leaving the British educational system in an uncertain state. Some critics maintain that the school system should not be devoted solely either to elitist standards or to market considerations but should provide a choice between (and ideally a mixture of) the academic/liberal tradition, the technical and the vocational. The lack of adequate vocational/technical education and training is still creating serious problems for employers in industry and manufacturing who complain that they are unable to find competently trained staff to fill vacancies. The future of British education and employment may well depend on how reforms and new educational institutions function and how they are perceived by teachers, parents, students and employers. However, the performances of some of them continue to be criticized on social and educational grounds. Other critics argue that the British educational system (at school and higher levels) is too diverse and that there should be more simplified and inclusive structures and study plans.

The school debate in Britain often reveals polarized points of view and strong reactions to both Labour and Conservative policies, as revealed in an Ipsos MORI poll for the trade unions NASUWT and Unison in April 2010. When asked if more schools in the future should be run by private companies, religious groups, charities or groups of parents rather than the LEAs, 44 per cent considered this a bad idea and 24 per cent a good idea. The majority (62 per cent) thought that local authorities are best placed to run schools. These replies are in strong contrast to both recent Labour measures aimed at diversity of school types and Conservative plans for sponsored ‘free schools’ outside LEA control, but funded by the state and run by a variety of groups. However, some fierce critics in the 2015 General Election campaign thought that too many free schools were being created at great expense in areas where there was no educational need for them and which have become isolated state-funded independents.

However, despite the responses to school education and performance in the last decade, 54 per cent of respondents to the above poll thought that state education was generally good compared with 19 per cent who thought that it was poor. Some 79 per cent of parents thought that their own children’s schools were good and 65 per cent regarded the schools attended by children of relations and

friends as good. If a local authority was failing to deliver good standards of education, respondents considered that the government (37 per cent) and the local authority (26 per cent) are responsible for addressing the failings, even when schools are directly run by other organizations. When asked if parents should be asked to pay additional fees for their children’s education to supplement funding from taxes, 18 per cent supported the idea but some 64 per cent opposed it.

Despite the fact that this poll was commissioned by trade unions, respondents’ replies indicated a traditional response to schooling and local direction of education by LEAs, which may be widely supported. It might also suggest that they wanted certainty, control and stability in their children’s schools rather than constant change and need for diversity on the part of central government. However, other critics argue that structural changes and constant reassessment of the school system make little difference to performance. They maintain that there is a crisis in the status of the teaching profession which must first be addressed and remedied. They feel that only an increase in status and a move to attract talented graduates to the profession will provide the quality of teaching that basically drives success in a school. It is argued that teacher quality and retention of good teachers should be the priority for politicians and

educationalists.

Given the alleged state of current British education, and some of the current fierce debates on the teaching profession, it is interesting to consider the results of recent polls. British Social Attitudes in its 2016–17 survey found that 53 per cent of respondents had a great deal of respect for teachers and 39 per cent had some. Eighty per cent of respondents reported at least some confidence in the British school system; 83 per cent said that state schools teach young people basic skills (reading, writing, maths) ‘well’ or ‘very well’; 60 per cent said that secondary schools bring out students’ natural abilities well or very well; 47 per cent said that schools prepare students well or very well for work; 87 per cent said that it was essential or very important that schools aim to help young people develop skills and knowledge required for getting a good job; and 74 per cent thought that schools should share responsibility with parents and carers for a child’s personal and social development.
