# 11

## The media

- The print media
- The broadcasting media
- Media ownership and freedom of expression
- Attitudes to the media
- _Exercises_
- _Further reading_
- _Websites_

This chapter examines the historical development of the British media and current debates about them. They have long been an important part of British life and include communication systems by which people are informed, educated and entertained. The term has historically referred to the print industries (the press or newspapers, magazines and books) and broadcasting (radio and terrestrial, cable, and satellite television). These systems have overlapped with each other and with other media forms such as film, theatre, records, discs and video. They are profitable businesses and closely connected to advertising, corporate sponsorship, commerce and industry.

The British media have evolved from simple methods of production, distribution and communication to their contemporary advanced technologies. For example, cable and satellite television expanded quickly from the mid-twentieth century and most media forms have since been affected by electronic developments and the national switch-over from analogue to digital transmission in 2012.

The Internet, online and digital communication systems, smartphones, tablets and computers have become dominant media forces and have challenged the more traditional forms. They are also a crucial part of business, education, politics, publishing, news and entertainment. They cover homes, places of business and leisure activities, and their influence is powerful and an inevitable part of daily life.

An Office for National Statistics (ONS) survey in 2018 drew attention to these changes. It found that 90 per cent of households in Great Britain had Internet access. Broadband Internet with a variety of fibre optic or cable connections was the most popular type and used by 98 per cent of households. Eighty-six per cent of adults used the Internet at least weekly in 2018 and daily use has doubled since 2006. Mobiles and smartphones are the most popular devices used to access the Internet, 77 per cent of adults used the Internet ‘on the go’ and email remains the most common Internet activity. In 2018, 78 per cent of all adults bought goods or services online and older adults showed the largest increase in online shopping. Online banking increased to 69 per cent, Netflix use grew to 46 per cent and watching videos on YouTube reached 62 per cent. Social networking media, such as Twitter, Facebook, LinkedIn, Instagram, WhatsApp and blogging, became popular forms of daily communication among a mixed generational demographic. Within the UK in 2017, 66 per cent of age groups engaged in social networking, such as 96 per cent for those 16–24, 88 per cent of those 25–34 and 83 per cent for those between 35–44. But despite

the claims of such markets to be competitive and service-oriented, there are complaints about their quality, coverage and influence.

The effects of these technological and cultural developments were reported in an Ofcom study in 2017, which illustrated the changes in media usage among generations. Teenagers and children watched a third less broadcast TV on traditional sets than they had in 2010, as the shift to digital viewing and the rise of services such as Netflix and Amazon increased. Children (4 to 15 years old) watched 101 minutes of traditional broadcast TV a day in 2016, while 16to 24-year-olds watched 114 minutes, less than the average older viewer who watched 212 minutes a day. The over-65s/under-65 pensioners watched 344 minutes a day of TV viewing.

Another media development has been the popularity of on-demand TV, such as BBC iPlayer (the BBC repeat service) and ITV Player, or Netflix and Amazon. The BBC has increased its children’s TV budget and supports existing children’s channels, CBBC and CBeebies. It has moved its youth channel BBC 3 TV online and increased investment in iPlayer to match audience demand and digital rivals. The corporations occupy a crowded market while trying to preserve their identities.

Radical change also has its commercial, artistic and social problems. Ofcom’s 2016 report into British broadcasting found dissatisfaction with television programmes. Three in ten (29 per cent) viewers felt that programme quality had deteriorated. There were too many repeats, little variety, triviality and a lack of quality. Older adults (aged 55 and over) tended to feel that TV programme standards had worsened in the past 12 months.

British children between 11 and 15 watch less ‘live’ television each day than their parents. Half of them watch video clips online at least once a week, compared with 20 per cent of adults. The popularity of clips has created a new fashion for video bloggers who can attract large numbers of followers and advertising revenue. Ofcom research also showed the extent to which young people are using social media such as Facebook, WhatsApp and Instagram to communicate with each other. This increase in online activity has raised safety concerns as young people are increasingly affected by online influences. Yet effective controls by the media industries and regulators are allegedly lacking.

Historically, print media such as newspapers and magazines were often the only source of news. As the media expanded in the twentieth century, news production was still a central service and surveys showed that 69 per cent of Britons obtained their daily news from television, 20 per cent from print newspapers and 11 per cent from radio. These figures have changed in recent years as Internet usage has grown, print newspaper sales and advertising income have declined, and free online news sites were attractive. Readers had reacted to newspaper owners’ introduction of subscription charges for their online news sites and rival free online news sites were effective competitors. A Tech digest survey in 2009 found that 74 per cent of respondents would react by trying to

find another free site, 8 per cent would use free headlines only and 5 per cent would pay to continue reading the site.

According to a 2018 Ofcom report, _News Consumption in the UK_, television is the most-used platform for news by UK adults (79 per cent), followed by the Internet (64 per cent), radio (44 per cent) and newspapers (40 per cent). But the Internet is the most popular platform among 16–24s (82 per cent) and ethnic minority groups (73 per cent). BBC One is the most-used television news source (62 per cent of adults), followed by ITV (41 per cent) and Facebook (33 per cent). Changing media habits mean that many people are using varied sources of news and information, sometimes simultaneously with other electronic devices and sometimes concentrating on short summaries rather than in-depth analyses. Critics argue that this variety of choice is the future format of news presentation.

The media continue to provoke debates and complaints about what is socially and morally permissible in their content and methods. Questions are asked about the role of advertising and sponsorship; the quality of the services provided at a time of rapidly expanding and diversified media outlets; the alleged danger of the concentrated ownership of media resources; media influence on politics; legal restraints upon media ‘freedom of expression’; the potential abuse of media power and influence; bias and manipulation; and the responsibility of the media to individuals and society. After a series of phone hackings and other press abuses the continuing British debate in recent years has been whether the press (and the media in general) should be more tightly controlled and by whom.

##### The print media

The print media (newspapers and magazines) began to develop in the eighteenth century. Initially, a wide circulation was hindered by transportation and distribution problems, illiteracy, government licensing and censorship restrictions. But, over the last 200 years an expanded educational system, the abolition of government control, developments in print technology and Britain’s small physical size have eliminated these difficulties and created a free, outspoken and often controversial print media.

The growth of literacy after 1870 provided the owners of the print media with an increased market. Newspapers and magazines, which had previously been limited to the middle and upper classes, reached a wider readership. They were used for news and information, but also for profit and entertainment. Media ownership, new print formats and financially rewarding advertising increased in the competitive atmosphere of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries. Owners also realized that political and social influence, and the

dissemination of combative ideology, could be achieved through control of the means of communication.

###### National newspapers

National newspapers are those which are mostly published from London (with some regional versions) and are available in all parts of Britain on the same day, including Sundays. Many are still delivered direct to the home from local newsagents. Good internal distribution systems enabled a national press to develop and online copies of newspapers now offer updated news and immediate availability everywhere.

Regional newsletters and newspapers appeared in the sixteenth century, and the first titled London newspaper, _Corante_ (the _Daily Courant_), was published in 1621. The first British newspapers with a limited national circulation appeared in the early eighteenth century and were followed by others, such as _The Times_ (1785), the _Observer_ (1791) and the _Sunday Times_ (1822). Most of these were ‘quality’ papers, catering for a relatively small, educated market at a time of mass illiteracy.

In the nineteenth century, the growth and diverse composition of the population conditioned the types of newspaper that were produced. The first ‘popular’ national papers were deliberately printed on Sundays, among them the _News of the World_ (1843) and the _People_ (1881). They were inexpensive and aimed at the expanding and increasingly literate working class. In 1896, Alfred Harmsworth produced the _Daily Mail_, which was targeted at the lower middle class as an alternative to the ‘quality’ dailies. Harmsworth then published the _Daily Mirror_ in 1903 for the working-class ‘popular’ market. Both the _Mail_ and the _Mirror_ were soon selling more than a million copies a day.

The early twentieth century was the era of mass-circulation papers and of

owners (described as press barons) like Harmsworth and Arthur Pearson. There was fierce competition between them as they fought for bigger shares of the market. Pearson’s _Morning Herald_ (later the _Daily Express_) was created in 1900 to compete with the _Daily Mail_ for lower middle-class readers.

The _Daily Mirror_ was the largest-selling national daily in the early twentieth century. It supported the new Labour Party and was designed for quick and easy reading by the industrial and increasingly politicized working class. The _Daily Herald_ (1911) also supported the Labour Party, until in 1964 it was sold, was renamed the _Sun_ and developed different political and news emphases. The competition between the _Sun_ and _Mirror_ continues today, with each aiming for a bigger share of the mass daily market. Battles are still fought between dominant proprietors, since newspaper ownership is concentrated in a few large publishing groups, such as Rupert Murdoch’s News UK (which has large media holdings in Britain, Australia and the USA) and Trinity Mirror.

PLATE 11.1 News UK (publisher of _The Times_ and _The Sun_) printing plant at Broxbourne, near Enfield, North London. News UK (formerly News International) moved its printing operation to Broxbourne from Wapping in central London in 2010. © John Stillwell – WPA Pool/Getty Images

The success of the early popular press was due to growing literacy; a desire for knowledge and information (as well as entertainment) on the part of the working class; and increased political awareness among workers caused by the rise of the Labour Party. Newspaper owners profited by the huge market, but they also satisfied demand. The price and content of mass papers reflected lower middle and working-class readerships and tastes. This emphasis attracted a considerable amount of consumer advertising, and owners were able to produce papers cheaply with the aid of modern printing methods and a nationwide distribution network.

The circulation of national papers rose rapidly, with 5.5 million daily sales by 1920. By 1973 these had increased to 17 million. However, newspapers had to cope first with competition from radio and film and later from television. Although they have survived, there has been a continuing decline since the 1970s in sales and numbers of national and other newspapers as they face pressure from the Internet, mobile phones and tablets. For example, the main British national newspapers in 2018 had average circulation sales of some 5.4 million on weekdays (down from 7.4 million in 2014) and 4.1 million on Sundays (down from 8.9 million on 2014).

The national press in Britain in 2018 consisted of ten main daily morning papers and eight main Sunday papers, after more papers had closed down. It is

in effect a London press, because many national newspapers have their bases in the capital, although editions of some nationals are now published outside London, in Europe and the USA. Most of them used to be located in the area around Fleet Street in central London, but all have now moved to other parts of the capital or offices outside London. These moves were due to high property rents, competition between papers and opposition from trade unions to the introduction of new printing technology. Print media have also had to cope with the expense of newsprint, declines in advertising revenue, rising production and labour costs, and competition from other media outlets, such as the Internet.

Heavy labour costs were due to the overstaffing and restrictive practices of the trade unions. Owners were forced into new ways of increasing productivity while cutting costs. Regional owners outside London, as in Nottingham with its main paper _The Nottingham Evening Post_, had pioneered the movement of newspapers and magazines into the new print technology, and London newspapers were obliged to follow in order to survive.

The use of computers meant that journalists’ ‘copy’ could be printed directly, without having to use the traditional intermediate and lengthy ‘hotmetal’ typesetting by printers. This gave owners flexibility in their printing and distribution methods and lower production costs. It allowed them to escape from trade-union dominance and the concentration of the industry in London. But it also resulted in job losses, trade-union opposition and industrial action, such as mass picketing.

New equipment, improved distribution methods and cuts in labour and production costs had increased the profitability of the print industries to some extent. Despite the attraction of other media, they still have a considerable presence. The business is very competitive and papers can suffer from a variety of problems. However, the high risks involved have not stopped the introduction of new newspapers. For example, the quality national daily, the _Independent_ began publication in 1986 and survived despite circulation losses until it closed print operation in 2016. It continued online-only and its Sunday print edition also closed. The concise, compact-sized _i_ from the _Independent_ ownership appeared in 2010 until sold to Johnson Press in 2016. The best-selling Sunday _News of the World_ which was founded in 1843, was closed by News UK in 2011 due to telephone hacking and invasion of privacy scandals. On 26 February 2012 _The Sun on Sunday_ was launched to replace the _News of the World_, with some of its former journalists, a new typeface in 2013, but a reduced circulation in 2018 of 1,286,859.

Most national papers are usually termed either ‘quality’ or ‘popular’ (tabloid) depending on their differences in content. Others are called ‘mid-market’ and fall between these two extremes (see Table 11.1). The qualities (such as _The Times_) report national and international news in depth and analyse current events and the arts in editorials and articles. Some critics argue, however,

PLATE 11.2 A selection of national newspapers comprising populars (tabloids), mid-markets and qualities on a news stand, Great Marlborough Street, London, 2008. © Ray Tang/Shutterstock

that their content has been dumbed down (trivialized) and their features have become overly trendy and down-market. The populars (such as the _Sun_) deal with few ‘hard news’ stories, tend to be superficial in their treatment of events, and it is felt that much of their material is sensationalized. It is argued that populars are not instructive, or concerned with raising the critical consciousness of their readers. Yet owners and editors reply that their readerships demand particular styles, interests and attitudes. ‘Mid-market’ papers, such as the _Daily Mail_ and _Express_, cater for intermediate groups.

Qualities and populars were also historically distinguished by their format. Populars were tabloid or small-sheet while qualities were broadsheet (large sheet). This distinction has disappeared in recent years as more broadsheets

have become tabloid, ‘compact’ (The _Times_) or ‘Berliner’ (the _Guardian_) in format. Only the _Financial Times_ and the _Daily Telegraph_ are still broadsheets. Total sales of popular papers on weekdays and Sundays exceed those of the qualities. Fourteen of the papers in Table 11.1 have lost some circulation since 2009 and the rest have gained slightly. _The Sun on Sunday_, which effectively replaced the defunct _News of the World_ in 2012, achieved the highest Sunday circulation in 2018, carried on the best-selling traditions of the latter paper, but its sales are now declining. Qualities are more expensive than populars and carry up-market advertising that generates essential finance. The populars carry less advertising and cater for more down-market material. However, all forms of print advertising revenue have declined in profitability and market share in

recent years.

There is currently no state control or censorship of the British press, although it is subject to laws of publication and expression and there are forms of self-censorship, by which it is supposed to regulate its own conduct. The press is also financially independent of the political parties and receives no funding from government (except for Welsh-language community papers).

It is argued that most newspapers are politically right-of-centre and sympathize with the Conservative Party. But their positions are arguably often driven by readers’ and owners’/controllers’ opinions and their views can vary considerably over time and under the influence of events. For example, the small circulation leftist _Morning Star_ has varied between Stalinist, Euro-Communist and Democratic Left views. Papers may have a political bias and support a specific party, particularly at election times, although this can vary. A few, such as those of the Trinity Mirror group, tend consistently and vigorously to support the Labour Party. Some like _The Times_ consider themselves to be independent. Others, like the _Guardian_, favour a left-of-centre position, while the _Daily Telegraph_ supports the Conservative Party. It appears that the British public receive a reasonable variety of political views and coverage from their newspapers.

Newspapers are dependent for their survival upon circulation/sales figures ; upon the advertising they can attract; and upon financial help from their owners. A paper may face difficulties and fail if advertisers remove their business, and all the media have experienced difficulties in attracting advertising revenue. However, a high circulation does not necessarily guarantee the required advertising and consequent survival, because advertisers have tended to place their mass-appeal consumer products on television, where they will benefit from a larger audience, although the Internet is now rapidly attracting more advertising. Most popular papers are in constant competition with their rivals to increase their sales. They attempt to do this by gimmicks such as bingo games and competitions, or by calculated (often sensationalist and ideological) editorial policies which are intended to catch a mass or particular readership.

Most newspapers now have colour pages and daily and weekend supplements covering a range of interests that attempt to attract the newspaper-reading public and appeal to advertisers. Owners may refuse to rescue those papers which make continuous losses. A number of newspapers in the twentieth and twenty-first centuries ceased publication because of reduced circulation, advertising revenue losses, refusals of further financial aid or a combination of factors. Despite a fall in hard-copy circulation, most national newspapers now have online Internet versions, which are sometimes free (at least in outline format), but have increasingly to be paid for by subscription charges. Some also offer paid access to features and articles. This rapidly expanding service provides an additional medium for information, communication and advertising revenue as well as continuously updated news. Subscriptions may be required in future for

most (or all) online services.

There is much debate about declining newspaper sales in Britain and of an industry fighting to preserve its relevancy. However, _Media Week_ in August 2013 pointed out that the National Readership Survey suggested that newspapers still have a role in British life. Its readership figures showed that the leading daily and Sunday newspaper businesses in the UK (_The Sun_ group – 13.5 million, _The Mail_ group – 12 million, the free paper _The Metro_ – 7.8 million, _The Mirror_ group – 7.9 million and the _Guardian/Observer_ group – 5.3 million) were read by 46 million people, either in print or online every week. Despite annual sales continuing to show substantial declines, some polls have recently shown that many people still believe in the traditional principles, values and coverage of the print press.

###### Local, regional and ethnic newspapers

Some 228 local weekly newspapers are published in towns and cities throughout Britain, in addition to some 72 regional dailies which serve larger cities and geographical areas. They tend to focus on local or regional news, with some national and international features, and are supported financially by regional advertising. Some 75 per cent of local and regional newspapers also operate an Internet website.

However, Audit Bureau of Circulation (ABC) figures for February 2017 showed declines of 11.2 per cent in print sales for the majority of local weekly newspapers and 12.5 per cent for regional daily papers. There had also been a reduction in individual titles, mergers between titles, and closure of papers.

According to ABC, these figures suggest a continuing decline in print papers in recent years, arguably caused by price rises and editorial cutbacks. The readership has moved to local or national online sources, resulting in a small local website growth. But pressure on advertising overall has resulted in UK regional and local newspapers experiencing further declines in revenue flows. The biggest local weekly sellers in 2016 were Belfast’s _Sunday Life_ and the Isle of Wight’s _County Press_, while the best-performing regional daily was the _Yorkshire Post_.

Nevertheless, innovations in newspaper ownerships continue. There is a growth in free local print newspapers of varying sizes, some of which have national circulations. It is estimated that 800 local and regional outlets operate online websites, which indicates an increasing switch from print to online services. Local website growth has partly offset losses in print sales and advertising revenue.

London seems to have coped with some of the difficulties associated with local media. It has a dominant evening paper (the _Evening Standard_, founded in 1827) with a daily circulation of 897,523 copies in November 2017. In January 2009, the _Standard_ was purchased by Alexander Lebedev and became a free daily tabloid newspaper (Monday to Friday). Its business model now includes

an e-edition with free subscription, mobile phone platform links, digital services, a television station and increased financial sponsorship and ownership. There are also about a hundred other local weeklies, dailies and evening papers of various sizes and types which appear in the Greater London districts.

Quality daily regional papers, such as _The Scotsman_ (Edinburgh), the _Glasgow Herald_, the _Western Mail_ (Cardiff), and the _Yorkshire Post_ (Leeds), have historically had good reputations and attract sales outside their specific regions. Other Scottish papers are the _Daily Record_, the popular _Sunday Mail_ (Glasgow) and the _Sunday Post_ (Dundee). Northern Irish papers include the Belfast-based _Belfast Telegraph_ and the _News Letter_, and others which serve the different sectarian communities. But many have suffered from decreasing sales and circulation.

There has been a growth of other ‘free newspapers’ throughout Britain, such as the tabloid daily _Metro_ with a circulation of 1,476,956 in London in November 2017 (which is widely available in urban areas throughout the country _–_ Monday to Friday mornings _–_ such as buses, shops, train stations, airports, hospitals and street stalls, and has an online service). While growth of new free papers seems to be slackening, some 650 of them, such as the _Manchester Metro News_ and the _Glaswegian_, are published weekly on a local basis and are financed by local advertising, to such an extent that news is often outweighed by the advertisements. It is estimated that they have a total weekly circulation of some 29 million. They are often delivered direct to homes, as well as being widely available elsewhere. However, like the national papers, the regional, free and local press has been severely affected by the recent downturn in advertising revenue, on which it is dependent for survival.

It is argued that ethnic minorities are still underrepresented in the British media. However, their communities do produce some 70 newspapers and magazines, which are increasing in number, are available nationally in the larger cities and are improving in quality. Some, such as Muslim magazines, are becoming more mainstream in an attempt to appeal to a younger Muslim and wider non-Muslim audience. There is a wide range of publications for Jewish, Asian, Afro-Caribbean, Chinese and Arabic readers, published on a daily or (more commonly) periodic basis, such as the _Asian Times_ and Afro-Caribbean papers such as the _Gleaner_ (founded in 1951), the _Voice_, _New Nation_ and _Caribbean Times_. A large recent immigration from Eastern Europe and the wider EU has created more ethnic communities and resulted in publications in Polish, Latvian and Rumanian, as well as existing French, Spanish, Italian and German media.

Some of the ethnic papers are resilient and enthusiastic, some quickly disappear and most lack financial support. Many have inadequate publishing sites and sales. They struggle to attract advertising from the private sector and rely for their income from the cover price and from local and national government advertising. Yet Sky Media/Ethnic Media Sales (EMS) in 2017 suggested a potential market. Census figures indicate that 14 per cent of the UK population

identify as an ethnic minority (28 per cent of all 16–34s) and spent up to 50 per cent of their TV viewing time watching ethnic channels. While 77 per cent of British Asians feel that mainstream advertising has no relevance to them, 70 ethnic newspapers and magazines were produced in 2013 for UK readers in this growing market. According to EMS, 67 per cent of Asian TV viewers read Asian newspapers or magazines and 96 per cent of ethnic press writers are ethnic, while 94 per cent of mainstream journalists, by contrast, are white. EMS also reported in 2013 that Asian and African communities in Britain experienced fast growth in mobile online usage; the minimum number of mobile phones owned by Asian households is three; and 67 per cent of Asian groups regularly use mobile phones.

###### Periodicals and magazines

There are some 8,000 periodicals and magazines in Britain (2014), which are of a weekly, monthly or quarterly nature and are dependent upon sales and advertising to survive (7993 million in 2014). They are aimed at different markets and levels of sophistication and either cover trades, professions and business (read by 95 per cent of occupational groups) or are consumer titles dealing with sports, hobbies and interests (read by 80 per cent of adults).

Although the number of periodicals has expanded overall, it is still difficult to break into the established consumer market with a new product. Some attempts, which manage to find a gap in the market, succeed, but many do not. Other new periodicals which are initially successful may also become victims of competing fashions and trends.

The teenage and youth magazine market is fiercely fought for, but has suffered large sales losses recently. This is attributed to greater Internet and mobile phone usage. The men’s general interest magazine market (some magazines having a specific ally ‘laddish’ appeal) is similarly volatile. Women’s periodicals, such as _Take a Break_, _Woman_ and _Woman’s Own_, have large and wide circulations. But the best-selling publications in December 2013, according to ABC, were _The National Trust Magazine_ covering heritage, country houses and gardens with a circulation of 2,024,010 in first place, followed by the supermarket periodicals _Asda Magazine_ (1,983,433) and _Tesco Magazine_ (1,935,680). Consistently high in the best-seller list are the weekly _TV Choice_, _What’s on TV_ and _Radio Times_, which contain feature stories and scheduled programmes for BBC and independent television and radio. Other magazines cover interests such as computers, rural pastimes, gardening, railways, cooking, a wide variety of sports, architecture and do-it-yourself skills. Well-stocked newsagents and general shops illustrate the large number of offerings.

Among the serious weekly journals are the _New Statesman and Society_ (a left-wing political and social affairs magazine); _The Economist_ (dealing with economic and political matters); the _Spectator_ (a conservative journal); and _New_

_Scientist_. _The Times_ publishes influential weekly magazines, such as the _Educational Supplement_ and the _Higher_ (education supplement). The lighter side of the market is catered for by periodicals such as _Private Eye_, which satirizes the shortcomings of British society.

Critics question the survival of magazines and their relationship with advertising, and online activity is important. Advertising revenue from print versions is crucial for the survival of most magazines which might not survive the loss of income if they stopped their print versions and moved completely online. It is difficult for web-only publishing formats to create a web-and-print model, although some blogs and special interest websites are successful. Consumer magazines and periodicals are struggling but in 2012 most of the magazine market was still print.

##### The broadcasting media

The broadcasting media are divided into what has traditionally been thought of as two sectors. The ‘public sector’ is the British Broadcasting Corporation (BBC) financed by the licence fee (payable by anyone who owns a television set). The ‘independent sector’ consists of privately owned commercial stations or channels, which are funded by advertising revenue and subscriptions. Both sectors cover radio, terrestrial (earth-based) television and cable/satellite television. Most services have now moved from analogue to digital operating/transmitting systems.

Since 2003, the Office of Communications (Ofcom) has replaced the roles and duties of former regulators in both public and independent sectors and is the single regulator for the broadcast media. But although the BBC is subject to Ofcom regulation on programme standards and economic management, the BBC Trust is responsible for managing its public service remit. Opinions differ as to the future of the BBC in its present role and the continuance of its funding by the licence fee.

Historically, radio was the first broadcasting medium to appear in Britain. Experimental transmissions were made at the end of the nineteenth century by Marconi and were developed further in the early twentieth century. After a period of limited availability, national radio was established in 1922 when the British Broadcasting Company was formed under John Reith.

In 1927 Reith became the first director-general of the BBC and set the tone and style for its development. Funded by the licence fee, it had a monopoly on national broadcasting and a paternalistic image. Reith insisted that it should be independent of government and commercial interests, strive for quality, and be a ‘public service broadcaster’ (defined as having a duty to inform, educate and entertain). On this basis, the BBC built a global reputation for impartial news reporting and excellent programmes.

The BBC’s broadcasting monopoly in radio and television (the latter starting in 1936 for a limited audience) led to pressure from commercial and political interests to widen the scope of broadcasting. As a result, independent television financed by advertising and under the supervision of the Independent Television Authority (ITA) was created in 1954 and the first programmes were shown in 1955. The BBC’s monopoly on radio broadcasting also ended in 1972 and independent radio stations were established throughout the country, funded by advertising.

A duopoly (two organizations) then covered broadcasting: the public service of the BBC and the independent service of the ITA. The latter was expanded as cable, satellite and other services developed. The ITA evolved into the IBA (Independent Broadcasting Authority), which was succeeded by the ITC (Independent Television Commission) and the Radio Authority. These were replaced in 2003 by Ofcom. British broadcasting has thus been conditioned by change and the competition between the BBC and independent organizations.

Substantial reforms to British broadcasting were made by Conservative governments in the 1980s and 1990s, which created more radio and television channels. A deregulation policy was supposed to promote competition among broadcasters and more choice for consumers.

Critics have criticized these changes. It was felt that a larger number of television and radio channels need not necessarily lead to greater choice, but rather to programmes of the same type. There is a finite number of people available to watch television or listen to radio, and advertisers’ budgets cannot be stretched to cover all available broadcast offerings. According to Ofcom (2014) television’s proportion of total revenue generated by advertising was 29 per cent in 2013, and had remained constant since 2008. It has, however, now been replaced by the Internet as the biggest advertising sector in the UK, and value shares in 2014 were television at 74.9 billion and the Internet at 77.2 billion.

The public service ethos of the BBC is also a statutory requirement for all terrestrial independent channels. Ofcom (as the regulator of communications) is concerned to maintain the quality of programmes by the terrestrial public service broadcasters (BBC1, 2, 3 and 4, ITV1, 2, 3 and 4, Channel 4, Channel Five and SC4). Under legislation they must provide a minimum level of different types of programming. Ofcom has found that standards are dropping; there is too much reliance on programmes with popular appeal; and the audience share for public service broadcasters is falling. According to Statista, the largest television broadcasters in the UK in 2017 by audience share were BBC (31.57 per cent), ITV (21.71 per cent), Channel 4 (10.23 per cent) and Channel 5 (6.53 per cent).

###### The BBC

The BBC is based at New Broadcasting House in central London, with centres throughout the UK providing regional networks for radio and television. It was

created by Royal Charter and has a Trust that is responsible for supervising programmes and that comprises trustees who are appointed by the Crown on the advice of government ministers and constitute an independent element within the organization of the BBC organization. The trust chooses the director general, who controls the daily operations of the BBC. The BBC leadership has recently been criticized for its handling of historic scandals and management procedures, such as salary structures in its workforce.

The BBC is financed by a grant from Parliament, which comes from the sale of television licences (75.1 billion in 2013–14). These are payable by anyone who owns a television set and are relatively low in international terms (7145.50 annually for a colour set and 749 for black and white in 2010). The BBC additionally receives income from selling its programmes and services abroad through its commercial arm Worldwide Ltd, which generates a quarter of BBC revenue. It also benefits from the sale of a programme guide (_Radio Times_), books, magazines, videos and DVDs.

The BBC is under pressure from government and critics. It has struggled to maintain its position as a public service broadcaster, funded by the licence fee, at a time of competition from independent rivals. Internal reorganization and cost-cutting led to a slimmer organization, but it is regarded by its critics as overstaffed with too many posts in management. It has developed alternative forms of funding, such as subscription and pay services and must include independent productions in its television offerings. Frequent demands for re-evaluation of the licence fee raise questions about the future of the BBC.

The BBC’s external services, which consist of radio broadcasts in English (World Service) and 42 other languages abroad, were founded in 1932 and since 2014 have been funded by the BBC. These have a reputation for objective news reporting and programmes. The BBC also began transmitting commercially funded television channels in 1991 by cable to Europe and by satellite links to Africa and Asia. Today, BBC News is an international 24-hour news and TV information channel. It has the largest staff and audience (74 million weekly viewers in more than 200 countries worldwide) of any BBC television service. It is owned, operated and funded by the BBC’s commercial arm.

The BBC is not a state organization, controlled by the government. However, it is not as independent of political pressures as many in Britain and overseas assume. Its charter has to be renewed by Parliament and government can, and does, intervene in the showing of programmes which are alleged to be controversial or against the public interest. The BBC trustees, although independent, are government appointees. Governments can also exert pressure upon the BBC when the licence fee comes up for renewal by Parliament. The BBC tries to be neutral in political matters, to such an extent that all political parties have complained that it is prejudiced against them, being either too liberal/critical or too conservative. The major parties have equal rights to broadcast short party political units on the BBC and independent television, usually at election times.

Historically, the BBC (with its monopoly on radio) was affected by the invention of television, which changed British entertainment and news habits. The BBC now has two main terrestrial television channels (BBC1 and BBC2), with an audience share of 31.57 in 2017. BBC1 is a mass-appeal channel and consists of news, plays and drama series, comedy, quiz shows, competitions of various kinds, variety performances, sport and documentaries. BBC2 tends to show more serious items such as news analysis and discussion, documentaries, adaptations of novels into plays and series, operas and concerts.

The Labour government from 2001 expanded BBC television services by the creation of two new channels BBC3 (contemporary entertainment, comedy, music and drama) and BBC4 (culture and the arts), as well as a children’s channel CBBC. They operated via digital cable, terrestrial, IPTV and satellite platforms. However, audience figures for these channels were initially low and in 2014 it was decided to drop BBC3 from the TV schedules and move it online, as part of cost-cutting plans.

BBC Radio performs an important service, although some of its audiences have fluctuated recently. There are five national stations; 40 local stations serving many districts in England and the Channel Islands; and regional and community services in Scotland, Wales, Shetland, Orkney, Foyle and Northern Ireland. They all have to compete for listeners between themselves and with independent stations but offer an alternative in news, debate and local information to pop based local and national independent stations. The national channels specialize in different tastes. In 2018, the audience share for Radio 1, which caters for pop music and a young audience, was 6.1m listeners; Radio 2 with 12.2m listeners has adult-oriented light music, news and comedy; Radio 3 with 6.6 million listeners provides classical music, modern jazz and world music, talks, discussions and plays; Radio 4 with 10.7 million listeners concentrates on the spoken word, news reports, analysis, talks and plays; Radio Five Live (established 1990) has sport and news programmes; the BBC Asian Network is aimed at British Asians; and BBC6 Music with 1.96 million listeners provides rock, funk and alternative music. Despite the challenge from streaming music services and on-demand competitors, radio listening is high and BBC Radio has 58.7 per cent of total national and local radio market share (with independent radio at 41.3 per cent).

###### Independent broadcasting (The Office of Communications – Ofcom)

Ofcom does not make or produce programmes itself. Its government-appointed board regulates the independent television and radio companies (cable and satellite), and formally the BBC. It grants licences to the transmitting companies which commission many of the programmes shown on advertising financed television channels (ITV, ITV 2, ITV 3 and ITV 4, Channel 4 and Channel 5).

The ITV Network is the biggest commercial television network in the UK, has 15 regional licences and provides television to viewers across the country. The licence-holders cooperate with each other to some degree, commission and schedule television programmes and provide regional programming. Most of the licences since 2004 in England and Wales have been held by ITV plc, and smaller companies in England, Scotland, Northern Ireland and the Channel Islands hold the rest.

The licences granted to ITV companies are renewable every ten years and the companies have to compete with any other interested applicants. They receive nothing from the television licence fee, which applies only to the BBC. The companies are thus dependent upon advertising and subscription finance and the sales of programmes, videos, DVDs, books, records and other publications.

ITV had a 21.71 per cent audience share in 2017. ITV1 is the oldest independent television channel and once seemed only to provide popular programmes of a light-entertainment type. Although its quality has improved and it has a high standard of news reports, drama productions and documentaries, critics argue that the hunt for audience ratings is again producing mass entertainment reality shows, music and food competitions, many quizzes and repeat comedies.

ITV2, ITV3 and ITV4 continue this mixture, with ITV4 containing many repeats.

Channel 4 (with 10.23 per cent of audience share) was established in 1982 to create a commercial alternative to BBC2. It is a public corporation, which is funded by selling its own advertising time. It was intended to offer something different and challenging in an appeal to minority tastes and provides programmes in Welsh in Wales (S4C). Channel 4 initially had problems with advertising and the quality of its programmes (commissioned from independent producers), but has now developed a considerable reputation for its news and documentary productions, art programmes and films, although its audience share is declining (6.53 per cent in 2017).

It has been argued that the former regulator, ITC, did not always adequately supervise independent broadcasting developments and allegedly lacked consistent policies. There had been questions over its system of awarding ITV1 licences and critics felt that there was a need to emphasize programme quality and production efficiency. Ofcom is an attempt to improve on this structure, but opinions are divided on its performance.

Television and its associated technological developments have become very attractive in Britain and a rich source of entertainment. At one stage, it was thought that cable television by subscription would considerably expand these possibilities. Cable television (with the main providers being Virgin Media, Sky, Talk Talk and BT) is growing through digital technology (with an increased

number of channels) and is potentially available to 12.5 million homes largely in urban and suburban areas, with 9 million subscribers. But it is challenged by satellite and streaming platforms.

Television broadcasting directly by satellite through subscription was established in Britain by mergers between existing companies which formed British Sky Broadcasting (BSkyB) with five satellite channels. It was the biggest UK satellite television programmer in 2010 with 10 million subscribers and has added

2.4 million more subscribers since then. Its channels provide news, light entertainment, sport and feature films. The choice of satellite channels is expanding steadily through digital technology with over 420 radio and television satellite servers providing programming in Britain. One in three homes in the UK and Ireland use the Sky service. However, despite its expanded and costly sports presentations, it still lags behind terrestrial ITV and BBC1 in audience viewing share and it faces increased competition from new Internet and streaming providers.

Ofcom also controls independent radio, which includes three national stations and some 150 local and regional stations throughout the country. All are funded by advertising and revenue figures suggest that radio is the fastest growing media outlet in Britain. The national independent radio stations were created by Conservative governments to expand radio broadcasting. The first licence was awarded in 1991 to Classic FM (popular classical music and news bulletins), the second in 1992 to Virgin 1215 (rock music), and the third in 1995 to Talk Radio UK (speech-based).

Independent local radio was originally intended to be public service radio, funded by advertising and tightly regulated local radio stations (LCRs). Each was owned by local people, with local broadcasting stations. Apart from the shared Independent Radio News, networking and syndication were infrequent, and each station produced a varied local output. This structure changed as independent radio became commercial radio. Expansion has occurred at city, local and community levels because broadcasting was deregulated by government in an attempt to increase the variety of radio and include more tastes and interests. However, in 2010, local and national independent stations accounted for only 41.3 per cent of total radio audience share. Critics argue that this low figure (compared with the BBC) is due to independent stations neglecting programmes with a local identity. Instead they have become very commercialized with networking and automation to produce genre programming and left local emphasis to community radio.

Although both BBC and commercial radio appeared to be growing in recent years, broadcasters warn that radio stations are threatened by streaming with smartphones as young people increasingly turn away from traditional radio. It is argued that the radio industry needs to become an international entertainment brand that can offer on-demand content rather than solely focusing on UK broadcasting.

###### The role and influence of television and other media

Television historically has been a popular entertainment activity which attracted large audiences for events, programmes and series in modern Britain. But arguably its latter spread over many different platforms has dissipated its earlier influence and dominance. According to TV licensing, some 96 per cent of homes in 2013 had television sets in their homes; 94 per cent of these were colour sets; over 50 per cent of homes have two sets or more; and most have been digitalized.

There are contrasting views about the survival of British television culture and TV viewing. A report from the marketing body Thinkbox in 2014 suggested that the traditional television set is still central to British viewing and is the favourite way to watch TV, particularly as on-demand and streaming services become available on better and bigger screens.

However, the telecom and media regulator Ofcom reported in 2014 that the number of households that own a television set had in fact declined by 500,000 as viewers chose to watch films and videos on smartphones, tablets, games consoles and laptops. Although a Telescope study found in 2013 that more people were watching TV despite a decline in the number of television sets per household, an Accenture survey in 2015 suggested that viewing films and TV programmes on a traditional set had fallen by 13 per cent over the year 2014–15, largely because of faster fibre optic broadband computer speeds. In this view, the image of the television set as the focal point for the nuclear family of the 1950s is breaking down.

It has also been recently suggested that radio (independent and BBC) was in fact more popular than television and indicated that some people were deserting the latter because of its alleged declining quality. Nevertheless, despite years when the statistics have been variable and a pattern of decline seems to be obvious, average television viewing time in 2014 was slightly down at

3.55 hours a day (25 hours per week) according to Thinkbox and about 94 per cent of individuals watched TV at least once a week.

Most of the programmes shown on television are made in Britain, although there are also many imported American drama, crime and musical series which prove popular. A few programmes come from other English-speaking countries, such as Australia, New Zealand and Canada. But there are relatively few foreign-language productions on British television and these are either dubbed or sub-titled. However, quality sub-titled Scandinavian, French and Italian crime series have proved very popular in recent years.

The range of programmes shown is very considerable, but they also vary widely in quality. Although some British television has a high reputation abroad, it does attract substantial criticism in Britain, either because of the alleged low

standard of some programmes, a lack of variety, the tameness of content or the frequent repetition of programmes. For example, some 60 per cent of the programmes shown over the Christmas period are repeats. News reports, documentaries and current-affairs analyses are generally of a high standard, as are some dramatic, educational, sporting, natural history and cultural productions. But there is also a wide selection of series, soap operas, competitions, films, quizzes, reality programmes and variety shows which are of doubtful quality to some, although they do appeal to significant sections of the viewing public.

Reality TV, dance and singing talent shows, confrontational and confessional programmes, and the profusion of makeover, decorating, food and property series have led to charges of a ‘dumbing-down’ of British television. Many of these programmes are calculated to appeal to a mass audience, a search for high ratings, and involve aggression, celebrity worship, desire for instant fame and emotional display. Independent television companies need such successful offerings to attract advertising and income. It is also necessary for the BBC to produce commercial offerings to demonstrate its own audience mass appeal.

Competition between the BBC and independent television is strong and the battle of the ratings (the number of people watching individual programmes) indicates their popularity, with Thinkbox for example reporting that in the first quarter of 2010, 61.46 per cent of all TV viewing was on commercial channels. But competition can mean that similar programmes are shown at the same time on the major channels, in order to appeal to specific markets and attract the biggest share of the audience. It is also argued that competition has reduced the quality of programmes overall. The BBC in particular is criticized for its increasing commercialism and failure to provide enough quality arts, drama and news programmes. It is argued that the BBC must maintain its public service obligations to quality and creativity in order to justify the licence fee.

There has been regular criticism about the alleged levels of sex, vulgarity, violence and bad language on British television and radio generally, and even before the ‘watershed’ of 9 p.m. in the evenings when young children may be watching. Some groups and individuals have attempted to reform and influence the kind of programmes that are shown. Academic research had denied a media influence on audiences, but some critics now suggest that individuals can in fact be morally harmed by the content, images, behaviour and speech of particular television and radio programmes, which can be rapidly imitated, particularly by children.

Previous Conservative governments considered that violence, sex and obscenity on television did affect and de-sensitive viewers and could encourage copycat behaviour. Attempts have been made to ‘clean up’ television, and Ofcom now monitors programmes, examines complaints, establishes codes of conduct for the broadcasting organizations and has tightened its rules concerning invasion of privacy by broadcasters. However, polls and surveys have

suggested that Britons are becoming more permissive about the portrayal of sex and different lifestyles in the media if this is relevant to a plot, and even more accepting if it occurs outside a family context on adult channels, video and films. There continues to be an ‘entertainment’ and ‘information’ expansion in Britain where online production companies fight to attract viewers and advertising revenue. But critics query whether this means more genuine media choice or declining quality. Digital broadcasting has increased television channels and transformed some media into interactive forces that combine the Internet and personalized streaming (such as Netflix) and programming in one package.

Broadcasters risk losing audiences and revenue as more people switch between different forms as an alternative to traditional television programmes.

Based on research by Ipsos MORI, the BBC reported in March 2018 that young people spend more time watching Netflix than BBC television, including its repeat function iPlayer. It is argued that this shift in viewing habits questions the long-term relevance of the BBC funded by licence fees. Netflix charges subscribers a monthly fee for on-demand access to its content archive of films and programmes, which became operative in Britain in 2012. Research showed that while Britons aged 16 to 34 spend about two hours a week with the BBC, ITV and Netflix, those in the 16–24 age bracket watch more Netflix streaming offers than the BBC television services provide.

Ipsos MORI research also reported that the BBC’s radio network has also been overtaken by music streaming services, such as Spotify and Apple Music. The last three months of 2017 revealed that people aged 15–34 listened to more hours of streamed music a week than BBC offers. The BBC wants more digital operations, to greatly increase the iPlayer attraction, and to provide more apps.

##### Media ownership and freedom of expression

The financial and ownership structures of contemporary British media industries are complex and have traditionally involved a range of media outlets which gradually included the press, radio and television.

Sometimes, an individual or company would own and specialize in a number of print products, such as newspapers and magazines. However, although this kind of ownership still exists, it is declining. Today newspapers may be owned and controlled by corporations that have wider media interests, such as films, radio, television, magazines, and satellite, cable and digital companies. Other newspaper and media-owning groups have diversified their interests even further, and may be involved in a variety of non-media activities. In Britain, only a few newspapers, such as the _Guardian_ and the _Morning Star_, have avoided being controlled by multinational and multi-media commercial concerns.

This involvement of large enterprises in the media, and the concentration of ownership in a few hands, such as newspapers and other media interests controlled by UK International and Trinity Mirror, has caused concern. Although these corporations do not yet amount to a monopoly situation, questions of ownership and control remain. Some critics argue that the state should provide public subsidies to the media industries in order to prevent them being taken over by big-business groups. But it is felt that there are dangers in allowing the state to gain any direct or indirect financial and political influence over the media.

Today the law is supposed to guard against the risks inherent in greatly concentrated ownership of the means of communication. The purchase of further newspapers by an existing owner is controlled by law, and newspaper owners’ shareholdings in independent radio and television stations are limited. Further restrictions, such as independent directors of newspapers, guarantees of editorial independence from owners’ interference, and trustee arrangements to allow newspapers to maintain their character and traditions, are usually imposed. These arrangements are intended to prevent monopolies and undue influence by owners. However, such safeguards do not always work satisfactorily in practice.

The questions of free expression in the media and control over press content continue to be of concern and have not been satisfactorily resolved. Some critics argue that the media do not have sufficient freedom to comment on matters of public and private interest. But the freedom of the media, as of individuals, to express themselves, is not absolute. Regulations are placed upon the general freedom in order to safeguard the legitimate interests of other individuals, other organizations and the state, so that a balance between competing interests may be achieved.

There are several legal restraints upon media freedom of expression. The _sub judice_ rule means that the media may not comment on court proceedings and must restrict themselves to reporting the court facts. The rule is intended to protect the individuals concerned, and if a media organization or person breaks the rule, it or they may be found guilty of contempt of court and fines or prison sentences may be imposed. Contempt of court proceedings may also be used by judges to obtain journalists’ sources of information, or to prevent the media from publishing court details and documents. Some journalists have refused to disclose their sources and have gone to prison.

The obtaining and publishing of state and official information is controlled by specific laws such as Official Secrets Acts and by devices such as D-notices (directives to the media concerning information that should not be divulged). The media are also liable to court proceedings for libel and obscenity offences. Libel is the making of accusations which are proved to be false or harmful to a person’s reputation. Obscenity covers any action that offends against public morality. In such cases, the media organization and all the individuals involved

may be held responsible. There are current debates about whether the strict English libel laws and their application should be relaxed because they may inhibit freedom of expression.

Legislation by a previous Labour government has had considerable media implications. The Human Rights Act (1998) is a two-edged sword in terms of free expression. On the one hand, it supposedly allows the media greater freedom of expression to comment on matters of public interest and importance. On the other, it allows individuals to complain and claim compensation if they feel that their individual rights and privacy have been infringed (for example, by the media).

This situation was exemplified by the so-called ‘Hacked-Off’ campaign in 2005 when individuals maintained that their phone voicemails had been hacked (broken into) and their privacy invaded by the press and others. Criminal trials followed against journalists, press managers and the police, some of whom were found guilty. Eventually, _The News of the World_ newspaper, which was implicated, was closed down by its proprietor, Rupert Murdoch, in July 2011 and the Leveson Inquiry into the practices and ethics of the press began in November 2011. A report by Lord Justice Leveson was published in November 2012 with proposals for closer control over the press. The government wanted a legislative underpinning of the press, and a Royal Charter on regulation of the press was granted by the Privy Council in October 2013, despite legal challenges by newspaper publishers. The latter wanted self-regulation and set up the unrecognized press-funded Independent Press Standards Organization (IPSO) in 2014, which consists of most British newspapers. It is an independent regulator of the newspaper and magazine industry to promote journalistic standards and to support members of the public in their complaints that the Editors’ Code of Practice has been broached. Another body, Impress, was approved by the Press Recognition Panel (PRP), but represents only some 25 specialist publications financed by private individuals. The resulting situation is complex, there is conflict between the media bodies, and there were demands in 2018 for another enquiry into media action. The debate continues on the freedom, independence and self-regulation of the press and the implied threat of governmental action against the press. It seems that the press will have to act strongly on allegations of press abuse. Otherwise state action could be invoked.

The Leveson Inquiry arguably preserves the unsteady institutional power

relationship between newspaper proprietors, politicians, government and the police. The Media Reform Coalition concluded in November 2013 that the outcome of the Leveson Inquiry illustrates the ways in which change and reform operate in Britain:

Time after time, newspaper readers have shown in polls that they trust neither politicians nor proprietors to deliver an ethical and independent press while a substantial majority of the public wants to see

action taken to limit the size and influence of the largest media outlets. However, it does seem that, for now, corporate media interests have outmaneuvered those fighting for meaningful change … There is no reason to think that anything has changed since the \[Leveson\] report was published and it will take action far beyond that of a public inquiry to tackle the problems that \[Leveson\] raised … and that still wait to be adequately addressed.

Similar questions of interpretation of what is allowable behaviour by the media and how they can be effectively policed arose when legislation was passed in 2006 which made incitement to religious hatred a criminal offence in England and Wales. Although much will depend upon actual court decisions, these may arguably restrict the media’s and artists’ rights of expression to comment critically on religion and religious belief. The question becomes one of distinguishing between permissible critical comment and allegedly illegal behaviour. The success of a charge of incitement seems to rest on the subjective or personal assessment of the complainant. A poll by the New Culture Forum in 2014 found that 77 per cent of respondents thought that people should be able to make statements about religion in books, television and other media that might offend some believers. Twelve per cent thought that they should not. Restrictions prevent absolute media freedom of expression and raise questions about the nature of such control. It is argued that, despite legislation, there is still a need for reform if responsible investigative journalism and the media are to do their job adequately. Britain is a secretive society, and it was thought that the Labour government’s Freedom of Information Act (2000) might break down some of the secrecy and executive control. But, while the media have been able to use the Act successfully in some cases, critics suggest that it is not working

satisfactorily and that official bodies avoid giving the information requested.

On the other hand, the media can act irresponsibly, invade individual privacy, behave in unethical and criminal ways and sensationalize events for their own purposes. The media have won some libel cases brought against them and gained important victories for open information. But they have also lost other cases because of their methods. Such media practices do cause concern and the government could impose statutory restrictions on invasions of privacy unless the media reform themselves. But some critics feel that freedom of expression could be less restricted than it is at present.

It is sometimes argued that the concentrated ownership patterns of the media might limit freedom of journalistic expression by allowing owners undue influence over what is included in their products. Former journalists have claimed that there is proprietorial interference in some of the media, which is not being curbed either by editorial guarantees or by government restrictions. On the other hand, editors and journalists can be very independently minded people, who will usually object to any attempts at interference. Some owners

PLATE 11.3 The worldwide headquarters of the BBC at New Broadcasting House, Portland Place, London, underwent major renovation and rebuilding which was completed in 2012–

13\. © David A. Eastley/Alamy Stock Photo

also seem to be careful not to tread on too many toes, because there are always competing media sources that are willing to publish the facts.

A further concern about limitations on media freedom has been the extent to which advertisers might dictate policy and content when they place their products. The question of advertisers’ influence is complex and might today be more applicable to the mass-consumer markets of radio, television and social media than the press. Advertisers dealing with the press are more concerned with the type or status of readers rather than with their numbers. Arguably, the media have not succumbed in a substantial degree to the direct manipulations of advertising agencies, in spite of their dependence upon advertising revenue.

##### Attitudes to the media

Opinion polls suggest that most British people have traditionally tended to see the media for what they are and have no great illusions about them. Respondents are generally sceptical of the press and journalists (who frequently appear at the bottom of lists of trustworthy professionals), are suspicious of the content of newspapers and television and do not believe that they present all sides of a question fairly.

There are also conflicting views about whether the media play a dominant part in influencing public opinion on political and social matters. The left-wing view assumes that they do and consequently disapproves of the alleged 70 per cent right-wing bias in the British media. But, while some people may have their attitudes directly shaped in these ways, it is argued that many readers and viewers have already made up their own minds and react against any blatant attempts at indoctrination. On some occasions (such as elections), the media may appear to have an effect on public opinion and may stress what they see as their influence (‘It’s _The Sun_ Wot Wun It’ ran a _Sun_ headline on 11 April 1992 after that year’s General Election). But it is also likely that the media may merely follow what they feel are popular trends and changing political and social views among the public, rather than themselves being the instigators of new ideas.

Many people reflect a popular cultural tradition in Britain and have learned to read between the lines of the media and are conditioned early in life ‘not to believe everything you read in the papers’, or hear ‘on the telly’. Since television in particular is often accused of being either right-wing or left-wing, depending on which government is in power, it would seem that the British people are receiving enough information of varying quality from all sides of the political spectrum. In practice, most people seem to make up their own minds, object to having politics and other concerns ‘thrust down their throats’ and many take a tired (and often cynical) attitude to such matters.

PLATE 11.4 Photographers and the press waiting outside the Lindo Wing of St Mary’s Hospital, London after the Duchess of Cambridge gave birth to Prince George on 23 July 2013. © Peter Macdiarmid/Getty Images

However, opinion polls in recent years have shown widespread distrust of the British media despite a BBC/Reuters/Media Center poll in 2006 that trust in the media had increased in Britain from 29 per cent to 47 per cent in the period 2002–06. Instead, Reuters Institute in June 2017 found that only 41 per cent of respondents agreed that the British news media did a good job in helping them distinguish fact from fiction, and communication agency Edelman’s 2017 Trust Barometer of 1,500 Britons found that only 24 per cent of respondents trusted British news outlets at all. The US Pew Research Centre reported in 2018 that the UK news media were the least trusted among eight European nations to ‘get the facts right’ and ‘cover important stories of the day’. The question of trust in the mainstream media has become very important in Britain, with some defending it and others criticizing its actions.

Write short essays on the following topics:

1.  Describe and comment critically on the structure of British broadcasting.

1.  Examine the problems of media freedom of expression.

1.  Discuss the division of British national newspapers into ‘populars’ and ‘qualities’.
