# 12

## Leisure, sport and the arts

- Leisure activities
- Sport
- The arts
- _Exercises_
- _Further reading_
- _Websites_

The preceding chapters have examined institutional building blocks and their distinctive cultures, which comprise British civilization. This chapter considers how leisure pastimes, sports and the arts in Britain are represented; how they contribute to a whole way of life; and how they are funded by state, voluntary and private contributions, and through their own profits.

The diversity of life in contemporary Britain is reflected in the ways the British organize such activities. They reveal different cultural habits, rather than one unified image, and are divided between participatory and spectator pastimes, which show varying degrees of active and passive engagement. They are also associated with national identities, iconic institutions, mass participation and minority interests. They may be influenced by social class, government financial aid, a changing national economy, personal disposable income, the expense of activities and equipment, and the pressures on leisure time in a work-oriented society.

According to the authors of a poll-based book (Jacobs and Worcester, _We British_, Weidenfield & Nicolson 1990: 124), ‘the variety of available leisure, arts and sporting activities disproves the criticism that Britain is a country of philistines who prefer second-rate entertainment to the best’. Yet there have been many recent complaints about a ‘dumbing-down’ of British cultural life in television programmes, films, the arts, literature, music and education; declining standards in sports and physical exercise, which have been connected to an increase in adult and child obesity; and decreased participation in and attendance at leisure events. Such criticism and observations are not new. They were also common in the nineteenth century when the working and middle classes were expanding rapidly and contrasts between high and low cultures were commented upon by critics, writers and artists.

Although public opinion polls in the 1990s and early 2000s indicated that Britain’s leisure industries were relatively healthy, a financial crisis and recession in 2007–10 seriously affected many individuals and businesses. People reduced their free-time activities because of stagnant wages; stayed at home rather than going out to socialize in pubs, bars and restaurants; ‘staycations’ (larger numbers of the population holidaying in Britain rather than abroad) became popular; consumer purchases of home entertainment electronic equipment, newspapers and magazines declined because of their cost; there was less personal disposable income; and many people budgeted more carefully. While the national economy had improved by 2017, individuals still experienced financial difficulties and low wages.

Nevertheless, the authors of _We British_ (1990: 133), reached conclusions about Britain’s cultural life 29 years ago, which are still arguably valid in large part today, although some observers of British life might disagree:

\[w\]e can report that the nation is in no telly-induced trance. Its tastes mix watching and doing, ‘high’ and ‘low’ cultures, with a richness that contradicts the stereotypes of the British as divided between mindless lager louts and equally money-grubbing consumers. The mix we have found will not please everybody. Not enough football for some, not enough opera for others. But that is what we should expect in the culture of a whole nation.

Evolving social pursuits and pastimes have implications for the larger society. Much leisure time in Britain was historically spent within the family home and there was a separation of individuals from the wider social context. Gradually, as the population grew, there was greater mixing between individuals outside the family. Although some home activities still remain important, many pastimes now involve developed technology and commercialized businesses, which are part of a larger public market in which producers try to attract and maximize consumers and sales. But this development has also led partly to more individual activities being enjoyed by young people within the home and its private spaces.

Access to leisure, sports and arts activities is unevenly distributed in the population, because it is dependent upon purchasing power, opportunity and availability. Exclusionary factors operate against some individuals and groups, such as lack of disposable income, the influence of class, disadvantaged backgrounds, inadequate knowledge of available activities, poor (or non-existent) musical and arts teaching in schools, cost of equipment, the price of tickets, shortage of time in busy lives, and fewer resources. This fragmentation ironically can create its own diverse and alternative forms of culture which, as in the past, may become mainstream.

The distribution of leisure, sports and the arts have recently appeared in discussions on how social ‘culture’ or ‘capital’ is defined and applied. The creative industries that service the ‘leisure market’ (such as cinema, theatre, publishing, music and the performing arts) are an important part of Britain’s social and economic life. They generate substantial amounts of revenue and export earnings which contribute to the gross domestic product (GDP), employ many people and have an influential value at home and abroad. Yet, participation in them is decreasing as income declines and entertainment venues close. Questions arise as to how and why some sectors of society benefit from cultural processes and others do not, and how finance and support may be raised for leisure, sports and arts activities.

For example, the UK government Department for Digital, Culture, Media and Sports (DCMS), created in 1992, sponsors cultural, sporting, tourism, creative and leisure industries in Britain. It provides financial aid (from central taxation) to museums, galleries and libraries; tourism: historic buildings and the arts in, for example, England through Arts Council England; and grants to local government for spending locally. Culture and sports finance are also given by central government to the devolved areas of Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland for internal distribution. Sometimes this aid can be controversial and criticized. Other voluntary and private organizations, such as the National Lottery, commercial companies, pressure groups and individuals provide substantial funding to leisure activities, sports and the arts, without which many of them would find it difficult to survive.

However, the DCMS also suffered from the 2007–10 financial recession and cuts were made to subsequent budgets up to 2016. While the cuts were sometimes smaller than expected, they were significant, especially for local government councils and regional arts groups which lost part of their public funding.

In this financially limited situation, critics ask whether state or public financial subsidization of ‘the arts’ and wider cultural activities is justified. This often leads to questions about the meaning of culture; who decides what culture is, and whether it ultimately depends on what individuals, rather than official institutions, consider to be culture? Such debates continue in Britain. ‘Culture’, for example, is sometimes defined anthropologically as ‘a whole way of life’ in which different forms react to each other, may share common values, or create their own modes of expression.

##### Leisure activities

Leisure activities in the country’s pre-industrial period, apart from some high cultural interests exclusive to the urban, metropolitan elite, were for most of the population largely conditioned by the rural and agricultural nature of British life. Settlements were isolated, and transport and roads were either poor or non-existent. People were restricted to their homes and villages and obliged to create their own entertainments. Some were home or family-based, while larger participatory and spectator activities, such as sports and seasonal events, were enjoyed by the larger community. They might also involve itinerant players, who travelled the countryside and provided alternative entertainments, such as drama and musical performances.

Improved transport and road conditions from the eighteenth century onwards enabled the rural population to travel to neighbouring towns where they took advantage of a variety of amusements and wider social opportunities. Spectator and participatory activities increased with the industrialization of the nineteenth century, as more of the population moved to the towns and cities

and as theatres, concerts, music halls and sports developed and became available to more people. The establishment of road and railway systems and the formation of bus and train companies initiated the pattern of cheap one-day trips around the country and particularly family holidays at the seaside, which were to grow into the mass charter and package tours of contemporary Britain. The arrival of radio, films and television in the early twentieth century resulted in a hugely expanded professional entertainments industry serviced by new theatres and cinemas, with the emerging BBC radio and television channels becoming a fulcrum for the whole family. The variety of offerings and levels of participation increased again dramatically from the 1960s and coincided with more leisure time, greater disposable income, the weakening of ‘high’ and ‘low’ cultural barriers and an explosion of ‘popular culture’, particularly in music, theatre and literature. This was followed by a robust expression of ‘youth culture’ focusing on music, crafts, art, theatre, fashion, photography and digital developments in the late twentieth and early twenty-first centuries. In all these changes, the mixture of participatory, spectator, home-based, individual and commercial leisure activities and traditions have continued.

Many contemporary pursuits have their roots in the cultural and social behaviour of the past, including boxing, wrestling, cricket, football and a range of other sports. Dancing, amateur theatre and musical events were focal parts of rural life for all classes and were often associated with the changing agricultural seasons. The traditions of hunting, shooting and fishing have long been practised in British country life (not only by the aristocracy), as have blood sports, such as dog and cock fighting and bear baiting, which are now illegal. However, organized betting on illegal dog and other animal fights does continue today in secluded locations.

A feature of contemporary Britain is the continuing attempt to stop some kinds of rural activities such as fishing and fox hunting, as well as culls (organized, humane killing) of deer and badgers. Activists have become more assertive in their objections to and campaigns against what they see as the cruelty of many rural traditions and the containment of the animal population, as well as in their opposition to the use of animals in commercial and medical experiments. For example, a MORI poll in 1997 showed that two-thirds of respondents favoured a complete ban on fox hunting with horses and dogs. Although the countryside lobby opposed such action, fox hunting was banned first in Scotland and then in England and Wales in 2004. Later polls found that support for the fox-hunting ban had fallen by a half, with an increase in respondents who said they were neutral on the issue. Apathy about fox hunting has grown and support to overturn the ban appeared to have increased with the alleged need to control the growth of the fox population. However, a MORI poll in December 2017 commissioned by the animal welfare charity the League Against Cruel Sports reported that 85 per cent of respondents thought that fox hunting should not be made legal again.

Other results opposed deer hunting (87 per cent), hare hunting and coursing (90 per cent), dog fighting (99 per cent) and badger baiting (94 per cent).

The British today enjoy a variety of cultural, sporting and leisure activities and more people have more free time. The growing problem is how to organize the work/life balance. Most workers have at least four weeks’ holiday a year, in addition to public breaks such as Christmas, Easter and bank holidays (days off work), although Britain has fewer such holidays than other European countries. The growing number of pensioners (some of whom are reasonably affluent) has created an economically rewarding leisure market which benefits both the elderly and service companies. Unemployment also means that some groups of people have more enforced free time (if not always the finances to enjoy it in full).

Consumer patterns and leisure activities have also changed and coincided with part-time and shift working and higher incomes for many of those in work. There is a demand for public facilities such as cinemas, shops, supermarkets, restaurants and leisure services to be open and available for longer, more convenient, periods during the week and at weekends. But such activities can suffer when there is an economic downturn and a lack of income to generate expansion. The 2007–10 recession seriously restricted Britons’ ability to enjoy and afford leisure opportunities, and visits to pubs and restaurants are still in decline. But parts of the economy have slowly improved, and despite wage stagnation, the most popular pastimes remain social or home-based (see Table 12.1). These

activities reflect relatively consistent and continuing patterns of behaviour.

| 5   | Eating out at restaurants | 76  |
| 6   | Internet/emailing | 75  |
| 7   | Days out or visits to places | 73  |
| 8   | Reading | 72  |
| 9   | Sport/exercise | 59  |
| 10  | Going to the cinema | 53  |

| 12  | Gardening | 50  |
| 13  | Theatre/music concerts | 43  |
| 14  | Visiting historic sites | 41  |
| 15  | DIY (do-it-yourself) | 39  |
| 16  | Visiting museums/galleries | 37  |
| 17  | Playing computer games | 28  |
| 18  | Arts and crafts | 23  |
| 19  | Playing a musical instrument | 9   |
| _Source_: Adapted from Department for Culture, Media and Sport – Taking Part – Statistical Release 2017. |     |     |

The Office for National Statistics (ONS) 2017, found that the majority of leisure time for both men and women was spent on mass media such as watching TV or listening to music. But men spent more time on sports, hobbies and computer games than women, who were more attracted to social activities. The over-65s enjoyed more leisure time than 25–34-year olds. People in skilled professions spent the least time on leisure, compared with those in sales and customer services. Leisure activities were highest for those in the south-east of England and lowest for those in Northern Ireland.

ONS statistics on family household expenses and living costs showed that the third highest category of spending was recreation and culture. Historically, there had been increasing rates of family expenditure from 1977 until the credit crisis in 2006, but spending patterns changed over time, because of greater disposable income in some periods and rising costs in others.

A 2013 ONS list of weekly recreation and cultural expenditure included, in descending cost, package holidays, UK and abroad (35 per cent); recreational and cultural services, such as sports admissions, leisure class fees, cinema, theatre and museum tickets, TV licences and subscriptions, and equipment hire (29 per cent); recreational items and equipment (16 per cent); newspapers, books and stationery (8 per cent); audio-visual, photographic and information

PLATE 12.1 Drinking outside the White Horse pub, Soho, London. Soho has long been a popular area for pubs, clubs, exotic entertainment, restaurants and theatre which have appealed to a wide variety of people. Its dubious image has often been bohemian, eccentric and raffish. © Kevin Foy/Shutterstock

processing equipment (8 per cent); and recreation and culture durables, such as television sets, computers and other electronic equipment (3 per cent).

Despite recession and other socioeconomic problems such as inequality of income and status, the 2013 ONS poll indicated that Britain overall is a relatively affluent society and spends a significant part of personal income on leisure activities. More Britons participate in a wide variety of available pastimes, sometimes with surprising priorities. For example, despite the effects and influence of the digital age, reading, although declining in recent years, compares favourably with the most popular habits such as watching television or playing music.

However, there have been significant leisure changes in recent years. According to FCOM statistics 2018, spending by main television channels such as the BBC, ITV, Channel 4 and Channel 5 on new UK-made TV programmes fell to a 20-year low and indicated that people are watching less television. Average daily broadcast viewing of television decreased by 9 minutes in 2017 and by 38 minutes since 2012. In 2018, according to [finder.com,](http://finder.com/) the figure was 22 hours a week, not including streaming services. But more than 10 million homes have subscription view-on-demand services such as Netflix, Amazon Prime and Now TV.

These figures illustrate the competitive shift within the UK television industry due to the rise of global Internet media companies and to the changing habits and preferences of UK audiences. More choice for viewers has led to competition for viewers in an increasingly fragmented TV market. While broadcast TV has declined, but remains popular among some groups, viewing among 16–34-year-olds is moving online and streaming services subscriptions are bigger than traditional pay television. This marks a major shift in the UK’s leisure and viewing habits. In 2018, 28.3 million homes in the UK had a television set.

Watching television is still a common leisure activity, whether individually or among family and friends and is a popular activity for age groups from 35 upwards. The continuing attraction of television as a popular entertainment medium may be due to the greater quality production offered by digital television after the transfer from analogue in October 2012.This gives a greater choice of channels, interactive alternatives, DVDs, streaming and high definition (HD) wide screens. But teenagers and young adults aged 15–24 are the most frequent cinema goers, although as a first choice the great majority listen to music.

Polls show, as expected, differences in age group usage when comparing the 16–24 age group with those aged 65 and over. Certain activities are cited less frequently as age increases, such as listening to music; Internet usage and emailing; sport and exercise; going out to pubs, clubs and bars; and going to the cinema.

The British spend some two-thirds of their spare time using electronic equipment. A large amount of money is spent on items such as television

sets, subscriptions to satellite television and cable television, radio equipment, videos, computers, DVD equipment and DVDs (although the latter are going out of fashion). There were 48.8 million Internet users in 2009 and 58 per cent of adults had accessed it at some time. By 2010, increasing numbers of homes and businesses were using high-speed broadband fibre optic connections and more people were using the Internet in preference to television, but many people still do not have adequate broadband access. Film streaming on the Internet via companies such as Netflix and Spotify is becoming increasingly popular, as well as catch-up services such as the BBC’s iPlayer, although some prefer to watch on a television screen rather than phones or computers.

In these examples, the home is still the chief place for family and individual entertainment and poses competition to other activities outside the home, such as the cinema, sports and theatre. Leisure activities for both males and females exclusively within the home include listening to the radio or music; watching television; studying; reading books and newspapers; relaxing; conversation; entertaining; hobbies; computer and tablet use; gaming; and texting.

Despite the competition from television, the cinema, electronic media and busy lives, reading still appears to be an important leisure activity for some people of all ages. It came eighth in a list of 19 with 72 per cent of all people over 16 in Britain in the 2017 DCMS survey, which was a small decline from previous years. Other polls, such as the Booktrust charity, indicate that while large minorities of people have negative attitudes towards reading, substantial sections of the population are more positive.

There is a large variety of books and magazines in all fields to cater for all tastes and interests. In 2016, according to Statista, British book sales improved to 195 million and sales amounted to an increase of 74.8 billion (Publishers Association). Exports were strong, particularly with academic and research publishers, and the biggest export market is Europe. But e-books continued to decline. The best-selling books are romances, thrillers, modern popular novels, detective stories, science fiction, children’s books and works of adventure and history. Classic literature is not widely read, although sales of older novels can still benefit from adaptations on television. The tie-in of books (of all types) with videos, DVDs and television series can be lucrative, although the DVD market is declining. Book publishing in the UK is in transition with some physical book sales and audio readers increasing and other categories decreasing.

Do-it-yourself hobbies (DIY), such as house painting, decorating and gardening, were once very popular and home repairs and improvements amounted to a large item in the total household budget. But there was a big decrease in 2015, with big national retail supply shops closing down as people buy and shop online. Until the recession, the number of restaurants had increased and the practice of eating out was popular, with expenditure on restaurants and hotels being a significant part of the household budget. But fashions and tastes have changed

and many restaurants have closed in recent years. This has been attributed to wage stagnation and a decreasing interest in restaurant culture. However, there are a variety of ‘ethnic’ restaurants (particularly Indian, Chinese, Italian and French) in British high streets and fast-food outlets serving pizza, hamburgers, kebabs, chicken and fish and chips. The quality of food in British restaurants is still very variable, although many have improved, and the variety and number of available cuisines has increased. Chefs have gained television exposure and raised an awareness of many varieties of food among the general public.

Visiting the pub is still a significant part of British life and leisure. Some seven out of ten adults visit pubs and one-third go once or more a week. But

PLATE 12.2 The Eagle and Child pub, St Giles, Oxford, also known as the Bird and Baby or simply the Bird.

Popular with many people in Oxford, including C.S. Lewis,

J.R.R. Tolkien and other writers (known as the Inklings). © Amy Welmers

the pub, as a social institution, has changed over the years, although it still caters for a wide range of different groups and tastes. The pub is said to be Britain’s most envied and imperfectly imitated institution, where people can gather on neutral ground and socialize on their own terms. However, according to the British Beer and Bar Association (2016), falling customer numbers and alcohol consumption, the recession, the ban on smoking in public places in 2005, rising property prices, takeovers by chain ownership and restaurants, high taxes on beer, the increasing cost of food and drink and a concern with trendiness and quick profits have led to a decline in Britain’s unique pub heritage. More people are also drinking at home and buying more alcohol (27 per cent) from supermarkets, off licences (off trade) and shops, as prices in pubs have risen steadily. The UK’s 50,000 pubs (2016) had been depleted each week with a loss of 10,500 (17 per cent) since 2000, although the decline now seems to be slowing down. Many buildings have been turned into gastropubs, clubs or bars in an attempt to emulate ‘café society’. Others became large spaces for vertical drinking with little of the traditional pub ambience and craft beers have become more popular. However, adults are drinking less often and in 2016, ONS statistics reported that only 57 per cent of respondents said that they drank alcohol in the week before being interviewed.

Pub licensing hours, which apply to opening times for the sale of alcohol, were liberalized in 2005 and pubs can open for extended drinking hours in the evening or into the early morning. Pubs may provide food in addition to drinks, and some, in more prosperous urban and rural areas, have restaurants attached. In recent years, there has been a mushrooming of bars and clubs with extended opening hours. The growth and popularity of the club scene with its music, drink and appeal to the young, offers considerable competition to the traditional pub trade, although people may move between different venues during an evening. Such developments (together with extended pub opening times and the availability of cheap alcohol) has also led to a ‘binge-drinking’ (drinking to excess) epidemic in city and town centres and an upsurge in anti-social behaviour on the streets, which has caused great concern and provoked calls for the system of licensing to be tightened. However, binge drinking decreased by 2014, arguably due to a reduction in disposable income among the young as a

result of the 2007–10 recession.

British nightlife for most young people is varied and lively, with nightclubs, large-scale rock gigs at arenas and sports grounds, music festivals (such as Glastonbury with top-lining bands) and outdoor ‘raves’ with a variety of acts and DJs. Use of so-called recreational drugs such as cannabis, ecstasy (despite well-publicized deaths from its usage) and cocaine has become so widespread that there have been calls for decriminalization of some hard and soft drugs. There is also a thriving and developed lesbian and gay (LGBT) club scene.

Holidays (location and duration) are often the largest part of British residents’ leisure plans. The choice is conditioned by their amount of free time

PLATE 12.3 The resort of Benidorm on Spain’s Costa Blanca has long been a favourite destination for British tourists. It helped to open up mass tourism and the package holiday phenomenon to the British. © Geoffrey Robinson/Shutterstock

and, until the recession led to stagnant wages for many workers, the cost. ABTA travel specialists in 2017 found that 13 per cent of British people took no holidays at all; 30 per cent spent their holidays only in the UK; 15 per cent travelled only abroad; and 42 per cent combined UK and overseas visits. ONS figures (Table 12.2) reported that in 2016 there were 70.8 million visits overseas by UK residents (an increase of 8 per cent since 2015). They spent 743.8 billion on overseas visits in 2016, 12 per cent more than in 2015. The first five countries in the table amounted to 49 per cent of all UK visits abroad and 46 per cent of total spend. It is ironic that so many Britons chose to spend their holidays in Europe when a majority later voted to leave the EU. Wide restrictions on travel within Europe may apply on exit.

Many European tourists also chose to visit the UK. At a time of increased reciprocal tourism, there were 37.6 million visits by overseas residents to the UK in 2016, 4 per cent more than in 2015. They spent 722.5 billion on their UK visits in 2016, 2 per cent more than in 2015 (Table 12.3). London attracted 19.1 million overnight visits in 2016, but other cities outside London also received many visitors, such as Edinburgh (1.7 million), Manchester

(1.2 million) and Birmingham (1.1 million). Travel restrictions may also apply to Europeans after Brexit.

Holidays regularly account for the first major leisure choice in Britain. But a declining number of Britons take their holidays in the UK itself, where the south-west English coastal resorts and Scotland are very popular in summer. But larger numbers now also go abroad in winter or summer or both, and the great days of the British seaside resorts have declined. According to ONS and The International Passenger Survey, the number of long holidays taken abroad by the British decreased in the recession by 15 per cent from 45.5 million visits in 2008 to 38.5 million in 2009, the lowest since 2000. Spain, France, Italy,

Portugal and Greece tend to be the main attractions for these holidaymakers, who buy relatively cheap package tours. Yet the British seem to have become more adventurous and 25 per cent now travel widely outside Europe to Asia, Africa, South America and the US on a variety of holidays. Large numbers of UK residents also travel to visit relatives and friends worldwide.

The 2007–10 recession altered these patterns to some degree, with more people taking their holidays in Britain. Domestic holiday tourism grew by 12 per cent from 75 million trips visits within the UK in 2008 to 84 million in 2009. Holiday visits abroad decreased by 15 per cent from 45.5 million visits in 2008 to 38.5 million in 2009. The pattern of overseas travel for Britons has recovered in recent years, with Spain remaining the most popular destination for UK residents going abroad on holiday. The Irish Republic is a popular destination for British residents travelling overseas to visit friends and relatives. UK residents aged 45 to 54 tend to take the most holiday visits abroad. It remains to be seen whether Britain’s exit from the EU will seriously affect travel plans in Europe.

In more normal times, many British people prefer to organize their own holidays and make use of the good air and sea communications between Britain and the continent. In Britain itself, different forms of traditional holiday still exist, from ‘bed and breakfast’ at a seaside boarding house, to hotels of varying quality, caravan sites and camping (the latter at 10 per cent of total holidays) or increasingly themed, upmarket and trendy stays at spas and expensive hotels. Increased car ownership has allowed greater travel possibilities, with day trips in Britain for example amounting to 51 per cent of total holidays in 2009. Today, more than seven out of ten households have the use of at least one car and 27 per cent have two or more.

##### Sport

There is a wide variety of sports in contemporary Britain, which cater for large numbers of spectators and active participants (the latter at different levels of ability and organization). Polling organizations, such as Mintel, further divide these into individual/keep-ft sports (e.g. gym work and swimming); team/ competitive sports (e.g. football and cricket); racquet sports played with an implement (e.g. tennis and squash); and outdoor/alternative sport (e.g. hiking and fishing ). Some of these sports have distinctive origins and were historically minority, exclusive or class-based (such as yachting and the contrasting codes of rugby union and rugby league), while others appeal now to majority tastes (like football and swimming). The number of people actively participating in sports increased in the 1990s. This coincided with a greater awareness of health needs and the importance of exercise, particularly when many Britons were overweight or obese. Weight remains an issue and fitness campaigns attempt to

increase an awareness of the benefits of health and exercise for all age groups. However, some polls such as Mintel, suggest that while playing sport is widely supported at both participatory and spectator levels, individual fitness-focused sports are more favoured than team or outdoor alternatives. It is argued that the sports participation market in 2016 consisted of those regular participants who contributed most of the 73.9 billion estimated as being spent on taking part, but was having difficult y in retaining players with more casual interests.

Expenditure on playing and watching sports, and buying sports equipment, amounts to a considerable part of household and individual budgets. Yet it is argued that Britain has inadequate sporting facilities and leisure centres at reasonable prices in both the public and private sectors and that sports participation and exercise has declined in recent years. It is recognized that the creation of more facilities can lead to greater national sporting interest and success, such as the achievements of the British cycling team at the Beijing Olympics (2008), the awarding of the 2012 Olympic Games to London, and the performances of British athletes in the capital’s Games. However, the legacy of the latter has not been as great as had been anticipated in terms of increased income and the encouragement of participation in sport. But reasonable success was achieved at the Rio Olympics in 2016 and at later Commonwealth Games and European and World Championships.

Surveys on sports and leisure participation in Britain reveal different emphases and contrasting results because some deal with the whole UK and others with the individual nations. For example, in June 2016, a survey from _Sport England_ found that the number of people aged 16 and over playing sport or engaging in exercise had fallen since the 2012 London Olympics and

15.8 million people play sport or exercise at least once a week, a drop of 0.4 per cent since 2012. However, this was still 1.7 million more people participating in sport than in 2005. The biggest decline was among people from ethnic minorities and economically deprived groups and five of the nine English regions recorded declines.

Sport England’s _Active Lives Survey_, October 2017, listed the top 15 participatory sports activities by English adults (aged 16+) at least twice in the preceding 28 days. These were running/jogging with 15 per cent of respondents, followed by fitness classes (14 per cent), gym sessions (12 per cent), swimming (11 per cent), exercise machines (9 per cent), climbing or mountaineering (5 per cent), interval sessions (5 per cent), football (5 per cent), weights sessions (5 per cent), generic fitness training (5 per cent), golf (2 per cent), badminton (2 per cent), tennis (2 per cent), rowing (2 per cent) and boxing (2 per cent). Swimming had dropped from first place and football from third place in the 2012 list, and the overall figures showed that of 29 sports, which ranged from majority to minority interests, nine had an increase and 20 suffered a decline. There are also other individual keep-ft sports, such as cycling, walking, jogging, aerobics, yoga and Pilates.

The above figures produced examples of physical activity levels for England in 2017. The inactive (30 minutes a week) amounted to 26 per cent of the total population; the fairly active (20–149 minutes a week) to 14 per cent; and the active (150 minutes a week) to 61 per cent. Somewhat different analyses were followed for Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland.

Various reasons are given for this apparent drop in participation. It is argued that the government has failed to deliver on the 2012 Olympic legacy through lack of planning, strategy and funding; people cannot afford or do not have the time for sport; there are insufficient resources for local government to repair facilities or to provide new ones; minority sports are ignored or not encouraged; and the negative effects of the 2007–10 recession continue with cuts to budgets, the closure of local government facilities and reduced personal incomes. A further issue is the use of illegal drugs which has tainted many sports and led to the banning of sports people.

The individual types of outdoor and indoor sports or exercise have also changed compared with earlier years, together with their popularity. Opinion polls in the early 2000s suggested that the most popular and accessible participatory activity for both men and women was walking (including rambling and hiking). Billiards/snooker/pool were the next most popular for men, followed by cycling, indoor swimming, football and golf. Keep ft/yoga was the next most popular sport for women, followed by indoor swimming, cycling and snooker/ pool/billiards. Fishing was the most popular country sport for men. Some of these have declined, despite government efforts to increase the exercise rates and to counter obesity. At the beginning of the 2000s, public and private gyms and indoor activities were very popular. But membership has declined because of costs, fees, lack of time for participants and the recession, and local councils often cannot afford the upkeep of existing public resources or the cost of providing new ones.

In addition to their individual keep-ft activities, many British people are also keen followers of amateur and professional team/competitive sports and attend a range of activities. Fans often show a tribal identity or loyalty with their favourite teams or competitive games (see Table 12.4). There is also a range of other popular sports, which attract varying degrees of attendance and participation, such as badminton, netball (women), basketball, volleyball, hockey (ice and field), darts, snooker, boxing, athletics, rowing and hockey.

Polls on sport and exercise participation in the UK suggest that, despite declines, consumers combine traditional sporting pastimes with public and private resources. Some 38 per cent of respondents say that they use existing public leisure centres for a wide range of activities from swimming, tennis and badminton to weights workouts with videos and computer games, while 23 per cent exercise at home; 16 per cent ride a bike (bicycle) outdoors and 14 per cent run or jog outdoors; 9 per cent attend fitness classes (whether public or private); 15 per cent go to private gyms; 6 per cent played team sports (football,

_Source_: adapted from [WorldAtlas.com.](http://WorldAtlas.com/) Updated John Misacki, August 2018. Includes spectator and participatory

1.  Football (soccer)
2.  Rugby football (union/league)
3.  Hockey (ice and field)
4.  Cricket
5.  Speedway
6.  Rounders
7.  Swimming
8.  Tennis
9.  Horse racing
10. Boxing

TABLE 12.4 Most popular sports events (attended) in the UK, 2018

netball and hockey) while 4 per cent enjoyed water sports (surfing, sailing and rowing). Some activities were restricted by low incomes, although others, such as outdoor running, cost little. Interestingly, 6 per cent of adults said that they used a health and fitness mobile app (14 per cent among 16–24s), which may increase greater sports participation rates in future.

A 2018 Eurobarometer report from the European Commission, based on survey data from Kantar Public, suggests that European levels of physical activity were low. In the UK, 13 per cent did so regularly, 34 per cent with some regularity, 16 per cent seldom and 37 per cent never. This latter low figure has appeared frequently in British statistics in recent years at around 30 per cent.

DCMS figures for its _Taking Part_ survey 2009–10 reported that 53.4 per cent of adults in England participated in active sport at moderate intensity for at least 30 minutes a week, which is the recommended amount required for a health benefit from sports activity, and 7 per cent of adults play sport every day. However, the 2009–10 ONS _Social Trends: Lifestyles and Social Participation_ surveys indicated that 40 per cent of UK adults said that lack of time was the main reason for not practising sport. Sports participation varied widely by age, with those aged 16–24 engaging at 75 per cent and those aged over 75 at 18 per cent. In contrast to adults, 55 per cent of children in school years 1–13 in England participated in at least three hours of quality school physical education (PE) and out-of-school sport in a typical week. There was no significant variation in participation due to deprivation between regions, or between rural and urban areas.

Active sport covers a wide range of activities in which participation has varied as has the number of spectators at amateur and professional sporting events over time. In 2007, _British Social Attitudes_ reported that 49 per cent of adults over 18 had attended a sports event at least once a year as a spectator,

PLATE 12.4 Football Association Cup Final at Wembley Stadium, 19 May 2018. Eden Hazard scoring winning goal (a penalty) for Chelsea (1) against Manchester United (0).

© Chris Brunskill Ltd/Getty Images

but that a lower proportion attended on a more regular basis. Today, it is argued that attendance is dropping because modern professional sports are too expensive for ordinary spectators to attend; that the cost and time demands of family-supported and amateur team sports are too great; and that fans are often inadequately treated by professional organizations and clubs. Nevertheless, active sport covers a wide range of participatory activities, as diverse as swimming, karate, netball, canoeing, roller-blading and curling. For children, the list of activities within sport reflects the many different types of activities appropriate for the 5–10 and 11–15 age groups. A feature in recent years has been the emergence of women’s teams in all major amateur and professional sports, which attract considerable spectator support and perform in international competition.

###### Football (Soccer)

Football (soccer) is played at amateur and professional level throughout most of the year and also at international level. It is a very popular sport in Britain and today transcends its earlier working-class origins. The professional game developed into a large, family-oriented organization, but has suffered from stadium tragedies, crowd violence, high ticket prices, declining attendances and financial

crises. However, enforced changes in recent years such as all-seater stadiums, greater security, improved facilities and lucrative tie-ins with television coverage (such as Sky Sports) have improved this situation. Yet, team sports still suffer from outbreaks of racism and hooliganism. Many of the top professional football clubs in the English Premier League have become public companies quoted on the Stock Exchange, have foreign owners and overseas players, and football is now big business, in which, critics argue, money and the ability to win big competitions are deciding factors, particularly in the Premiership. It is also argued that these structures have hindered the advance of young English players as most top teams consist largely of overseas players.

There is a widening gulf between the top clubs (such as Chelsea, Arsenal, Manchester City, Liverpool and Manchester United) and others in the lower divisions and the bottom half of the Premier League. Some 80 per cent of England’s soccer clubs lose money despite television income, which goes largely to the 20 clubs in the Premiership. Most football clubs (even at the top end of the Premiership) are in a precarious financial position, with only a few making a profit, many losing control over their costs, suffering accumulating debt and some going into administration (a form of bankruptcy). It is argued that this

PLATE 12.5 Wembley Stadium has been the home of English football since 1923 and a new stadium built on the foundations of the old was opened in Spring 2007. It holds 90,000 spectators and hosts other events such as rugby, American football and pop concerts. Some fans prefer the old ground to the new one. © Charles Bowman/Robert Harding/Shutterstock

situation is due to poor club organization, bad business decisions, huge salaries for players, inflated transfer fees, fans deserting their clubs because of high ticket prices and a recent lack of quality and success on the pitch in Britain and in European and World cup competitions. Football such as the English team’s improved performance in the 2018 World Cup in Russia brings European and world competition and huge financial rewards, but failure results in serious financial loss. Women’s football has been successful at club and international levels and is increasingly influential and popular, but suffers from a lack of financial input and support.

###### Rugby

Rugby football is popular and is widely watched and played, with two forms of the sport being played. Rugby union was once confined to amateur clubs and was an exclusively middle-class and public-school-influenced game, but it became professional in 1995 (at least for the top clubs), now covers a wider social spectrum and received a boost when England won the World Cup in 2003. England’s fortunes and performance have varied since then, and Scotland, Wales and Ireland have also had their ups and downs. Rugby league is played by professional teams, mainly in the north of England and still tends to be a working-class sport in terms of participation and support. Both types of rugby are also played internationally.

PLATE 12.6 A traditional English cricket match between two villages, Chagford and Feniton, at Chagford, Devon, June 2008. © Christopher Jones/Shutterstock

###### Cricket

Cricket is a summer sport in Britain, but the England team also plays in the winter months in Commonwealth countries. It is now both an amateur and professional game, having started as largely amateur. The senior game is professional and is largely confined to the English county sides (and one Welsh county side) which play in the County Championship. Attendance at the traditional long three-day county cricket matches continues to decline, although one-day 20–20 games attract large numbers of spectators and substantial financial backing. It is felt that the contemporary game has lost some of its attractiveness as it has moved in overly professional and money-dominated directions. It was in danger of becoming a minority sport. But it regained popularity in the summer of 2005 when England beat the visiting Australians in the Ashes Test Match series and the short game also achieved success internationally, with England becoming 20–20 world champions in 2010. Since then, English Test Match cricket, particularly against the Australians in Australia in 2014, and 20–20 matches have varied between considerable success and embarrassing failures.

###### Other sports

There are many other indoor and outdoor sports that reflect the diversity of traditional interests in British life. Among these are golf, horse racing, hunting, riding, fishing, shooting, hockey, bowls, darts, snooker, sailing, mountaineering, walking, ice sports, motorcar and motorcycle racing and rally driving. American football and basketball are increasingly popular because of television exposure and are also played in Britain. These sports may be either amateur or professional, and spectator or participator-based, with car and motorcycle competitions, greyhound racing and horse racing being the most watched on television, at racecourses and on the few remaining motor circuits.

PLATE 12.7 Surfing on the Cornish coast (here Polzeath, Hayle Bay) has attracted growing numbers of participants, nationally and from abroad. © David Hughes/Robert Harding/ Shutterstock

###### Gambling

The professional sporting industry is now very lucrative, and closely associated with sponsorship schemes, television income, brand merchandizing and sports sales. Gambling or betting on sporting and other events has always been a popular, if somewhat disreputable, pastime in Britain, and is now much more in the open, if not totally acceptable. But there have been recent fraud cases and addiction abuse in the gambling industry, with calls for greater control and cash limits. Most gambling (through betting shops or bookmakers on the High Street and online) is associated with horse and greyhound racing but can involve other sports, such as football. Weekly football pools (betting on match results) are popular and can result in large financial wins, although the traditional appeal and involvement have diminished.

The partial acceptability of gambling in Britain was reflected in the establishment of a National Lottery in 1994. It is similar to lotteries in other countries and considerable amounts of money can be won. Some of its income has funded artistic, community, charitable, leisure and sports activities, which are in need of finance to survive. It also helped to finance the London Olympics in 2012 and to support elite athletes. But falling ticket sales and profits have recently meant that the lottery could no longer guarantee financial support for all its ‘good causes’.

A 2016 report for the Gambling Commission found that 45 per cent of people had participated in some form of gambling in 2015; 15 per cent gambled online; 97 per cent of online gamblers gambled at home; 33 per cent used a mobile phone or tablet; and 25 per cent had bet in-play. In terms of attitudes and problems, 39 per cent of respondents felt that gambling is fair and can be trusted; 0.7 per cent were identified as problem gamblers; 5.5 were identified as low or moderate risk gamblers; and 40 per cent thought that gambling is associated with crime.

A 2010 report found that in a top list of 61 per cent of men (women 56) gambled on the National Lottery Draw; 25 (25) per cent on an alternative lottery; 22 (25) per cent on scratchcards; 9 (1) per cent on football pools; 6 (11) per cent on Bingo; 16 (10) per cent on slot machines; 7 (2) on fixed odds betting terminals; 21 (11) on horse races; 8 (2) on dog races; 16 (2) on sports betting; 6

(2) on non-sports betting; and 9 (2) on casino games.

The _Guardian_ newspaper reported in 2014 that 55 per cent of the UK population gambled, down from 57 per cent in 2012, but that people aged 18–24 were 5 per cent more likely to gamble than they were a year ago in the country’s 9,128 betting shops. Its analysis also found that northern, urban cities and London boroughs with high levels of unemployment bet four times as much on gambling machines than richer rural areas in southern England with lower unemployment. The previous Labour government had also controversially opened up the possibility for more casinos in some big cities, although

there has been little progress due to local and national protests. Local government is now able to limit the winnings on gambling machines because of increased addiction to the games. But cases of acute addiction have resulted in demands for more restrictions.

###### Sporting institutions

Many sports have contributed to institutionalized and iconic features of British life and provide a certain degree of national identity. For example, Wimbledon is tennis; the Football Association Cup Final is football in England (at Wembley Stadium); St Andrews is golf in Scotland; the Open Championship is the major golf tournament held annually at golf courses throughout the country; Twickenham in England, Murrayfield in Scotland, and the Millennium Stadium in Cardiff, Wales (known since 2016 for sponsorship purposes as the Principality Stadium) are rugby union; Lords Cricket Ground in London is cricket; the Derby at Epsom is flat horse racing, the Grand National in Liverpool is steeplechasing; Henley Regatta is rowing; Cowes Week off the Isle of Wight is yachting; Royal Ascot is horse racing; and the British Grand Prix is Formula 1 motor racing at Brand’s Hatch and other circuits. Some of these sports may appeal only to certain sections of the population, and others may still be equated with wealth and social position.

Although tobacco sponsorship of most sporting events is now banned, some people feel that the professionalization and commercialization of sport in Britain has tended to weaken what is thought to be the traditional sporting image of the amateur, of fairness, of group loyalty and an emphasis upon playing the game for its own sake. Yet these values still exist to some degree, in spite of greater financial rewards for professional sport, the influences of sponsorship and advertising and more cases of unethical behaviour in all sports. Football, cricket and rugby players, for example, have been accused of abusive and aggressive behaviour on and off the field.

British governments have only recently taken an active political interest in sport. They are now more concerned to promote sport at all levels and there are Ministers for Sport in England, Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland, who are supposed to coordinate sporting activities throughout the country. An earlier Labour government was concerned to improve sporting facilities in Britain by setting up sports councils, colleges, funds and action zones, operating on a regional basis. However, nationally funded provisions for sport in Britain are still inadequate and there is a lack of professional coaches, capital investment and sporting facilities compared with other countries. Local authority sports areas, school sports fields and leisure centres, continue to be sold off, despite a Labour government’s attempt to focus more money on playing fields and open spaces in deprived areas. The ‘legacy’ effect supposedly generated by the 2012 Olympic Games does not seem to have had widespread positive results, except

for specific events such as the Commonwealth Games in Glasgow in 2014 and the Gold Coast in Australia in 2018.

The sporting notion of ‘a healthy mind in a healthy body’ has long been a principle of British education. All schools are supposed to provide physical recreation and a reasonable range of sports is usually, if not always, available for schoolchildren. Schools may play soccer, rugby, field hockey or netball during the winter months, and cricket, tennis, swimming and athletics during the summer. Some schools may be better provided with sporting facilities than others and offer a wider range of activities. There have also been government attempts to increase fitness in children and to reduce childhood obesity, with marginal success.

However, there are complaints from parents that physical education (PE) classes, team games and competitive sports are declining in state schools and that there is a lack of professionally trained PE teachers. School reorganization and the creation of large comprehensives have reduced the amount of interschool competition, which used to be a feature of education; some left-wing councils are opposed to competitive activities; there is a shortage of playing fields for many schools; and a lack of equipment and coaching facilities. The position is acute in inner-city areas, and is of concern to those parents who feel that their children are being prevented from expressing their physical natures. They maintain that the state school system is failing to provide sporting provision for children, with some turning to the independent sector, which is usually well provided with sports facilities. As part of its attempt to make independent schools justify their charitable status, governments insist that they share sporting facilities with local state schools.

Frequent promises are made by governments to provide more financial aid in an attempt to improve the availability and standards of state school sports. Other organizations and voluntary groups also try to address the lack of sporting facilities and recent achievement in Britain by embracing the notion of competition between schoolchildren; bringing through promising children; and creating databases of sporting facilities. Nevertheless, it is estimated that 70 per cent of the most talented youngsters drop out of sport between the ages of 14 and 17, as opposed to the lower numbers in other countries. But better facilities in themselves may not be enough and critics maintain that some schools and local areas have reasonably good provisions already. Others argue that more people of all ages should be encouraged to take up sport and that there should be greater cooperation between schools and local communities in the use of facilities and coaching.

##### The arts

The contemporary British ‘arts’ derive from ancient oral, written, spatial and musical traditions, which have flourished successfully for centuries and have

developed new forms and styles. However, a current debate in Britain concerns the condition of the arts in difficult economic times and whether their funding should come from public taxation, private donors, the income of arts businesses or a mixture of sources.

Some of the arts still have an exclusive image associated with notions of high (‘highbrow’) culture, especially in literature, music, painting, sculpture, crafts, theatre, opera and ballet, which have usually been the province of the urban and privileged metropolitan middle and upper classes and, sometimes, the landed aristocracy. This attitude has lessened to some degree since the Second World War under the impetus of increased educational opportunities and the gradual relaxation of social barriers. The growth of mass and popular culture in many fields has increased the potential audience for a wider and more inclusive range of cultural activities, and the availability and scope of different varieties of the arts have spread to greater numbers of people. These activities may be amateur or professional and continue a mixture of participatory, spectator and home-based entertainment.

It is also suggested that the genuine vitality and innovation of the arts in Britain today derive from the millions of people across the country who are engaged in amateur, and often minority, music, art and theatre, rather than from the professional and commercial arts world. Many towns, suburbs and villages have amateur groups, whether choirs, music sections, orchestras, string quartets, pipe bands, brass bands, choral groups, opera circles or drama/writing clubs. There are also professional arts and cultural festivals held each year throughout Britain, many of which are of a high standard and appeal to diverse tastes. These range from the Glastonbury (pop/rock) Festival to the Glyndebourne Opera Festival in East Sussex.

The funding of the arts in Britain is precarious in both the private and public sectors. The public sector is divided between local authorities and four devolved Arts Councils which receive grants from central authorities and income from the council tax to fund artistic activities in their areas. The amounts vary between different areas of the country, and recipients are criticized for either spending too much or too little on cultural activities. The creative industries themselves, in their many different forms, are a major British industry, which according to the Department for Digital, Culture, Media and Sport (DCMS) in 2017 contributed 792 billion to the British economy, employs about a million people and provides many cultural exports. Some critics argue that the arts should not depend upon subsidies from taxpayers but should themselves support their loss-making fellow artists.

Members of the devolved Arts Councils in England, Scotland, Wales and Northern Ireland are appointed by the Secretary of State for Digital, Culture, Media and Sport. They are responsible for dividing up an annual government grant to the arts and the finance has to be shared among theatres, orchestras, opera and ballet companies, art galleries, museums and other cultural organizations. The division of limited funds has attracted criticism. It means that many

artistic institutions are often dependent upon the private sector and individuals to supply donations, sponsorship and funding to their state and local government money, in order to survive and provide a service. However, cultural organizations, both large and small, such as the Royal Opera House (Covent Garden), museums and village musicians, have also received much-needed finance from the National Lottery.

###### Theatre

British theatre can be lively and innovative and has an international reputation. There are some 300 commercial or professional theatres, in addition to a large number of amateur dramatic clubs, fringe and pub theatres throughout the country. London and its suburbs have about 100 theatres, but the dominant influence is the capital’s ‘West End’. The majority of the West End theatres are commercial in that they are organized for profit and receive no public funds. They provide a range of entertainment offerings from musicals to plays and comedies.

However, some of the other London and regional theatres are subsidized from grants supplied by the Arts Council, such as the National Theatre, the Royal Shakespeare Company (as well as at Stratford-upon-Avon) and the English Stage Company. These offer a variety of plays from the classics to modern drama. The subsidized theatres in both London and the regions regularly ask for more state financial aid, which the government is often loath to give. The government subsidy is considerably less than that given to most comparable theatres in continental Europe. But there is a feeling in some quarters that these theatres should be more competitive and commercially minded like the West End, although Arts Council and local government grants have been cut because of austerity programmes.

Many of the theatres in the regions outside London used to be repertory theatres, which meant that they provided a specific number of classical and innovative plays in a given season, and had a resident theatre company and organization. The repertory companies had traditionally been the training ground for British actors and actresses, which allowed them to learn their craft while working as part of a permanent company. Their numbers have now greatly decreased, many have lost local government subsidies and they may face further decline.

Theatres in London and elsewhere have had difficult times in recent years in attracting audiences and in remaining solvent, although the West End theatres bring considerable finance into the British economy and are popular with tourists. They have had to cope with increased competition from alternative and new entertainment activities. However, commercial theatres in some regional cities are proving popular, offer competition to the remaining repertory theatres and serve a public demand for a broader range of arts offerings, and actors learn

PLATE 12.8 West End commercial theatres on Shaftesbury Avenue, regarded as the centre of London’s theatreland and popular with tourists for the variety of its entertainment venues.

© Alex Segre/ Shutterstock

wider and different skills. They provide a range of innovative entertainment, shows and plays, often prior to a London run.

Opera in Britain occupies a similar position to that of the theatre and is divided into subsidized, commercial and amateur companies. The Royal Opera operates from the refurbished Covent Garden Theatre in London. It provides London seasons and occasional regional tours, while the English National Opera Company supplies distinctive English language and contemporary musical offerings from its base at the London Coliseum. There is a range of other opera companies, both in London and the regions, such as the English Touring Opera, the Welsh National Opera and Scottish Opera. There are also several light opera

groups, and ballet companies such as the Ballet Rambert, the London Festival Ballet, the Scottish Theatre Ballet and the Royal Ballet, the latter of which operates in London and Birmingham. A number of contemporary dance companies have also been formed in recent years, and the high number of different dance forms are very popular.

Britain has many quality orchestras, although most of them are based in London, such as the London Symphony Orchestra, the London Philharmonic and the BBC Symphony Orchestra. There are superb regional symphony orchestras, such as the Hallé in Manchester, the City of Birmingham Symphony, the Ulster Orchestra, the BBC National Orchestra of Wales and the Royal Scottish National Orchestra, along with a number of chamber groups in London and the regions. Most opera, ballet and orchestra activities have their greatest appeal in London and still cater for a minority of the people. More popular forms, such as brass bands, choral singing and light music, once had a large following, but audiences and participation have declined. The more exclusive entertainments are heavily dependent upon Arts Council subsidies, local government grants and private donations, and critics question whether scarce public funding is appropriate for these activities.

Engagement with the arts is mainly reflected in participation or attendance figures, and may involve more than one activity. A DCMS _Taking Part_ survey of adults in England, 2016–17, reported in September 2017 that 77 per cent of respondents had engaged with the arts in one or more forms.

Those defined by _attendance_ of people in England were events such as dance, opera/operetta, ballet, culturally specific festivals, video or electronic art event, jazz performance, classical music concert, street arts, craft exhibition, carnivals, pantomime, public art display, art/photography/sculpture exhibition, play or drama, musicals and other live music events. In terms of live musical tastes outside musicals, operas, classical concerts and jazz performances, DCMS reported the attractions of other styles from rock to choral music (see Table 12.5) in 2015–16. Visits to heritage sites, museums, art galleries and libraries also appeared in the list.

The most popular _participatory_ art forms are crafts such as calligraphy, pottery or jewellery, using a computer to create original artworks or dance, photography as an artistic activity, playing a musical instrument for own pleasure, painting, drawing, printmaking or sculpture, and textile crafts.

The variety of these activities suggest a significant ly vibrant arts scene in Britain, despite decline, lack of interest and financial restraints in some fields, and there are other amateur and regional cultural forms. Responses to the arts can vary over time to reflect cultural changes, personal taste and economic conditions.

Generally, fewer adults in England engage with the arts three or more times a year, compared with more who engaged at least once, and attendance is greater than participation. Nevertheless, the examples of attendance and participatory

|     |     |     |

activity above suggest a positive response to the arts in Britain and a relatively impressive spread of artistic forms.

While attending the cinema as a pastime scored 53 per cent in tenth place in a 2017 DCMS poll, the history of the cinema in Britain has shown a decline since its early days as a very popular form of mass entertainment and from 1946, when total annual visits reached 1.6 billion. The domestic film industry has suffered from a lack of investment and government help, although British films with British actors continued to be made abroad and in Britain with foreign financial backing. Some government, British Film Institute (BFI) and National Lottery finance has now been provided to support British film making. The British film industry still produces highly regarded quality films, although many films made in Britain are either foreign or co-productions.

In 1960 there were over 3,000 cinemas in Britain, but many have now either gone out of business, or changed to other activities such as clubs, dancing and bingo. Annual audience figures dropped from some 501 million in 1960 to 193 million in 1970. This decline was hastened by the arrival of television and continued as new forms of home entertainment, such as videos, DVDs and the Internet, added to the competition, and annual admissions sank to 55 million by 1984.

However, new screens have been built since 1996. According to the UK Cinema Association, there were 801 cinema sites with 4,309 screens situated either in single buildings or in multiplexes in 2017. There was an increase in admissions to 142 million in 2000 and 176 million in 2002 before declining again to 168 million in 2016, down 2 per cent on 2015, according to the BFI. It reported combined box Office film receipts in 2016 of 71.2 billion in the UK, down 1 per cent on 2015.

The top four films released at the UK box Office in 2016 were UK productions, including the top title _Rogue One: A Star Wars Story_ with gross receipts of 766 million. The next three most popular releases were: _Fantastic Beasts and Where to Find Them_ (755 million), _Bridget Jones’s Baby_ (748 million) and _The Jungle Book_ (746 million). Six UK films were in the top 20 films of 2016, down from eight in 2015. One was a British independent film, _Absolutely Fabulous: The Movie_, which was based on a very popular television series.

While admissions dropped in 2016, improved audience figures overall since 1984 had been encouraged by cheaper tickets, a wider range of films (not limited to blockbusters), responses to competition, an increasing appeal to younger people, the provision of an alternative leisure activity within modern surroundings, and developments such as streaming. Bigger audiences were also due partly to the success of British-based films, such as Harry Potter films and smaller independent films. But more than 30 per cent of the population do not go to the cinema, 47 per cent of those aged over 35 never go at all, and audience sizes can vary considerably.

The attraction of British popular music increased in Britain and abroad from the 1960s, and was both an economic and cultural phenomenon. Following the Beatles and Rolling Stones, the domestic market for music sales greatly increased. However, in recent years, critics felt that there was a staleness in the popular field which affected mainstream pop genres, hip-hop, rap, avant-garde and ‘ethnic’ music alike. Some attributed this to commercial manipulation, overly packaged offerings and standardized bands, and others to a lack of substantial and consistent talent or of memorable tunes and songs. Old-guard pop stars complained about the inadequacy of contemporary British pop music with its bland material and ephemeral artists, most of which had difficult y breaking into the global (and particularly the American) market. Members of 1990s bands have also criticized modern pop music as self-absorbed and lacking social commentary.

However, there has been a recent resurgence in British popular and rock music, characterized by single artists, bands and songwriters, in an expanded domestic and international following. The British Phonographic Industry reported that there had been a 9.5 per cent rise in music consumption in 2017 as demand for music in the UK grew and music sales were at their highest for 20 years. Sales of vinyl records rose 26.8 per cent to 4 million; LPs accounted for some 1 in 10 physical purchases; 68.1 billion digital audio streams were sent (over half of UK music consumption); UK acts made up eight of the top-10 best-selling artist albums; and 135.1 million albums or their equivalent were streamed, purchased or downloaded.

This recovery was generated by the stadium tours and radio, and online streaming of artists such as Ed Sheeran, Sam Smith, Stormzy, Adele, Little Mix, Rag’n’Bone Man, Kendrick Lamar and Liam Gallagher according to Performing

Rights Society (PRS) for Music. British songwriters received increased royalty payments in this expanding market as the influence of UK music was boosted overseas. Crucially, payments to artists were strengthened by better business technology after a period of poor or no control. Arrangements were made with royalty collection societies, online revenue was tightened up and streaming services, such as Spotify and Apple Music organized efficient subscription schemes. But PRS reported in 2018 that although its total royalty revenues had increased

12.7 per cent to 7717 million, some music websites were still not compensating artists fairly, and piracy and downloading continue.

Most record sales are pop record albums, sales of classical albums have increased steadily, and vinyl or plastic records have seen significant growth and popularity amounting to 1 in 10 in 2017, but CD and DVD sales have dropped. According to UK Music, the music industry in 2013 contributed a significant total value of 73.8 billion (up by 9 per cent from 73.5 billion in 2012) to the British economy and had total music exports of 72.2 billion or 12.6 per cent of global sales, accounting for one in eight of all artist albums sold. Polls suggest that 81 per cent of Britons between 16 and 24 spend their leisure time listening to music at least once a week. However, the music business remains volatile, accounts for only 16 per cent of the UK entertainment market and some technology companies feel that the industry is not keeping up, financially and technically, with the very varied and challenging structural ways in which music is being used today.

There is a wide range of museums and art galleries in Britain (1,860), which provide for a variety of tastes. Most of them are financed and controlled by local authorities, although some are commercial ventures and others, such as national institutions like the British Museum and the National Gallery in London, are the province of the Secretary of State for Culture. Entry to many of the public museums and art galleries is now free of charge, after a period when entrance fees were levied for some institutions. But museums and art galleries are generally finding it difficult to operate on limited funds and are dependent upon local government grants, Arts Council subsidies and National Lottery donations. DCMS survey statistics in 2016–17 for England reported that 52.3 per cent of respondents had visited a museum or gallery at least once in a 12-month period and there were proportional increases in Wales, Scotland and Northern Ireland. This annual figure was slightly down on 2013 but the proportion of adults who had visited a museum or gallery at least once a month and at least 3–4 times a year had increased significant ly. These figures reflected the considerable popularity in recent years of exhibitions such as _Matisse: Cut-Outs_ at Tate Modern and _Vikings: Life and Legend_ at the British Museum. Later exhibitions have showcased Caravaggio, Modigliani, Van Gogh, Picasso, _Degas from the Burrell_ (the National Gallery, London, 2018) and _Rodin and the Art of Ancient Greece_ (the British Museum, 2018). Special exhibitions are a

PLATE 12.9 The Millennium Walkway (from the north to the south bank of the river Thames in London) and Tate Modern, a very successful gallery and exhibition hall created in 2000 from a disused power station, which specializes in contemporary art. © Andy Lauwers/ Shutterstock

prominent feature of regional galleries and museums which focus on themes of local interest. In 2016/17, 52 per cent of adults were aware of local or national events or activities in the UK between 2014 and 2018 to commemorate the Centenary of the First World War, and 74.2 per cent of adults supported the UK commemoration.

The public library is a long-established British cultural institution which is having financial difficulties and fighting hard to survive. Libraries were very much a feature of nineteenth-century social development and were intended to supply a local source of learning and culture for regional audiences, and every city and town and many villages had their own libraries.

There were 4,145 public libraries in the UK in 2014, a reduction from 4,482 in 2009–10 (Statista). These figures marked a large decline in the number of library buildings since the great days of the public library tradition in the nineteenth and early-twentieth century. They now provide books, music CDs, audio-books, records and DVDs on loan to the public, together with information, microfilm, microfiche, computer and Internet facilities. Some have mobile library buses which take books out to communities and rural areas, but this traditional service is also declining.

DCMS and ONS statistics show that between 2006 and 2010 there was a decrease in adults over 16 visiting a public library in England from 48 to 39 per cent. In 2016–17, 34 per cent of adults had used a public library service. This is significant ly lower than in 2005–06 (48.2 per cent), but similar to 2015–16 (33.4 per cent and the biggest decreases have been in the 16–24 age group. Public library use declined for males and females over the period 2005–06 to 2016–17, but in 2016–17, 38.3 per cent of females used a library compared with 29.6 per cent of males.

An aspect of this decline is that public libraries run by local government have been gradually closed down in recent decades and austerity measures to reduce the budget deficit have led to more closures nationwide. But some libraries are being staffed by volunteers who preserve some services.

Many people choose to borrow books from libraries rather than buying them. Fifty-one per cent of the UK population have a current library card, but most of these holders are over 60 years of age. The use of library websites has increased to 14.2 per cent of adults, but the decrease in book loans is 4 per cent, although e-book marketing has recently promoted a temporary surge in library membership. The number of adult fiction books, adult non-fiction and child non-fiction books issued have all decreased over time, but children’s fiction books have generally increased.

DCMS figures report that in 2014–15, 48 per cent of adults in Great Britain aged 15 and over read a daily newspaper at least three times a week, which is a decrease on 2013–14 when the figure was 51 per cent. On average since 1981 the proportion of people reading national newspapers has fallen by approximately 10 per cent over a period of declining newspaper circulation. It is difficult to assess whether reading generally as a leisure and cultural form will decline over time or whether reading will continue in different ways through alternative electronic forms. DCMS figures for 2014–15 reported that 64 per cent of adults had read for pleasure in their own time.

Bodies such as the Reading Agency are optimistic and argue that the remaining public libraries are popular and well-used, especially by young children if appropriate arrangements are made for them. Writers who organize library events report that there is still a thirst for and curiosity about reading and books.

As with sport, certain arts activities and their associated buildings have become virtual institutions, such as the West End, the remaining repertory companies, the Last Night of the Proms (Promenade Concerts, an annual season of classical music held at the Albert Hall, London), the Royal Festival Hall, the National Theatre, Tate Britain, the National Gallery and the Royal Shakespeare Theatre at Stratford-upon-Avon. Tate Modern is associated with often controversial prizes, such as the Turner Prize. These institutions reflect Britain’s lively (and internationally important) contemporary art scene.

Write short essays on the following topics:

1.  What impressions do you gain of the British people, based on their leisure, sporting and artistic activities?

1.  How would you account for the fluctuations in cinema attendance in Britain in the twentieth and twenty-first centuries?

1.  Critically examine the results of polls and surveys in this chapter. What information do they provide about the cultural lives of the British people?
